احتج المعتزلة بوجوه : منها ما يدل على إثبات مذهبهم ومنها ما يدل على إبطال مذهب الخصم. القسم الأول أربعة : الأول : فعل الواجبات هو الدين والدين هو الإسلام والإسلام هو الإيمان، ففعل الواجبات هو الدين أما إن فعل الواجبات هو الدين لقوله تعالى بعد ذكر العبادة وإقام الصلاة وإيتاء الزكاة وذلك دين القيمة ) ، إذ لا يخفى أن لفظة ذلك إشارة إلى جميع ما تقدم من الواجبات على معنى ذلك الذي أمرتم به دين الملة القيمة، ففعل الواجبات هو الدين ( وأما أن الدين هو الإسلام فلقوله تعالى : إن الدين عند الله الإسلام ﴾ [آل عمران : ١٩]، وأما أن الإسلام هو الإيمان، فلان الإيمان لو كان غير الإسلام لما قبل من مبتغيه لقوله تعالى : ومن يبتغ غير الإسلام ديناً فلن يقبل منه ﴾ [آل عمران : ٨٥] ولاستثناء المسلمين من المؤمنين في قوله : فأخرجنا من كان فيها ﴾ [الأنعام : ٩٩] الآية ) أن كلمة غير في قوله : فما وجدنا فيها غير بيت من المسلمين ﴾ [الذاريات : ٣٦] ليست صفة على معنى فما وجدنا فيها أي في تلك القرية شيئا غير بيت من المسلمين إنه كاذب بل هي استثناء والمراد بالبيت أهل البيت فيجب أن يقدر المستثنى منه على وجه يصح، وهو أن يقال : فما وجدنا فيها بيتاً من المؤمنين إلا بيتاً من المسلمين فقد استثنى المسلم من المؤمن فوجب أن يتحد الإيمان بالإسلام، ( قلنا : لفظ ذلك ) في تلك الآية ( إشارة إلى الإخلاص ) الذي يدل عليه لفظ مخلصين لا إلى المذكورات ( لأنه واحد مذكر فلا يصلح ) أن يكون ( إشارة إلى الكثير والمؤنث ) فإن أكثر المذكورات مؤنث (وهو) أي جعله إشارة إلى الإخلاص ( أولى من الذي ذكرتم، والظاهر المطابق لنهاية العقول أن يقال من تقدير الذي أمرتم به أو الذي ذكر ( إذ فيه ) أي في كونه إشارة إلى الإخلاص ( تقرير اللغة ) على أصلها، وفي كونه إشارة إلى المذكور مطلقا إخراجها عنه (هذا) كما مضى، (و) أما المقدمة ) الثالثة : ( وهي أن الاسلام هو الإيمان فهي ( إنما تصح وتثبت بالدليل الأول (لو كان الإيمان ديناً غير الإسلام لأن الآية إنما دلت على أن كل دين مغاير للإسلام فإنه غير مقبول، لا على أن كل شيء مغاير له غير مقبول فالاتحاد بين الإسلام والإيمان إنما يثبت بهذه الآية إذا ثبت كون الإيمان دينا ) وفيه مصادرة، لا يخفى) لأن كون الإيمان ديناً أي عمل الجوارح الذي هو الإسلام في قوة كونه عين الإسلام فإثبات الثاني بالأول يكون دوراً من قبيل أخذ المطلوب في إثباته ولو اقتصر على منع كونه دينا، إذ هو في قوة أول المسألة أعني كون الإيمان عمل الجوارح لكان أولى، وأما قضية الاستثناء فإنها تدل على تصادق المسلم والمؤمن دون الإسلام والإيمان، ألا يرى أن الضاحك يصدق على الباكي ولا تصادق بین الضحك والبكاء فضلا عن الاتحاد ..
The Mu'tazila argued by [several] proofs: some indicate the establishment of their doctrine, and some indicate the invalidation of the opponent's doctrine. The first division comprises four: The first: Performing obligatory acts is the religion (din), and the religion is Islam, and Islam is faith (iman); thus performing obligatory acts is faith. As for that performing obligatory acts is the religion, [it is] due to His saying, exalted, after mentioning worship, establishing prayer, and giving zakat: 'That is the religion of the straight [path]' (al-Bayyinah 5). For it is not hidden that the word 'that' (dhalika) refers to all the preceding obligations, meaning 'that which you have been commanded [is] the religion of the straight nation.' So performing obligatory acts is the religion. As for that the religion is Islam, [it is] due to His saying, exalted: 'Indeed, the religion with Allah is Islam' (Aal-Imran 19). And as for that Islam is faith, [it is] because if faith were other than Islam, it would not be accepted from its seeker, due to His saying, exalted: 'And whoever seeks a religion other than Islam, it will never be accepted from him' (Aal-Imran 85); and due to the exclusion (istithna') of Muslims from believers in His saying: 'And We brought out whoever was in it' (al-An'am 99) [and] the verse that the word 'other' (ghayr) in His saying: 'And We found not in it other than a house of Muslims' (al-Dhariyat 36) is not an adjective, meaning 'We found not in it, i.e., in that town, anything other than a house of Muslims'—it is false; rather, it is an exception (istithna'), and what is meant by 'house' is the people of the house. So the excluded-from (mustathna minhu) must be estimated in a way that is correct, which is to say: 'We found not in it a house of believers except a house of Muslims.' So the Muslim is separated/excepted from the believer, so it is necessary that iman and Islam are identical. We said: The word 'that' in that verse refers to sincerity (ikhlas), which is indicated by the word 'sincere' (mukhlisina), not to the enumerated things, because it is singular masculine and cannot refer to many things that are feminine. And making it refer to sincerity is more appropriate than what you mentioned. The apparent meaning consistent with the Nihayat al-'Uqul is to say: from the estimation of 'that which you were commanded' or 'what [was] mentioned.' For in making it refer to sincerity there is a confirmation of the language in its original [usage]; whereas making it refer to the mentioned things in an absolute way takes it out of that [original usage]. This is as has passed. As for the third premise (that Islam is faith), it is only correct and established by the first proof if faith were a religion other than Islam, because the verse only indicates that every religion different from Islam is unacceptable, not that everything different from it is unacceptable. So the identity between Islam and faith is established by this verse only if it is established that faith is a religion—and that is a petition of principle (musadara), as is clear, because faith being a religion, i.e., action of the limbs, which is Islam, is in effect the same as being exactly Islam, so establishing the second by the first is a circle (dawr) of the type of taking the desired [conclusion] in establishing it. If he confined himself to denying that it is a religion, that would be in effect the first point of the question, i.e., that faith is action of the limbs, that would be more appropriate. As for the matter of the exception (istithna'), it only indicates the coextensiveness of 'muslim' and 'mu'min', not of Islam and faith. Do you not see that 'the one who laughs' is true of 'the one who weeps,' but there is no coextensiveness between laughter and weeping, let alone identity?
Source pages p. 355

Commentary
This passage presents and refutes the first proof of the Mu'tazila that faith (iman) is identical to performing obligatory acts (fard acts). The proof is structured as a three-step syllogism: (1) Performing obligatory acts is the religion (din), as the Qur'an says 'That is the religion of the straight path' after mentioning worship, prayer, and zakat—the word 'that' (dhalika) referring to those acts. (2) The religion is Islam, as per 'Indeed, the religion with Allah is Islam.' (3) Islam is faith, because the verse 'Whoever seeks a religion other than Islam, it will never be accepted from him' implies that anything other than Islam is not faith; and also the verse about Lot's people where 'house of Muslims' is excepted from general believers—showing that 'muslim' and 'mu'min' are coextensive, so Islam and iman are identical. Therefore, faith is performing obligatory acts. The refutation follows: First, the word 'that' in the 'religion of the straight path' verse does not refer to the enumerated acts but to sincerity (ikhlas), because 'that' is masculine singular whereas most of the acts are feminine; the commentator cites the Nihayat al-'Uqul to support this reading. Second, the third premise (Islam = iman) is circular: to prove it, one must already assume that faith is a religion (din), which is exactly what the Mu'tazila are trying to prove—this is a petition of principle (musadara). Moreover, the exception (istithna') in the Lot verse only shows that every believer in that context was a Muslim, not that the two concepts are identical; a simple coextensiveness does not imply identity, as in the example of 'the one who laughs' and 'the one who weeps'—they may apply to the same person but the actions are not identical.

إخلاص (ikhlas)
مصادرة (musadara)
دور (dawr)
استثناء (istithna')
تصادق (tasadiq)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The exception in the Lot verse proves that Islam and iman are identical.
Correction: The refutation shows that the exception only indicates coextensiveness (the same people are called both), not identity of the concepts, as illustrated by the example of 'one who laughs' and 'one who weeps'—they can apply to the same individual without the actions being identical.

Mu'tazila's Second Proof: The Prayer Verse and Its Critique
The Mu'tazila cite 'And Allah would not let your faith go to waste' interpreted as prayer.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Mu'tazila's proof from the prayer verse (al-Baqarah 143) and the author's critical response, including the self-defeating implication for their own definition of faith.

Core Idea
The Mu'tazila's prayer-verse proof backfires, for it would reduce faith to a single act.

The Mu'tazila sought to prove that faith consists of acts by citing the verse where Allah says He will not let 'your faith' go to waste, glossing 'faith' as prayer. However, the author points out that this interpretation leads to contradictions with other texts and even undermines the Mu'tazila's own definition of faith as all obligatory acts. This lesson examines the proof and its critique.

Guided Path
1
Understand the proof
The Mu'tazila cite Quran 2:143, interpreting 'faith' as prayer towards Jerusalem, to argue that faith is an act.
This was revealed after the change of qibla.
2
Note the hadith objection
The hadith of Jibril distinguishes Islam from iman, suggesting that faith is inner belief, not outward acts. The author mentions this hadith as a counter to the Mu'tazila.
3
Examine the author's response
The author argues that a single hadith cannot override multiple clear Qur'anic verses, and the hadith should be interpreted as referring to the 'fruit' of faith, not faith itself.
This is a methodological point about conflicting evidences.
4
See the self-defeating implication
The author points out that if the verse meant prayer alone, then prayer alone would be faith. But the Mu'tazila believe faith is all obligatory acts, so their proof actually undermines their own position.
This is a reductio ad absurdum.
5
Reflect on the marginal note
The commentator clarifies why the author said 'the apparent matching' – it addresses an opponent's interpretation of the word 'that' in the verse.
This is a technical note about the Arabic grammar.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الثاني : ( من تلك الوجوه قوله تعالى : وما كان الله ليضيع إيمانكم [البقرة : ١٤٣ ] أي صلاتكم إلى بيت المقدس)، وذلك لنزول الآية بعد تحويل القبلة فقال الله أن تشهد أن لا إله إلا الله الحديث، فإنه يدل على أن الإسلام مغاير للإيمان وثمرة له، قلت : وقد سبق أن الدليل الواحد لا يعارض المتعدد عند بعض المحققين سيما وذلك الواحد حديث، والمتعدد آيات فينبغي أن يحمل تفسير الإسلام في الحديث على تفسير ثمرته . قوله : ( والظاهر المطابق (إلخ إنما قال : الظاهر لجواز أن يكون الخطاب في قوله : الذي ذكرتم للخصوم الذين جعلوا لفظ ذلك في الآية إشارة إلى جميع ما تقدم على معنى ذلك الذي أمرتم به كما ذكره الشارح فيما سبق وإن لم يذكره المصنف . قوله : (أي صلاتكم لو صح هذا لزم أن يكون الصلاة وحدها إيماناً مع أنه فعل جميع الواجبات عندهم .
The second of those proofs: His saying, exalted: 'And Allah would not let your faith go to waste' (al-Baqarah 143), i.e., your prayer towards the Sacred House (Bayt al-Maqdis), because the verse was revealed after the change of the qibla. (Then) He said: 'that you testify that there is no god but Allah'—the hadith. For it indicates that Islam is different from faith and is a fruit of it. I say: It has already preceded that a single proof does not contradict multiple proofs according to some investigators, especially since that single [proof] is a hadith, and the multiple [proofs] are verses. So the interpretation of 'Islam' in the hadith should be taken as an interpretation of its fruit. His saying: 'And the apparent matching (al-zahir al-mutabiq) etc.' He only said 'apparent' because it is possible that the address in his saying 'that which you mentioned' is directed to the opponents who made the word 'that' in the verse refer to all that preceded, meaning 'that which you were commanded' as the commentator mentioned previously, even if the author did not mention it. His saying: 'i.e., your prayer' – if this were correct, it would entail that prayer alone is faith, whereas [according to them] faith is the performance of all obligatory acts.
Source pages p. 356

Commentary
This passage presents and critiques the second proof of the Mu'tazila. The proof is based on the Qur'anic verse 'And Allah would not let your faith go to waste' (al-Baqarah 143), which was revealed after the change of the qibla from Jerusalem to Mecca, and the word 'faith' is interpreted as 'prayer' (salat) towards Jerusalem. The Mu'tazila argue that since prayer is an act, faith must be an act. The author responds: (1) There is a hadith of Jibril that distinguishes Islam from iman, indicating that Islam is the outward submission and iman is inward belief; the response to this hadith is that a single hadith cannot contradict multiple clear Qur'anic verses. (2) The hadith should be interpreted as referring to the 'fruit' of faith, not faith itself. (3) If the verse is taken literally, it would mean that prayer alone is faith, but the Mu'tazila themselves believe that faith is all obligatory acts, not just prayer—so this proof actually undermines their position. The marginal note clarifies that the commentator's phrase 'the apparent matching' addresses an opponent's interpretation of the word 'that' in the earlier verse.

Key Terms
صلاة (salat)
إيمان (iman)
إسلام (Islam)
تحويل القبلة (change of qibla)
ثمرة (thamara/fruit)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The verse 'And Allah would not let your faith go to waste' definitively proves that faith is only prayer.
Correction: The author shows that this interpretation would make prayer alone constitute faith, contradicting the Mu'tazila's own broader definition of faith as all obligatory acts, and thus the proof is self-defeating.

Key Takeaway

Third Objection: Highway Robber Not a Believer
Defense against objection that highway robber is not a believer

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the objection that a highway robber is not a believer is refuted by analyzing Qur'anic verses and the scope of divine promise.

Core Idea
The promise of no disgrace is specific to the companions, not all believers.

This lesson addresses a key objection against defining faith as mere assent (tasdiq). The objector argues that a highway robber, though he may assent, is not considered a believer, implying that avoiding major sins is part of faith. The response carefully examines the Qur'anic verses cited and shows that they do not support the objection.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection and the verses cited
Read the passage from the beginning to understand the objector's claim and the Qur'anic proofs provided.
Notice the two verses used: 2:114 and 3:192.
2
Understand the objector's logic
The objector argues that the highway robber will be disgraced (يخزى) on the Day of Judgment, while believers are not disgraced (لا يخزي). Therefore, the robber is not a believer.
This logic assumes that 'no disgrace' applies to all believers.
3
Examine the defender's first response
The defender points out that the verse 'لا يخزي الله النبي والذين آمنوا معه' (66:8) is specific to the companions due to the phrase 'معه' (with him). Thus, it does not include highway robbers.
This limits the scope of the divine promise.
4
Consider the alternative grammatical reading
The defender also offers a possible reading where the relative clause is the subject and its predicate is 'their light before them', allowing that a believer might be disgraced temporarily by entering the Fire, even if eventually saved.
This shows the verse can be interpreted differently.
5
Conclude why the objection fails
The objection fails because the evidence cited does not prove that the robber is not a believer; it only shows he may be disgraced, which does not negate faith.
Faith and temporary punishment are compatible.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الثالث : قاطع الطريق ليس بمؤمن فيكون ترك المنهي داخلاً في الإيمان، وإنما قلنا: هو ليس بمؤمن لأنه يخزى يوم القيامة لقوله تعالى : ﴿ فيهم ولهم في الآخرة عذاب عظیم ﴾ [البقرة : ١١٤]، قال المفسرون: أي ولهم في الآخرة عذاب النار فالمذكور في الكتاب معنى القرآن لا نظمه مع قوله تعالى حكاية على سبيل التصديق والتقرير ﴿ ربنا إنك من تدخل النار فقد أخزيتة ﴾ [آل عمران : ١٩٢] فإن هذين القولين معا يدلان على أن قاطع الطريق يخزى يوم القيامة ، والمؤمن لا يخزى في ذلك اليوم لقوله تعالى : ﴿ يوم لا يخزي الله النبي والذين آمنوا معه ﴾ [التحريم:۸]، قلنا : عدم الإخزاء لا يعم المؤمنين جميعاً بل هو مخصوص بالصحابة كما يدل عليه لفظ معه ولا قاطع طريق فيهم فلا يتم هذا الاستدلال وأيضاً يجوز أن يكون الموصول مع صلته مبتدأ خبره نورهم بين أيديهم وحينئذ جاز أيضاً أن يكون المؤمن مخزى في يوم القيامة بإدخاله في النار وإن كان مآله الخروج منها .
The third: The highway robber is not a believer, so abandoning what is forbidden would be included in faith. We say: he is not a believer because he will be disgraced on the Day of Resurrection due to His saying, the Most High: '...in them and for them in the Hereafter is a great punishment' [2:114]. The commentators said: meaning 'and for them in the Hereafter is the punishment of the Fire'. So what is mentioned in the Book is the meaning of the Qur'an, not its arrangement, along with His saying, the Most High, reporting by way of affirmation and confirmation: 'Our Lord, indeed whoever You admit to the Fire, You have disgraced him' [3:192]. For these two statements together indicate that the highway robber will be disgraced on the Day of Resurrection, and the believer is not disgraced on that day due to His saying, the Most High: 'The Day when Allah will not disgrace the Prophet and those who believed with him' [66:8]. We say: the absence of disgrace does not encompass all believers, but rather it is specific to the companions, as indicated by the word 'with him', and there is no highway robber among them, so this inference is not valid. Also, it is possible that the relative clause is the subject whose predicate is 'their light before them', and in that case it is also permissible that the believer may be disgraced on the Day of Resurrection by being admitted into the Fire, even if his final outcome is to come out of it.
Source pages p. 357

Commentary
This passage presents the third objection against defining faith as mere tasdiq (assent). The objector argues that a highway robber (qati' al-tariq) is not considered a believer by consensus, yet if faith were only assent, he would be a believer as long as he assents. The objector infers that avoiding prohibitions (ترك المنهي) must be part of faith. The response counters by examining the Qur'anic verses cited. The objector used verses that threaten disgrace on the Day of Resurrection for the robber, and argued that believers are not disgraced based on verse 66:8. The defender points out that this verse is specific to the companions because of the word 'with him' (معه), so it does not prove that all believers are immune from disgrace. Moreover, even if a believer is temporarily admitted to the Fire, it is not permanent disgrace; his final outcome is paradise. Thus the objection fails. The defender also offers an alternative grammatical reading that allows for the possibility of a believer being disgraced temporarily.

Key Terms
قاطع الطريق
يخزى
لا يخزي
التصديق

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The verse 'Day when Allah will not disgrace the Prophet and those who believed with him' applies to all believers unconditionally.
Correction: The phrase 'with him' limits the promise to the companions (الصحابة) who were with the Prophet, not all believers.

Checkpoint
According to the response to the third objection, why does the verse 'لا يخزي الله النبي والذين آمنوا معه' not prove that all believers are immune from disgrace?
A) Because the verse is abrogated.
B) Because the verse is specific to the companions (الصحابة) and does not include highway robbers.
C) Because the highway robber repents before death.
D) Because disgrace only applies to disbelievers.

Key Takeaway
The objection fails because the cited verse does not guarantee all believers immunity from disgrace, and faith can coexist with temporary punishment.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
1. Have you ever felt secure in your faith because you assumed a particular verse guaranteed your safety? This lesson shows that even the promise of no disgrace was specific to the companions. What verse or hadith do you rely on for your salvation, and is your interpretation truly justified? Will you now re-examine your assumptions?
?
2. The objector thought that any disgrace (such as entry into Fire) automatically implies one is not a believer. But the defender shows that a believer might be temporarily punished. Are you prepared to face the possibility of temporary punishment in the afterlife for your sins, or do you expect to be completely spared? How does this affect your current actions and efforts to repent?
?
3. The highway robber is considered a non-believer by the objector, yet the defender does not directly answer that—instead they focus on the verse. Do you ever judge others' faith based on their actions, perhaps too quickly? How do you reconcile the fact that a person may have faith yet commit major sins? Will you now be more cautious in judging others while still holding yourself accountable?

Hadiths Seeming to Negate Faith: Hyperbole and Counter-Evidence
Responds to hadiths that seem to deny faith to sinners, interpreting them as hyperbole and citing contradictory hadiths.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the defender refutes hadiths that appear to deny faith to sinners by interpreting them as hyperbole and citing contradictory traditions.

Core Idea
Hadiths threatening faith for sins are warnings, not literal negations.

The fourth objection draws on prophetic statements like 'The adulterer does not commit adultery while he is a believer.' This lesson examines the defender's twofold response: first, that such statements are hyperbolic encouragements to avoid sin, and second, that they are counterbalanced by explicit hadiths affirming Allah's mercy for those who die upon tawhid.

Guided Path
1
Identify the objection's hadiths
Read the passage and locate the two hadiths cited by the objector: 'The adulterer does not commit adultery while he is a believer' and 'There is no faith for one who has no trustworthiness.'
These hadiths seem to negate faith for major sinners.
2
Understand the hyperbole interpretation
The defender says these are hyperbole (مبالغة) meaning such acts are not proper to true faith. Note the reasoning: to preserve the linguistic meaning of faith as assent.
Hyperbole is a common rhetorical device in warnings.
3
Examine the counter-hadith of Abu Dharr
Read the narration where the Prophet emphatically affirms that anyone who dies on 'La ilaha illa Allah' enters paradise, even if they commit adultery and theft. See how this contradicts the literal reading of the objection's hadiths.
The repetition underscores the Prophet's firm response.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الرابع: نحو قوله عليه السلام لا يزني الزاني وهو مؤمن لا إيمان لمن لا أمانة له، قلنا: مبالغة على معنى هذه الأفعال ليست من شأن المؤمن كأنها تنافي الإيمان ولا تجامعه ويجب الحمل على هذا المعنى كيلا يلزم نقل لفظ الإيمان عن معناه اللغوي، ثم إنها أي الأحاديث الدالة على اعتبار الأعمال كترك الزنا مثلاً في الإيمان معارضة بالأحاديث الدالة على أنه أي مرتكب الزنا مثلاً مؤمن وأنه يدخل الجنة حتى قال النبي عليه الصلاة والسلام لأبي ذر لما بالغ في السؤال عنه وإن زنى وإن سرق على رغم أنف أبي ذر. وروي عن أبي ذر رضي الله عنه قال : أتيت النبي عليه الصلاة والسلام وعليه ثوب أبيض وهو نائم ثم أتيته وقد استيقظ فقال : ما من عبد قال : لا إله إلا الله ثم مات على ذلك إلا دخل الجنة قلت وإن زنى وإن سرق ؟ قال : وإن زنى وإن سرق، قلت : وإن زنى وإن سرق ؟ قال : وإن زنى وإن سرق قلت : وإن زنى وإن سرق ؟ قال : وإن زنى وإن سرق على رغم أنف أبي ذر.
The fourth: Similar to his saying, peace be upon him: 'The adulterer does not commit adultery while he is a believer', 'There is no faith for one who has no trustworthiness'. We say: it is hyperbole, meaning that these actions are not characteristic of the believer, as if they contradict faith and do not coexist with it. It is necessary to interpret it in this sense so as not to necessitate transferring the word 'faith' from its linguistic meaning. Moreover, these hadiths that indicate the consideration of actions, such as abandoning adultery, as part of faith are contradicted by hadiths that indicate that the perpetrator of adultery, for example, is a believer and will enter paradise, until the Prophet, peace and blessings be upon him, said to Abu Dharr when he was extreme in asking about it: 'Even if he commits adultery and steals, despite the nose of Abu Dharr.' And it is narrated from Abu Dharr, may Allah be pleased with him, he said: I came to the Prophet, peace and blessings be upon him, while he was wearing a white garment and sleeping, then I came to him after he had woken up, and he said: 'There is no servant who says "There is no god but Allah" and then dies upon that except he enters paradise.' I said: 'Even if he commits adultery and steals?' He said: 'Even if he commits adultery and steals.' I said: 'Even if he commits adultery and steals?' He said: 'Even if he commits adultery and steals.' I said: 'Even if he commits adultery and steals?' He said: 'Even if he commits adultery and steals, despite the nose of Abu Dharr.'
Source pages p. 357

Commentary
This passage responds to the fourth objection, which cites hadiths that seem to deny faith to those who commit major sins, such as 'The adulterer does not commit adultery while he is a believer' and 'There is no faith for one who has no trustworthiness.' The defender interprets these as hyperbole (مبالغة), meaning that such sinful actions are not proper to true faith and are incompatible with its perfection, but not that faith is literally removed. This interpretation preserves the linguistic meaning of faith as assent. Furthermore, the defender points out that these hadiths are contradicted (معارضة) by other authentic hadiths, notably the narration of Abu Dharr, where the Prophet emphatically confirms that anyone who dies saying 'La ilaha illa Allah' enters paradise, even if they committed adultery and theft. The repetition in Abu Dharr's question shows his astonishment, and the Prophet's repeated affirmative response underscores the overarching mercy of Allah and the efficacy of tawhid despite major sins. Thus, the objection is refuted by showing that the hadiths in question are not to be taken literally and are counterbalanced by stronger texts.

Key Terms
مبالغة
معارضة
لا إله إلا الله
أبو ذر

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That these hadiths literally negate faith for sinners, so that a person who commits a major sin is no longer a believer.
Correction: The defender shows they are hyperbolic warnings, and that other hadiths explicitly affirm faith and salvation for sinners who die upon tawhid.

Checkpoint
How does the defender interpret hadiths like 'لا يزني الزاني وهو مؤمن'?
A) As abrogated by later verses.
B) As hyperbole (مبالغة) meaning such acts are incompatible with perfect faith, not that faith is removed.
C) As evidence that faith increases and decreases.
D) As applying only to hypocrites.

Key Takeaway
Hadiths that seem to deny faith to sinners are best understood as hyperbolic warnings, and they are contradicted by explicit hadiths that affirm salvation through tawhid despite major sins.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you read the hadith 'The adulterer does not commit adultery while he is a believer,' do you feel a sense of self-righteousness or do you examine your own sins and ask: 'What does my faith actually look like when I fall into sin?' How will you ensure your faith remains genuine, not just a label?
?
The Prophet told Abu Dharr 'even if he commits adultery and steals, despite the nose of Abu Dharr.' If you knew someone who had committed such sins, would you still view them as a potential believer, or would you write them off? What does your answer reveal about your understanding of Allah's mercy and the weight of tawhid?
?
If you died tonight with just your tawhid, but a history of hidden sins, would you be confident in the promise of paradise, or do you rely on your deeds more than the declaration of faith? What practical change does this lesson demand from you today?

Faith and the Sleeper: Defending Assent as the Reality of Faith
Objection and answer regarding faith during sleep.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the Ash'ari response maintains that a person who has previously believed remains a believer even when asleep or heedless, using the principle that the Lawgiver treats the 'considered' as the 'actual'.

Core Idea
Faith is established by past assent; the Lawgiver treats the believer as such even when unconscious.

This passage addresses a critical objection: if faith is merely assent, then how can a sleeping or heedless person be considered a believer? The answer reveals a key principle of Islamic theology — that the ruling (ḥukm) of a person can be based on their past state, not just the present moment. The Lawgiver (al-shāriʿ) applies this principle consistently.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection
Read the first part of the passage (up to 'خلاف الإجماع'). The objector claims that if faith is only assent, sleepers and the heedless are not believers, contradicting consensus.
This objection targets the view that faith is solely tasdiq.
2
Understand the response's first point
The response says 'المؤمن من آمن في الحال أو في الماضي' — the believer is one who has believed in the present or the past. The term 'believer' applies based on past actualization, not continuous present assent.
This introduces the concept of derived nouns and their application.
3
Grasp the Lawgiver's principle
The response explains that the Lawgiver gives the ruling of the one who actually performed (المحقق) to the one considered as such (الحكمي). This is why a sleeper is still called a believer.
The principle 'الحكمي كالمحقق' is a key theological and legal tool.
4
See the counter-objection
The response turns the objection back: if we reject this principle, then the opponent's view (that faith includes actions) would also fail because sleepers are not performing actions.
This shows the opponent must accept the principle to avoid inconsistency.
5
Reflect on the conclusion
The only escape is to accept that the considered (ḥukmī) is like the actual (muḥaqqiq). This upholds the consensus that sleepers are believers.
The principle is applied consistently to both belief and actions.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الأول : لو كان الإيمان هو التصديق لما كان المرء مؤمناً حين لا يكون مصدقاً كالنائم حال نومه والغافل حين غفلته، وإنه خلاف الإجماع، قلنا : المؤمن من آمن في الحال أو في الماضي لا لأنه حقيقة فيه وإن أمكن أن يدعى فيه ذلك كما هو مذهب جماعة في المشتقات بل لأن الشارع يعطي الحكمي حكم المحقق، وإلا أي وإن لم يكن الأمر كما ذكرناه، ورد عليهم مثله في الأعمال فإن النائم والغافل ليسا في الأعمال المعتبرة في الإيمان فلا يكونان مؤمنين، ولا مخلص إلا بأن الحكمي كالمحقق.
The first: If faith were assent, then a person would not be a believer when he is not giving assent, like the sleeper during his sleep and the heedless during his heedlessness, and that is contrary to consensus. We say: the believer is one who believed in the present or in the past, not because it is actually true of him (even if that can be claimed as the doctrine of a group regarding derived nouns), but because the Lawgiver gives the ruling of the one who actually performed the act to the one who is considered as such. Otherwise, if the matter is not as we said, then the same objection would apply to them concerning actions, for the sleeper and the heedless are not engaged in the actions considered in faith, so they would not be believers, and there is no escape except that the one considered as such is like the actual doer.
Source pages p. 358

Commentary
This passage presents the first of three proofs against the opponent's view that faith includes actions. The objector argues that if faith were only assent (tasdiq), then someone who is asleep or heedless—and thus not currently assenting—would not be a believer, but the consensus is that they are believers. The response makes two points. First, the term 'believer' is applied to one who has performed the act of faith in the present or past, not because the attribute is actually inherent at every moment, but because the Lawgiver (الشارع) gives the same ruling to the person who is considered to have faith (الحكمي) as to the one who actually has it (المحقق). Second, the objection is turned back against the opponent: if the same reasoning were applied to actions (which the opponent includes in faith), then the sleeper and heedless would also not be believers because they are not performing actions, which the opponent would have to avoid. Therefore, the only consistent position is that the considered ruling (الحكمي) is equivalent to the actual (المحقق).

Key Terms
النائم
الغافل
الحكمي
المحقق
الشارع

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Faith requires continuous, conscious assent at every moment.
Correction: Faith is established by past assent; the Lawgiver treats the person with past faith as a believer even when unconscious, based on the principle that the considered (ḥukmī) is like the actual (muḥaqqiq).

Checkpoint
Why is a sleeping person still called a believer according to the response?
A) Because sleep does not remove the capacity for faith.
B) Because the Lawgiver gives the ruling of a believer to one who has believed in the past.
C) Because angels write down faith continuously.
D) Because the sleeper is actually assenting in his heart.

Key Takeaway
A person who has sincerely believed in the past remains a believer even when unconscious, due to the Lawgiver's principle of granting the same ruling to the considered as to the actual.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you are asleep or distracted, do you ever doubt your own faith because you are not consciously thinking of Allah? How does this lesson's principle of 'considered as actual' reassure you that your past sincere belief is enough, and how should that change your fear of losing faith in moments of heedlessness?
?
If the Lawgiver treats you as a believer based on your past assent, are you living in a way that honours that ruling? What specific action will you take today to remember that you are considered a believer even when you are not actively thinking about it?
?
This lesson says the considered is like the actual. How does this truth affect your view of others who may appear heedless but have sincerely believed? Can you name one person you might have judged as less faithful and commit to treating them with the same ruling as a believer?

Second Aspect: The Believer Who Prostrates to the Sun
Refutes the claim that if faith were only assent, one who assents but prostrates to the sun should be a believer.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the external ruling of disbelief can apply to someone who internally assents, and why this does not contradict faith being only assent.

Core Idea
Outward actions indicate faith but are not part of its essence.

The objector argues that if faith is only assent, then a person who assents but prostrates to the sun should still be a believer. Yet the consensus judges such a person a disbeliever. How can this be? The response distinguishes between the apparent evidence and the inner reality, showing that the external ruling does not require a change in the definition of faith.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Carefully read the Arabic passage and its English translation to grasp the objection and response.
Notice the key term 'ظاهر' (apparent) in the response.
2
Identify the objection and consensus
Pinpoint the objector's claim that such a person should be a believer, and the consensus that he is not.
3
Understand the defender's distinction
The defender says the prostration is apparent evidence of lack of assent, and we judge by the apparent, but refraining from prostration is not part of the definition of faith.
Focus on the phrase 'ونحن نحكم بالظاهر'.
4
Reflect on the internal vs external nuance
Consider that if we knew his heart was firm in assent, he would not be a disbeliever before Allah, but outwardly we rule disbelief.
This nuance preserves faith as assent while accounting for legal consequences.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الثاني: من صدق بما جاء به النبي و مع ذلك سجد للشمس ينبغي أن يكون مؤمناً، والإجماع على خلافه، قلنا : هو دليل عدم التصديق أي سجوده لها يدل بظاهره على أنه ليس بمصدق ونحن نحكم بالظاهر فلذلك حكمنا بعدم إيمانه لا لأن عدم السجود لغير الله داخل في حقيقة الإيمان حتى لو علم أنه لم يسجد لها على سبيل التعظيم واعتقاد الإلهية بل سجد لها وقلبه مطمئن بالتصديق لم يحكم بكفره فيما بينه وبين الله، وإن أجري عليه حكم الكفر في الظاهر .
The second: If one assents to what the Prophet brought and yet prostrates to the sun, he should be considered a believer, but the consensus is otherwise. We say: that is evidence of lack of assent; that is, his prostration to it apparently indicates that he is not an assenter, and we judge by the apparent, therefore we judge him to be a non-believer, not because refraining from prostration to other than Allah is part of the reality of faith. Even if we knew that he did not prostrate to it for the purpose of veneration and belief in divinity, but prostrated to it while his heart was tranquil with assent, we would not judge him to be a disbeliever between him and Allah, although the ruling of disbelief is applied to him in the external.
Source pages p. 358

Commentary
This passage presents the second proof. The objector argues that if faith were only assent, then a person who assents to the Prophet's message but also prostrates to the sun should still be a believer, yet the consensus is that he is not. The response distinguishes between the external judgment (الظاهر) and the internal reality. The act of prostration to the sun is taken as evidence (دليل) that the person does not truly assent, because it contradicts the core of the message (tawhid). We judge by the apparent, so we pronounce him a disbeliever. However, the defender explicitly states that refraining from prostration to other than Allah is not part of the definition (حقيقة) of faith; rather, it is an indicator. If there were certainty that the person's heart is firm in assent and his prostration was not out of worship, then internal judgment is different, though outwardly the Islamic ruling of disbelief applies. This nuance protects the definition of faith as assent while accounting for the legal consequences of contradictory actions.

Key Terms
السجود للشمس
الظاهر
حكم الكفر
دليل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: If someone is judged a disbeliever in this world, they must be a disbeliever in God's sight.
Correction: The passage explains that external rulings are based on apparent evidence; internal reality (true assent) may differ, and only Allah knows the heart.

Checkpoint
According to the response, why is a person who assents but prostrates to the sun judged a disbeliever outwardly?
A) Because prostration to the sun is a major sin that negates faith.
B) Because his action is apparent evidence that he does not truly assent.
C) Because faith requires avoiding all sins.
D) Because the consensus is that such a person is a hypocrite.

Key Takeaway
Faith is internal assent; outward actions serve as apparent indicators for legal rulings, but the ultimate judgment belongs to Allah.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Are there any outward actions in your life that, if observed by others, would make them doubt the sincerity of your inner faith? What would those actions be, and are you willing to change them?
?
This lesson teaches that we judge by the apparent but Allah knows the true state of the heart. Have you ever judged someone's faith harshly based on an outward action, forgetting that only Allah knows their inner reality? How can you cultivate a balance between upholding apparent standards and leaving judgment to Allah?
?
The passage says that if the heart is firm in assent but the person prostrates without worship, he is not a disbeliever before Allah. This means the inner reality can be hidden even in the face of a major outward violation. Does this comfort you or challenge you? Are you more concerned about how you appear or about the state of your heart before Allah?

Faith and Disbelief
intermediate
6 min
The Verse on Belief and Polytheism: Objection and Reply
Refutes the claim that the verse combining belief and polytheism contradicts faith as assent to all that the Prophet brought.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the verse 'And most of them do not believe in Allah except while they are polytheists' is used as an objection against the definition of faith as assent to everything the Prophet brought, and how the responder refutes it using two key arguments.

Core Idea
The verse uses 'iman' in a restricted sense, not as full assent to everything.

This lesson examines the third scriptural proof used against the Ash'ari definition of faith. The verse combines belief and polytheism, seemingly contradicting the idea that faith is assent to all that the Prophet taught. We'll see how the response handles this by distinguishing between the general meaning of iman and its usage with the preposition 'bi', and by noting that the objection equally applies to the opponent's view.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own dialectical rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice the objection and the two-part response.
2
Identify the objector's argument
The objector uses the verse to argue that faith (iman) can coexist with polytheism, so faith cannot be defined as assent to everything the Prophet brought, because that would exclude polytheism.
The objector assumes that the verse uses 'iman' in its full sense.
3
Understand the first response: shared obligation
The responder says the objection is 'shared obligation' (مشترك الإلزام) — it applies to both sides. Polytheism contradicts faith by consensus in any definition, so the verse does not favor one view over another.
This argument neutralizes the objection without defining faith.
4
Understand the second response: restricted meaning
The responder then explains that in this verse, 'iman' with the preposition 'bi' means assent specifically to Allah, not comprehensive assent to all teachings. Thus the verse does not refute the definition of faith as comprehensive assent.
This is the key resolution.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الثالث : قوله تعالى : ﴿ وما يؤمن أكثرهم بالله إلا وهم مشركون ﴾ [ يوسف : ١٠٦ ]، فإنه يدل على اجتماع الإيمان مع الشرك ، والتصديق بجميع ما جاء به الرسول لا يجامع الشرك لأن التوحيد مما علم مجيئه به فلا يكون الإيمان عبارة عن ذلك التصديق، قلنا : ذلك الذي ذكرتموه مشترك الإلزام لأن الشرك مناف للإيمان إجماعاً، وفعل الواجبات لا ينافيه فلا يكون إيماناً، ثم نقول في حله إن الإيمان المعدى بالباء هو التصديق ولم يقصد الله ظاهراً وهو به في الآية التصديق بجميع ما علم مجيئه في الدين بل بما قيد به.
The third: His saying, the Most High: 'And most of them do not believe in Allah except while they are polytheists' [12:106], for it indicates the combination of faith with polytheism, whereas assent to everything the messenger brought cannot combine with polytheism because tawhid is known to be among what he brought. Therefore faith is not defined as that assent. We say: that which you have mentioned is a shared obligation (i.e., it applies to both sides) because polytheism is contradictory to faith by consensus, and performing obligatory acts does not contradict it, so it is not faith. Then we say in its resolution: Indeed, faith when used with the preposition 'bi' is assent, and Allah did not intend in the verse—apparently—assent to everything known to have been brought in religion, but rather to what is restricted by it.
Source pages p. 358

Commentary
This passage presents the third proof from the Qur'anic verse 12:106, which states that most people do not believe in Allah except while being polytheists. The objector argues that this verse shows that faith (iman) can coexist with polytheism (shirk), but if faith were defined as assent to everything the Prophet brought, it would be incompatible with polytheism because tawhid is a necessary part of that message. Therefore, faith cannot be merely that assent. The response makes two points. First, the objection is 'shared obligation' (مشترك الإلزام), meaning it equally applies to the opponent's view: polytheism is contradictory to faith by consensus in any definition, so the verse cannot be used to define faith. Second, the verse uses 'iman' with the preposition 'bi' (آمن بالله), which here means a specific kind of assent restricted to Allah, not comprehensive assent to all teachings. Thus, the verse does not negate the definition of faith as comprehensive assent; it simply uses the word in a narrower sense.

Key Terms
مشركون
مشترك الإلزام
الإيمان المعدى بالباء

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That the verse 'وما يؤمن أكثرهم بالله إلا وهم مشركون' proves that true faith (iman) and polytheism (shirk) can genuinely coexist in a single heart.
Correction: The verse uses 'iman' in a restricted sense (assent to Allah's existence), not the full theological definition of faith as comprehensive assent to everything the Prophet brought.

Checkpoint
Why does the responder say the verse 'وما يؤمن أكثرهم بالله إلا وهم مشركون' does not disprove that faith is assent to all that the Prophet brought?
A) Because the verse is abrogated.
B) Because the verse uses 'iman' in a restricted sense (assent to Allah), not comprehensive assent.
C) Because polytheists do not actually have any faith.
D) Because the verse applies only to the Meccan polytheists.

Key Takeaway
The verse 'وما يؤمن أكثرهم بالله إلا وهم مشركون' does not refute the definition of faith as comprehensive assent because it uses 'iman' in a restricted sense and the objection equally applies to the opponent.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you ever find yourself believing in Allah in a restricted way—acknowledging His existence but ignoring His commands in certain areas of your life? How does this lesson’s distinction between comprehensive and restricted assent expose your own partial faith? What is one specific area where you need to move from restricted belief to full submission, starting today?
?
The objector in this passage used a Quranic verse to support a flawed conclusion. When have you used a religious text selectively to justify a comfortable but incomplete understanding of your faith? How will you guard against taking verses out of context to avoid the full weight of Islamic teachings?
?
Shirk contradicts faith by consensus, yet the verse mentions people who 'believe' while also committing shirk. Have you ever considered that hidden shirk (like showing off in worship) might coexist with a general belief in Allah? Ask yourself: In which acts of worship am I seeking people’s approval more than Allah’s? What will you do today to purify your intention?

The Second Objection: Prostration to the Sun Does Not Negate Tasdiq? Actually It Does Outwardly
Sanusi presents the second objection and explains why outward action indicates lack of tasdiq.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how outward actions can serve as evidence of inner disbelief without breaking the definition of iman as tasdiq.

Core Idea
Outward shirk indicates lack of inner tasdiq, but innermost faith is known only to God.

This lesson addresses a second objection to the definition of iman as tasdiq. Someone might claim that a person who verbally affirms the Prophet's message but then prostrates to the sun should still be considered a believer according to the tasdiq definition. The answer clarifies that the outward act is taken as evidence of a lack of genuine assent, and we judge accordingly in this world, while leaving inner realities to God.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection and answer
Read the Arabic passage once, then the translation. Notice the structure: first the objection, then the answer introduced by 'قلنا'.
The objection is presented as a hypothetical that seems to contradict consensus.
2
Identify the two key claims
The objector claims that tasdiq plus prostration should equal believer. The answer claims that prostration outwardly indicates no tasdiq.
The answer does not deny the possibility of hidden tasdiq, but focuses on outward judgment.
3
Understand the distinction between outward and inner
Note that the answer distinguishes between how we judge in this world (by outward) and how God judges (by inner reality).
This protects the definition of iman as tasdiq from being invalidated by the objection.
4
Reflect on the implications
Ask yourself: does this mean a person can be a disbeliever outwardly but a believer inwardly? What does that tell us about the role of action in defining iman?
This lesson helps clarify the primacy of tasdiq while accounting for external indicators.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الثاني: من صدق بما جاء به النبي و مع ذلك سجد للشمس ينبغي أن يكون مؤمناً، والإجماع على خلافه، قلنا : هو دليل عدم التصديق أي سجوده لها يدل بظاهره على أنه ليس بمصدق ونحن نحكم بالظاهر فلذلك حكمنا بعدم إيمانه لا لأن عدم السجود لغير الله داخل في حقيقة الإيمان حتى لو علم أنه لم يسجد لها على سبيل التعظيم واعتقاد الإلهية بل سجد لها وقلبه مطمئن بالتصديق لم يحكم بكفره فيما بينه وبين الله)، وإن أجري عليه حكم الكفر في الظاهر .
The second: Whoever affirms what the Prophet brought, yet despite that prostrates to the sun, should be a believer, but the consensus is contrary. We say: This is evidence of lack of assent, i.e., his prostration to it indicates outwardly that he is not an assenter, and we judge by the outward appearance; therefore we judge that he is not a believer, not because refraining from prostration to other than God is part of the reality of faith, so that even if we knew that he did not prostrate to it out of veneration and belief in divinity, but prostrated to it with his heart at peace with assent, we would not judge him as a disbeliever in his relation to God, even though the ruling of disbelief is applied to him outwardly.
Source pages p. 358

Commentary
This passage presents the second objection: if a person affirms all that the Prophet brought (i.e., verbally expresses tasdiq) but then prostrates to the sun, common sense and consensus say he is not a believer. The objector argues that tasdiq is present, so he should be a believer. The answer clarifies that such an outward act of prostration is taken as evidence that the person is not genuinely assenting in his heart. We judge by the outward signs, and thus we rule that he is a disbeliever. However, the ruling is based on the apparent lack of tasdiq, not because the act of prostration is itself a part of the definition of iman. If it were known that his heart truly assented and he prostrated without veneration (e.g., under coercion or in error), then before God he would not be a disbeliever, even though the outward judgment remains one of disbelief for practical purposes (e.g., legal rulings). Thus, this objection does not undermine the definition of iman as tasdiq.

Key Terms
التصديق (al-tasdiq)
سجد للشمس (sajada lil-shams)
الظاهر (al-zahir)
حكم الكفر (hukm al-kufr)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some may think that a single outward act of shirk automatically negates tasdiq in the heart, or that the definition of iman must include avoidance of such acts.
Correction: The answer clarifies that the act is evidence of lack of tasdiq, but inner faith could theoretically coexist with an exceptional external act, and only God knows the heart. The definition of iman remains tasdiq; the ruler judges by outward signs.

Checkpoint
According to the answer to the second objection, why does prostration to the sun lead to a judgment of disbelief?
A) Because prostration to the sun is itself a part of the reality of disbelief.
B) Because it outwardly indicates lack of genuine assent, and we judge by the outward.
C) Because true tasdiq requires not prostrating to anything else.
D) Because the person denied the oneness of God.

Key Takeaway
The passage shows that outward acts can indicate inner disbelief but do not alter the definition of iman as tasdiq.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you perform an outward act of worship, do you examine your heart to ensure it aligns with inner tasdiq, or do you rely on the act alone? Challenge yourself to be honest about one action this week.
?
Have you ever judged someone's iman based on an outward mistake, forgetting that their heart may be with tasdiq before God? How can you change this habit starting today?
?
The passage teaches that we judge by outward, but God knows the inner. Are you more concerned with appearing righteous to people or with your secret state before God? What would you need to change to prioritize the latter?

The Third Objection and the Shared Obligation
Sanusi presents the third objection and his first counterargument.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the objection from Quran 12:106 is defused using the argument of shared obligation (ishtirak al-ilzam).

Core Idea
The objection cuts both ways, so it fails to refute the doctrine.

The third objection attempts to prove that iman cannot be tasdiq because the Quran mentions both iman and shirk together. Sanusi first responds by pointing out that the same problem applies to the objectors' own position: if shirk contradicts iman by consensus, then any definition of iman that allows shirk is problematic for them too. This first move neutralizes the objection without yet resolving it fully.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection from the verse
Read the Arabic of the verse and the statement of the objection: that it shows coexistence of iman and shirk.
Notice the logical structure: if iman = tasdiq of everything, then shirk denies tawhid, so the verse cannot refer to that iman.
2
Follow the initial response
The response begins with 'قلنا' and presents the shared obligation argument. Identify why the same problem applies to the opponents.
The opponents define iman as acts, but shirk also contradicts those acts.
3
Understand 'shared obligation'
This is a standard kalam technique: show that the argument proves too much and also refutes the opponent's view. Here, the coexistence of shirk disproves both sides' definitions.
Thus the objection is neutralized without defining iman yet.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الثالث : قوله تعالى : ﴿ وما يؤمن أكثرهم بالله إلا وهم مشركون ﴾ [يوسف: ١٠٦] فإنه يدل على اجتماع الإيمان مع الشرك، والتصديق بجميع ما جاء به الرسول لا يجامع الشرك لأن التوحيد مما علم مجيئه به فلا يكون الإيمان عبارة عن ذلك التصديق قلنا : ذلك الذي ذكرتموه مشترك الإلزام لأن الشرك مناف للإيمان إجماعاً، وفعل الواجبات لا ينافيه فلا يكون إيماناً ثم نقول في حله إن الإيمان المعدى بالباء هو التصديق ولم يقصد الله ظاهراً وهو به في الآية التصديق بجميع ما علم مجيئه في الدين بل بما قيد به سبحانه وتعالى والتصديق بالله لا ينافي الشرك إذ لعله بوجوده وصفاته الحقيقة هو لا بالتوحيد) الذي هو من الصفات السلبية، وحاصله أن الإيمان في اللغة التصديق مطلقاً وفي الشرع هو التصديق مقيداً بأمر مخصوص هو جميع ما علم كونه من الدين ضرورة والمذكور في الآية محمول على معناه اللغوي، واعلم أن الإمام الرازي قرر في النهاية الوجه الثالث هكذا : المراد بالإيمان هنا التصديق وهو مجامع للشرك فالإيمان الذي لا يجامع الشرك وجب أن يكون مغايراً للتصديق، ثم أجاب عنه بأن ذلك حجة عليكم لأن أفعال الواجبات قد تجامع الشرك والإيمان لا يجامعه فدل على أن فعل الواجبات ليس بالإيمان وعلى هذا التقرير يظهر اشتراك الإلزام لا على ما في الكتاب. قوله : ( لا على ما في الكتاب حيث بدل التقرير وأقام المنافاة مقام المجامعة، فليتأمل.
The third: The saying of God Most High: 'And most of them do not believe in God except while they are associating partners [with Him]' (12:106). For it indicates the coexistence of faith (al-iman) with association (al-shirk), but assent (al-tasdiq) to everything the Messenger brought does not coexist with association because monotheism (al-tawhid) is among what is known to have been brought by him, so faith cannot be that assent. We say: That which you mentioned is a shared obligation (ishtirak al-ilzam) because association is contrary to faith by consensus, and performing obligatory acts does not contradict it, so that [i.e., performing obligations] is not faith. Then we say in resolving it: Faith qualified with the preposition bi- (iman billah) is assent, but God manifestly did not intend in the verse assent to everything known to have been brought in the religion, but rather to what is qualified by Him, Most High, namely assent to God which does not negate association, for it is perhaps with respect to His existence and essential attributes, not with respect to monotheism which is among the negative attributes. The summary is that faith in language is absolute assent, and in the revealed law (al-shar‘) it is assent restricted to a specific matter, which is everything known necessarily to be of the religion. And the mention in the verse is taken in its linguistic sense. Know that Imam al-Razi established in al-Nihayah the third aspect thus: What is meant by faith here is assent, and it coexists with association; so faith that does not coexist with association must be different from assent. Then he answered that this is an argument against you because acts of obligations may coexist with association, while faith does not coexist with it, so it indicates that performing obligations is not faith. According to this formulation, the shared obligation becomes apparent, not as in the book. His saying: 'Not as in the book' because he changed the formulation and put opposition in place of coexistence, so reflect.
Source pages p. 358

Commentary
This passage presents the third objection, which uses the Quranic verse 12:106 to argue that iman can coexist with shirk (polytheism). The objectors claim that if iman is tasdiq of all that the Prophet brought, then it cannot coexist with shirk because tawhid (monotheism) is a necessary part of that message. Therefore iman must be something else. The answer consists of two parts. First, the argument is a 'shared obligation' (ishtirak al-ilzam), meaning it applies equally to the objectors: shirk contradicts iman by consensus, and their definition of iman (as acts) would also be contradicted by shirk, so their own position is problematic. Second, the verse is interpreted linguistically: the iman mentioned is the linguistic meaning of 'assent' (tasdiq), without the full religious restrictions. In Arabic, 'iman billah' (to believe in God) can mean merely to acknowledge His existence and attributes, which polytheists do. The technical religious definition of iman—assent to everything necessarily known from the Prophet—is not what the verse intends. The passage then mentions Imam al-Razi's treatment, which frames the objection differently: he says that if the verse is taken to mean that iman (as tasdiq) coexists with shirk, then the iman that does not coexist with shirk must be other than tasdiq. Al-Razi's answer is that this is a proof against the objectors because acts of worship coexist with shirk (polytheists may perform some duties), but iman does not, so acts of worship are not iman. The marginal note at the end clarifies that al-Razi changed the argument from coexistence to opposition. The commentary thus preserves the nuance and shows the depth of the refutation.

Key Terms
الإيمان (al-iman)
الشرك (al-shirk)
التصديق (al-tasdiq)
اشتراك الإلزام (ishtirak al-ilzam)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the main purpose of the shared obligation argument?
A) To prove that iman is tasdiq
B) To show that the verse also refutes the opponents' definition
C) To deny the authenticity of the verse
D) To argue that shirk is not a sin

Key Takeaway
The shared obligation argument neutralizes the verse objection without yet defining iman.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The verse says even those who believe often still commit shirk. Examine your own life: in what subtle ways might you be associating something with Allah (e.g., seeking approval of people, trusting in your own plans)? What concrete step will you take today to purify your intention solely for Allah?
?
Sanusi's argument shows that a flawed objection does not prove your position is correct. Are you willing to honestly examine criticisms of your beliefs, even if they challenge your comfort? What would it mean for you to seek truth over victory in discussions about faith?
?
The shared obligation humbles both sides, reminding us that we do not have a monopoly on truth. When you disagree with another Muslim on a matter of creed, do you listen with the intention to learn, or with the intention to prove yourself right? How can you change your approach starting today?

Linguistic vs. Religious Definition of Iman
Sanusi resolves the verse by distinguishing linguistic and religious iman, then notes Imam al-Razi's approach.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Sanusi distinguishes between the linguistic and religious meanings of iman to resolve the verse and how Imam al-Razi's alternative formulation reinforces the argument.

Core Idea
The verse uses 'iman' in its linguistic sense, not the technical religious definition.

After the shared obligation argument, Sanusi provides a direct resolution: the word 'iman' in Quran 12:106 is used in its linguistic sense of simple assent, not the full religious sense that includes assent to everything necessary. This distinction allows the verse to be true without contradicting the technical definition. The passage also includes an alternative formulation by Imam al-Razi that highlights the same point.

Guided Path
1
Read the resolution part
Start from 'ثم نقول في حله' and read through the explanation that the verse refers to the linguistic meaning.
Note the key point: 'الإيمان في اللغة التصديق مطلقاً وفي الشرع هو التصديق مقيداً'
2
Understand the linguistic vs. religious distinction
See how Sanusi explains that 'iman billah' can simply mean acknowledging God's existence, which polytheists do. The full religious iman requires accepting all necessary truths like tawhid.
3
Examine Imam al-Razi's alternative
Read the last part where al-Razi's formulation is mentioned. He poses the objection as: if the verse means iman (as tasdiq) coexists with shirk, then the iman that does not coexist must be different from tasdiq. His answer: that proves acts are not iman.
This shows a variant of the shared obligation argument.
4
Reflect on the marginal note
The phrase 'لا على ما في الكتاب' indicates that the author points out a difference in how the argument is presented.
This highlights careful attention to textual accuracy.

?

?

Argument from the Hadith 'Faith Has Seventy-Something Branches'
Sanusi presents the opponents' argument and then deconstructs it with objections and linguistic analysis.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the hadith 'Faith has seventy-some branches' is used to argue that iman includes actions, and the nuanced responses that challenge a straightforward reading.

Core Idea
The hadith's 'highest branch' creates a paradox if shahada is superior to heartfelt assent.

This passage delves into a key proof text in the debate about the definition of iman. Opponents of the view that iman is only heartfelt assent use the hadith listing branches of faith, with the shahada as the highest. However, the commentary shows that this interpretation raises its own difficulties, leading to a series of subtle responses.

Guided Path
1
Read the hadith and the opponents' claim
Read the Arabic passage and translation. Notice how the opponents use the hadith to support their definition of iman as including all acts of obedience or as a composite of assent, confession, and action.
The hadith is quoted at the beginning.
2
Identify the objection raised
The text presents an objection: if the shahada is the highest branch, then it would be superior to heartfelt assent itself, which is problematic. Understand why this objection matters.
This objection challenges the coherence of the opponents' interpretation.
3
Examine the three responses
Three responses are offered to the objection: (1) the hadith is specified by consensus, (2) superiority is in specific aspects like protecting life, (3) the elative indicates absolute increase, not over the annexed term. Compare these responses.
Each response tries to preserve the hadith's authority while avoiding the problematic implication.
4
Analyze the discussion on 'biḍ‘' and the number
The passage then explains the meaning of 'biḍ‘' and the significance of the number seventy. Some say it indicates mere multiplicity, others that it means seventy-seven. Consider how this discussion affects the argument.
This shows the hadith's wording is open to interpretation.

احتج الآخرون القائلون بأن الإيمان فعل الطاعات بأسرها، والقائلون بأن الإيمان فعل الطاعات بأسرها والقائلون بأنه مركب من التصديق والإقرار والعمل جميعاً ، بقوله عليه السلام : الإيمان بضع وسبعون شعبة أعلاها قول لا إله إلا الله. قوله : ( والقائلون بأنه مركب إلخ يرد عليهم أن الحديث على ما حملوه عليه يدل على أن الشهادة أعلى وأفضل من التصديق القلبي بجميع ما علم مجيء الرسول صلى الله عليه وسلم بالضرورة، وليس كذلك ويمكن أن يدفع بأنه مخصص بالإجماع أو يريد أنها أفضل منه من حيث أنها تحقن الدماء والإذلال لأنها أفضل منه من كل وجه أو المراد بإضافة أفعل التفضيل هاهنا الزيادة المطلقة لا الزيادة على المضاف إليه أي المشهور المعروف من بينها بالفضل بين أهل الملل. قوله : لا إله إلا الله، وبهذا أيضاً يندفع ما قيل من أن الحديث يدل على أفضلية قولنا : لا إله إلا الله محمد رسول الله من الصلاة والحج وأمثالهما مع أن الأمر بالعكس، وأما ما قيل في الجواب أن أفضليته من حيث أنه سبب لعصمة الأنفس والدماء والأموال لا مطلقاً وفيه بحث لأن من الصلاة والحج الشرعيين أيضاً كذلك، وقد يقال : أفضليته من حيث أنه أساس الكل وما لم يوجد لم يعتبر شيئا منها ففيه حيثيتان ليستا في غيره من الأعمال وهو التقدم والتوقف عليه . قوله: (الإيمان بضع وسبعون أي الإيمان الكامل الذي في أعلى مراتبه وهو المشتمل على أصوله وفروعه الإسلامية وفرضها وواجبها ومندوبها والبضع في العدد بكسر الباء، وبعض العرب يفتحها، وهو ما بين الثلاث إلى التسع وهذا الحديث حجة على الجوهري، حيث زعم أنه يقال : بضع سنين وبضعة عشر رجلاً وبضع عشرة امرأة، فإذا جاوز لفظة العشرة ذهب البضع فلا يقال بضع وعشرون . قيل : المراد بالعدد المذكور مجرد الكثرة لا الحصر لأن خصائل الإيمان أكثر منه، ويدور في خلدي والله أعلم أن المراد المبالغة في الكثرة لأن سبعين يستعمل للكثرة كثيراً قال الله تعالى : في سلسلة ذرعها سبعون ذراعاً [الحاقة: ٣٢]، فلما زاد على ما يستعمل في الكثرة بعد ذكره آل المعنى إلى أنه أكثر من الكثير، وذهب بعض العلماء إلى أن المراد سبع وسبعون وقد جاء في رواية الإيمان سبع وسبعون ونص الراغب الأصفهاني في
The others who say that faith (al-iman) is the performance of all acts of obedience, and those who say that it is composed of assent (al-tasdiq), verbal confession (al-iqrar), and action (‘amal) altogether, argued by his saying, peace be upon him: 'Faith has seventy-some branches, the highest of which is the saying: There is no god but God.' His saying: (And those who say that it is composed, etc.) It is objected against them that the hadith, according to their interpretation, indicates that the testimony is higher and better than heartfelt assent to everything known necessarily that the Messenger brought, and that is not the case. It can be repelled by saying it is specified by consensus, or that it means it is better than it in that it protects blood and honor, because it is better than it from every aspect, or by intending by the addition of the elative here absolute increase, not increase over the annexed term—i.e., the well-known and famous among them for merit among the people of the religions. His saying: 'There is no god but God'—and by this also is refuted what is said that the hadith indicates the superiority of our saying 'There is no god but God, Muhammad is the Messenger of God' over prayer, pilgrimage and the like, although the opposite is true. As for what is said in the answer, that its superiority is in that it is the cause of the inviolability of lives, blood and property, not absolutely, there is discussion in it, because the prescribed prayer and pilgrimage also [do that]. It may be said that its superiority is in that it is the foundation of all, and if it did not exist, none of them would be considered; so it has two aspects that are not present in other acts: precedence and dependence upon it. His saying: (Faith has seventy-some branches) meaning complete faith in its highest degrees, which includes its Islamic principles and branches, its obligatory duties, required acts, and recommended acts. The word biḍ‘ (some) in number is with kasra of the ba’, and some Arabs open it, and it means between three and nine. This hadith is evidence against Jawhari, who claimed that it is said 'biḍ‘ years' and 'biḍ‘ atashar rajulan' and 'biḍ‘ ashrat imra’ah', and if the word surpasses ten, the word biḍ‘ is no longer used, so it is not said 'biḍ‘ wa-‘ishrun'. It is said: what is meant by the mentioned number is mere multiplicity, not enumeration, because the characteristics of faith are more than that. And it occurs to my mind—and God knows best—that the intended meaning is exaggeration in multiplicity because seventy is often used for multiplicity, as God Most High said: 'in a chain whose length is seventy cubits' (69:32). When it increased beyond what is used for multiplicity after mentioning it, the meaning becomes 'more than much'. Some scholars hold that the intended number is seventy-seven, and it has come in a narration 'faith is seventy-seven branches', and al-Raghib al-Isfahani stated in...
Source pages p. 359

Commentary
This passage presents the argument from the hadith 'Faith has seventy-some branches, the highest of which is 'There is no god but God'. Opponents who define iman as doing all acts of obedience, or as a composite of belief, confession, and action, use this hadith to support their view because the branches include actions. The commentary then discusses several points. First, an objection is raised: if the hadith is taken as listing branches, then the highest branch—the shahada—would be superior to heartfelt assent to all necessary teachings, which seems problematic since heartfelt assent is the core. Several responses are given: the hadith might be specified by consensus; or the superiority is specific to certain functions like protecting life and property; or the elative 'a'la' indicates absolute superiority in a specific aspect, not superiority over everything. Second, the phrase 'There is no god but God' is discussed: it is the foundation, and all other acts depend on it, so it has precedence. Third, the meaning of 'biḍ‘' (between three and nine) and the number seventy are clarified: the number is not meant literally but indicates multiplicity, and there are variant narrations. This lesson shows the complexity of using this hadith to define iman.

Key Terms
الإيمان (al-iman)
بضع وسبعون شعبة (biḍ‘ wa-sab‘una shu‘bah)
الشهادة (al-shahada)
التصديق القلبي (al-tasdiq al-qalbi)
أفعل التفضيل (af‘al al-tafḍil)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think the hadith straightforwardly proves that iman includes actions, and that the 'highest branch' (shahada) is superior to inner belief.
Correction: The commentary shows that if shahada is the highest branch, it would be superior to heartfelt assent, which is problematic. Various responses mitigate this, and the number is not literal but indicates multiplicity.

Checkpoint
Why do some scholars object to using the hadith 'Faith has seventy-some branches' to argue that the testimony is superior to heartfelt assent?
A) The hadith is weak.
B) If the testimony is the highest branch, it would imply it is superior to the very assent that is the foundation of faith.
C) The hadith only refers to external actions, not inner belief.
D) The branches are all equal.

Key Takeaway
The hadith does not simply prove that action is part of faith; the meaning of 'highest branch' is nuanced, and the number seventy indicates multiplicity rather than an exact count.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You just read that the 'highest branch' of faith is the shahada, but the commentary warns that this does not mean it is superior in every way. In your own practice, do you treat the shahada as the mere starting point, or as the living foundation that should shape every other act? What would change if you truly saw it as the root from which all branches grow?
?
The passage reveals that the number 'seventy' in the hadith is not meant to be precise but to indicate abundance. Are you guilty of reducing your faith to a checklist of actions, secretly believing that if you tick enough boxes you are safe? Where in your heart do you need to move from counting deeds to nurturing a living iman?
?
The argument in this lesson shows that the shahada protects lives and honor. In your daily interactions, do you treat the shahada of others as a shield, or do you allow judgments that deny the full weight of that testimony? What can you do today to honor the shahada of those around you, even when you disagree with their actions?

Faith and Disbelief

Two Aspects of Increase in Faith
Explains two ways faith increases: by intensity and by detailed belief.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the two distinct ways faith (iman) increases: through greater certainty and through detailed knowledge of revealed beliefs.

Core Idea
Faith increases both in intensity and in the number of specific beliefs.

This passage from al-Sanusi explains how faith can increase despite being a single act of belief. He refutes an objection that would make all believers equal, then presents two aspects: increase in certainty (e.g., Prophet Ibrahim's request) and increase in the number of articles believed in detail after summary belief. The passage also addresses a potential objection about detailed beliefs not truly increasing faith.

Guided Path
1
Understand the objection and its refutation
Read the opening lines where an objection is raised that would equate the faith of the Prophet and common believers. Note how it is refuted by consensus and by reference to Prophet Ibrahim's request for reassurance.
The objection is based on a misunderstanding of what faith is.
2
Identify the first aspect: increase in intensity
Find the explanation that certain belief (yaqin) can increase, as shown by Ibrahim's request. Also note that a strong assumption that excludes the opposite has the same ruling as certainty.
This aspect deals with the strength of conviction.
3
Identify the second aspect: increase in detailed belief
Look for the statement that detailed belief in individual items of revelation is a part of faith, distinct from the initial summary belief. This allows for increase as more details are known.
Faith increases by number of specific beliefs, not just intensity.
4
Address the objection about details
Consider the potential objection that learning details does not add new faith because they were already believed summarily. The reply clarifies that in the Prophet's time, the total body of revelation grew, so faith increased.
This shows the applicability of the second aspect historically.
5
See the scriptural evidence
Examine the verses cited: 'When His verses are recited to them, they increase them in faith' and 'that my heart may be at ease.' These support both aspects respectively.
The Qur'an itself indicates increase in both ways.
