The Fifteenth Proof: Criminals in Saqar
Sanusi refutes the 15th proof by abandoning the verse's apparent generality.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Sanusi's response to the fifteenth proof that Quran 74:40-46 condemns all criminals to Hell, showing that the verse's apparent meaning must be abandoned to avoid implying every criminal denies the Day of Judgment.

Core Idea
Quranic descriptions of Hell's inhabitants are not automatically universal over all sinners.

The fifteenth proof uses the description of criminals being asked why they entered Saqar, and they answer they used to deny the Day of Judgment. The objector argues this proves every criminal is a disbeliever. Sanusi gives the same refutation: the verse's apparent meaning would be false if applied to all sinners, so it is 'abandoned in its apparent meaning' (matrūk al-ẓāhir).

Guided Path
1
Read the objection and response
Read the Arabic and translation. The objector cites the dialogue in Surah al-Muddaththir. Note how they conclude that every 'criminal' (mujrim) is a disbeliever.
The term 'mujrim' is broader than just disbelievers.
2
Identify the parallel with the fourteenth
Sanusi says 'its answer has already passed'. Notice the identical structure: the verse's apparent meaning leads to a false consequence (every criminal denies Judgment).
3
Understand 'matrūk al-ẓāhir'
This phrase means the verse is not taken in its literal, general sense. It is interpreted more narrowly. This is a key tool for reconciling revelation with rational principles.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الرابع عشر : قوله تعالى : وأما الذين فسقوا فمأواهم النار ﴾ [السجدة : ٢٠] الآية ) وتمامها كلما أرادوا أن يخرجوا منها أعيدوا فيها وقيل لهم ذوقوا عذاب النار الذي كنتم به تكذبون ﴾ [السجدة : ٢٠ ]، فإنه يدل على أن كل فاسق كافر ( قلنا : ) ليس قوله : وأما الذين فسقوا ﴾ [السجدة : ٢٠ ]، باقياً على عمومه الظاهر لأنه ( يقتضي أن كل فاسق مكذب بالقيامة وإنه باطل قطعاً. الخامس عشر قوله تعالى: يتساءلون عن المجرمين ما سلككم في سقر [المدثر : ٤٠-٤٢ ] إلى قوله : وكنا نكذب بيوم الدين ﴾ [المدثر : ٤٦] فثبت بذلك أن كل مجرم داخل في النار كافر ولا شبهة في أن الفاسق مجرم لا يدخل النار، قلنا : قد مر جوابه ) وهو أن الآية متروكة الظاهر وإلا لزم كون كل مجرم مكذباً القيامة وهو باطل قطعاً. السادس عشر قوله تعالى : وسيق الذين كفروا ﴾ [ الزمر : ٧١] إلى قوله وسيق الذين اتقوا [ الزمر : ۷۳] إذ يعلم منه أن الإنسان إما متق يساق إلى الجنة أو كافر يساق إلى النار (و) الجواب عنه : (قد مر مثله)، وهو أن ذكر قسمين لا يدل على عدم قسم ثالث . ( السابع عشر : قوله عليه السلام من ترك صلاة متعمداً فقد كفر) وقوله عليه السلام : من مات ولم يحج فليمت إن شاء يهودياً وإن شاء نصرانياً قلنا : الآحاد لا تعارض الإجماع المنعقد قبل حدوث المخالفين والثامن عشر ولاية الله وعداوته ضدان فلا واسطة بينهما وولاية الله إيمان وعداوته كفر، قلنا : لا نسلم عدم بين كل ضدين) فإن السواد والبياض متضادان وبينهما واسطة فجاز أن يكون بين ولايته وعداوته واسطة
The fourteenth: His saying, exalted be He: 'But as for those who defiantly disobeyed (fasaqū), their refuge is the Fire' [as-Sajdah 32:20] – the verse, and its completion: 'Every time they wish to emerge from it, they are returned to it, and it is said to them, "Taste the punishment of the Fire which you used to deny."' [32:20]. Indeed, it indicates that every sinner (fāsiq) is a disbeliever. We said: His saying: 'But as for those who defiantly disobeyed' [32:20] is not remaining on its apparent generality because it would require that every sinner denies the Resurrection, and that is definitively false. The fifteenth: His saying, exalted be He: 'They will ask one another about the criminals, "What put you into Saqar?"' [al-Muddaththir 74:40-42] up to His saying: 'And we used to deny the Day of Judgment.' [74:46] So it is established by that that every criminal entering the Fire is a disbeliever, and there is no doubt that the sinner is a criminal [who enters the Fire]. We said: Its answer has already passed: the verse is abandoned in its apparent meaning, otherwise it would require that every criminal denies the Resurrection, and that is definitively false. The sixteenth: His saying, exalted be He: 'And those who disbelieved will be driven' [az-Zumar 39:71] up to His saying: 'And those who feared their Lord will be led' [39:73]. For it is known from it that a person is either a righteous person (muttaqī) led to Paradise or a disbeliever led to the Fire. The answer to it has already passed similarly: the mention of two groups does not indicate the absence of a third group. The seventeenth: His saying, peace be upon him: 'Whoever intentionally leaves a prayer has disbelieved,' and his saying, peace be upon him: 'Whoever dies without performing Hajj, let him die as a Jew if he wishes or as a Christian if he wishes.' We said: Isolated reports (āḥād) do not contradict the consensus (ijmāʿ) that was established before the emergence of dissenters. The eighteenth: The friendship (wilāyah) of God and His enmity (ʿadāwah) are two opposites, so there is no intermediate state between them; and the friendship of God is faith and His enmity is disbelief. We said: We do not concede that there is no intermediate state between every pair of opposites. For black and white are opposites and there is an intermediate between them. So it is possible that there is an intermediate between His friendship and His enmity.
Source pages p. 368

Commentary
This passage presents five concise objections and responses (the fourteenth through eighteenth). The pattern is similar: each proof from revelation or tradition is refuted by showing that its apparent meaning leads to an absurd consequence or that it is not general. The fourteenth proof from Quran 32:20 (about sinners entering the Fire and being told it is because they used to deny) is refuted by noting that it would require every sinner to deny the Resurrection, which is false. The fifteenth from Quran 74:40-46 makes a similar argument about criminals, and the response is the same: the verse's apparent meaning is abandoned. The sixteenth from Quran 39:71-73 divides people into two groups (believers and disbelievers), but the response says mentioning two groups does not preclude a third. The seventeenth uses two hadiths (one about leaving prayer, one about dying without Hajj), and the response says isolated reports (āḥād) cannot override the consensus that existed before these reports emerged. The eighteenth argues that God's friendship and enmity are opposites with no middle ground, but the response gives the counterexample of black and white, which have intermediate colors, so there can be an intermediate state—faith-disobedience without being a disbeliever. These responses demonstrate the Ashʿarī method of defending the possibility of a third category (the fāsiq) between faith and disbelief.

Key Terms
متروك الظاهر
مجرم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is Sanusi's response to the fifteenth proof?
A) The verse is abrogated by other verses.
B) The verse applies only to polytheists.
C) The verse's apparent meaning is abandoned because it would imply every criminal denies Judgment.
D) The verse is a warning to believers.

Key Takeaway
Scriptural passages that mention Hell's inhabitants must be understood in their restricted context, not as general statements about all sinners.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you read the criminals in Saqar saying 'We were not of those who prayed,' does that sting? Are there moments in your day when you rush through prayer or skip it entirely—and do you brush it off with 'I still believe'? What would it take to make that prayer the anchor of your faith, not a routine you break without regret?
?
This lesson shows that a single verse, taken out of context, can lead to a terrible error about God's mercy. Have you ever used a Quranic verse to justify your own laziness or sin, assuming you are safe because 'I'm not that bad'? What is one area where you have been comfortable in your disobedience, and what is the first step you will take today to change it?

The Sixteenth Proof: Two Groups in Az-Zumar
Sanusi refutes the 16th proof by denying that binary division is exhaustive.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Sanusi argues that dividing people into two groups (believers and disbelievers) does not preclude a third intermediate group of sinful believers.

Core Idea
Mentioning only two categories does not prove there is no third.

The sixteenth proof cites Quran 39:71-73, which describes the disbelievers being driven to Hell and the righteous being led to Paradise. The objector argues this proves there are only two categories: believer (righteous) and disbeliever. But Sanusi points out that mentioning two groups does not rule out a third intermediate group. This lesson explains that simple binary divisions in scripture are not exhaustive.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection and response
Read the Arabic and translation. The objector uses the two verses about the 'drive' of the disbelievers and the 'lead' of the righteous. Note the conclusion: humanity is either believer or disbeliever.
The verses describe two groups, but not necessarily all possible groups.
2
Identify the logical flaw
Sanusi says 'the mention of two groups does not indicate the absence of a third group'. This is a basic logical point: an enumeration of examples does not preclude other categories.
This is similar to the fallacy of false dilemma.
3
Apply to the fāsiq
The third group is the fāsiq (sinful believer). The Quran does not mention them in those verses, but that doesn't mean they don't exist.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: If the Quran only speaks of believers and disbelievers in a passage, then those are the only categories.

The Seventeenth Proof: Hadith Threats
Sanusi refutes hadith-based proofs by prioritizing consensus over isolated reports.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Sanusi defends against hadith-based objections by arguing that isolated reports (āḥād) cannot contradict established consensus (ijmāʿ).

Core Idea
Isolated hadith cannot override the consensus of the community.

The seventeenth proof uses two hadiths: 'Whoever leaves prayer intentionally has disbelieved' and 'Whoever dies without Hajj, let him die as a Jew or Christian'. The objector claims these prove major sins cause disbelief. Sanusi responds that these are isolated reports (āḥād) and cannot contradict the consensus (ijmāʿ) that existed before these reports appeared. This lesson explains the hierarchy of sources in Islamic legal-theological reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Read the hadiths and response
Read the Arabic and translation. Note the two hadiths: one about prayer, one about Hajj. They seem to equate leaving a pillar with disbelief.
These are well-known hadiths used by some to argue for takfīr.
2
Understand the concept of āḥād
Isolated reports (āḥād) are hadiths that have not reached the level of tawātur (mass transmission). In Islamic methodology, they do not establish certainty.
3
See the role of consensus
Sanusi says these āḥād reports cannot contradict the consensus (ijmāʿ) that was already established before the dissenters appeared. The consensus was that major sins do not remove faith.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: All authentic hadiths must be taken literally and applied universally, even if they conflict with consensus.
Correction: In classical methodology, āḥād hadiths cannot overrule established ijmāʿ. They require interpretation or contextualization.

Checkpoint
Why does Sanusi reject the hadiths as proof that leaving prayer causes disbelief?
A) The hadiths are weak in transmission.
B) They are isolated reports (āḥād) that cannot contradict prior consensus.
C) They are metaphorical.
D) They were abrogated by later verses.

Key Takeaway
Consensus (ijmāʿ) takes precedence over isolated reports (āḥād) in establishing theological doctrine.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you encounter a hadith that seems to say something different from what you believe, do you immediately assume your belief is wrong, or do you investigate whether the hadith has been properly understood in light of the broader consensus of scholars? How does this lesson challenge your approach?
?
Are there areas of your faith where you hold a view based on a single text, ignoring the weight of scholarly consensus? Are you willing to submit to the collective understanding of the early community, even if it requires reinterpreting a text?
?
If you were asked to defend the position that major sins do not lead to disbelief, could you do so using the methodological principle of this lesson—consensus over isolated reports—or would you rely on your own reasoning? What does your answer reveal about your trust in the tradition?

The Eighteenth Proof: Opposites Have No Middle Ground?
Sanusi refutes the claim that opposites have no middle, using black and white as an example.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Sanusi's refutation of the argument that God's friendship and enmity are strict opposites with no intermediate state, using the counterexample of black and white.

Core Idea
Not every pair of opposites lack an intermediate; there can be a middle state.

The eighteenth proof claims that God's friendship (wilāyah) and enmity (ʿadāwah) are opposites, and opposites have no middle ground. Therefore, a person must be either a friend (believer) or enemy (disbeliever) of God. Sanusi counters by giving the example of black and white: they are opposites but there is an intermediate color (grey). Similarly, there can be an intermediate state between God's friendship and enmity, namely the state of a sinful believer who is neither a friend in the complete sense nor an enemy. This lesson clarifies that absolute opposition does not always exclude intermediaries.

Guided Path
1
Read the argument and response
Read the Arabic and translation. The objector claims that since friendship and enmity are opposites, and opposites have no middle, one must be either a friend (believer) or enemy (disbeliever).
The term 'ḍiddān' means two opposites.
2
Examine the counterexample
Sanusi provides the example of black and white (sawād wa bayāḍ). They are opposites but there is an intermediate (e.g., grey). This shows the premise 'no intermediate between opposites' is false.
3
Apply to faith and sin
The intermediate state is the fāsiq (sinful believer). He has faith (wilāyah from one angle) but also sin (enmity from another), though not complete enmity.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الرابع عشر : قوله تعالى : وأما الذين فسقوا فمأواهم النار ﴾ [السجدة : ٢٠] الآية ) وتمامها كلما أرادوا أن يخرجوا منها أعيدوا فيها وقيل لهم ذوقوا عذاب النار الذي كنتم به تكذبون ﴾ [السجدة : ٢٠ ]، فإنه يدل على أن كل فاسق كافر ( قلنا : ) ليس قوله : وأما الذين فسقوا ﴾ [السجدة : ٢٠ ]، باقياً على عمومه الظاهر لأنه ( يقتضي أن كل فاسق مكذب بالقيامة وإنه باطل قطعاً. الخامس عشر قوله تعالى: يتساءلون عن المجرمين ما سلككم في سقر [المدثر : ٤٠-٤٢ ] إلى قوله : وكنا نكذب بيوم الدين ﴾ [المدثر : ٤٦] فثبت بذلك أن كل مجرم داخل في النار كافر ولا شبهة في أن الفاسق مجرم لا يدخل النار، قلنا : قد مر جوابه ) وهو أن الآية متروكة الظاهر وإلا لزم كون كل مجرم مكذباً القيامة وهو باطل قطعاً. السادس عشر قوله تعالى : وسيق الذين كفروا ﴾ [ الزمر : ٧١] إلى قوله وسيق الذين اتقوا [ الزمر : ۷۳] إذ يعلم منه أن الإنسان إما متق يساق إلى الجنة أو كافر يساق إلى النار (و) الجواب عنه : (قد مر مثله)، وهو أن ذكر قسمين لا يدل على عدم قسم ثالث . ( السابع عشر : قوله عليه السلام من ترك صلاة متعمداً فقد كفر) وقوله عليه السلام : من مات ولم يحج فليمت إن شاء يهودياً وإن شاء نصرانياً قلنا : الآحاد لا تعارض الإجماع المنعقد قبل حدوث المخالفين والثامن عشر ولاية الله وعداوته ضدان فلا واسطة بينهما وولاية الله إيمان وعداوته كفر، قلنا : لا نسلم عدم بين كل ضدين) فإن السواد والبياض متضادان وبينهما واسطة فجاز أن يكون بين ولايته وعداوته واسطة
The fourteenth: His saying, exalted be He: 'But as for those who defiantly disobeyed (fasaqū), their refuge is the Fire' [as-Sajdah 32:20] – the verse, and its completion: 'Every time they wish to emerge from it, they are returned to it, and it is said to them, "Taste the punishment of the Fire which you used to deny."' [32:20]. Indeed, it indicates that every sinner (fāsiq) is a disbeliever. We said: His saying: 'But as for those who defiantly disobeyed' [32:20] is not remaining on its apparent generality because it would require that every sinner denies the Resurrection, and that is definitively false. The fifteenth: His saying, exalted be He: 'They will ask one another about the criminals, "What put you into Saqar?"' [al-Muddaththir 74:40-42] up to His saying: 'And we used to deny the Day of Judgment.' [74:46] So it is established by that that every criminal entering the Fire is a disbeliever, and there is no doubt that the sinner is a criminal [who enters the Fire]. We said: Its answer has already passed: the verse is abandoned in its apparent meaning, otherwise it would require that every criminal denies the Resurrection, and that is definitively false. The sixteenth: His saying, exalted be He: 'And those who disbelieved will be driven' [az-Zumar 39:71] up to His saying: 'And those who feared their Lord will be led' [39:73]. For it is known from it that a person is either a righteous person (muttaqī) led to Paradise or a disbeliever led to the Fire. The answer to it has already passed similarly: the mention of two groups does not indicate the absence of a third group. The seventeenth: His saying, peace be upon him: 'Whoever intentionally leaves a prayer has disbelieved,' and his saying, peace be upon him: 'Whoever dies without performing Hajj, let him die as a Jew if he wishes or as a Christian if he wishes.' We said: Isolated reports (āḥād) do not contradict the consensus (ijmāʿ) that was established before the emergence of dissenters. The eighteenth: The friendship (wilāyah) of God and His enmity (ʿadāwah) are two opposites, so there is no intermediate state between them; and the friendship of God is faith and His enmity is disbelief. We said: We do not concede that there is no intermediate state between every pair of opposites. For black and white are opposites and there is an intermediate between them. So it is possible that there is an intermediate between His friendship and His enmity.
Source pages p. 368

Commentary
This passage presents five concise objections and responses (the fourteenth through eighteenth). The pattern is similar: each proof from revelation or tradition is refuted by showing that its apparent meaning leads to an absurd consequence or that it is not general. The fourteenth proof from Quran 32:20 (about sinners entering the Fire and being told it is because they used to deny) is refuted by noting that it would require every sinner to deny the Resurrection, which is false. The fifteenth from Quran 74:40-46 makes a similar argument about criminals, and the response is the same: the verse's apparent meaning is abandoned. The sixteenth from Quran 39:71-73 divides people into two groups (believers and disbelievers), but the response says mentioning two groups does not preclude a third. The seventeenth uses two hadiths (one about leaving prayer, one about dying without Hajj), and the response says isolated reports (āḥād) cannot override the consensus that existed before these reports emerged. The eighteenth argues that God's friendship and enmity are opposites with no middle ground, but the response gives the counterexample of black and white, which have intermediate colors, so there can be an intermediate state—faith-disobedience without being a disbeliever. These responses demonstrate the Ashʿarī method of defending the possibility of a third category (the fāsiq) between faith and disbelief.

Key Terms
الضدان
واسطة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: If two things are opposites, they must be absolute with no middle ground.
Correction: Some opposites, like black and white, admit intermediate shades. So too, faith and disbelief can have a middle state (the fāsiq).

Checkpoint
What counterexample does Sanusi give to refute the claim that opposites have no intermediate?
A) Hot and cold
B) Day and night
C) Black and white
D) Life and death

Key Takeaway
The existence of an intermediate between opposites is possible, as shown by the example of black and white.

Will you now see them as an enemy of God or as someone in the intermediate state? What responsibility does that place on you to help them move towards complete friendship with God?
?
The lesson uses the example of black and white to show there is a grey area. Examine your own heart: Are you content to remain in a 'grey' state of faith, committing sins while hoping for mercy? Or does this lesson compel you to strive for the pure white of complete obedience? What is one thing you can change today to leave the grey zone?

The Grave Sinner Is Not a Hypocrite: Hadith Response
Sanusi addresses the claim that the grave sinner is a hypocrite, using a hadith and its interpretation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the hadith about the signs of a hypocrite is interpreted to exclude the grave sinner from being a hypocrite in faith.

Core Idea
The hadith’s signs point to hypocrisy in actions, not in religion.

This lesson examines a key argument that the grave sinner (fāsiq) is a hypocrite (munāfiq) based on a famous hadith. The response shows that the hadith cannot be taken literally and actually refers to hypocrisy in deeds, not in foundational belief. We will walk through the transmitted proof and its careful rebuttal.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage carefully, then its literal translation. Note the structure: claim, proof, response, and further interpretation.
The passage is dense with commentary; focus on the main argument.
2
Identify the claim and transmitted proof
The claim is that the grave sinner is a hypocrite. The proof is the hadith: 'The sign of the hypocrite is three...' Understand why this proof is initially compelling.
3
Understand the response
The response says the hadith is abandoned in its apparent meaning (matrūk al-ẓāhir). It then offers an alternative interpretation: it refers to hypocrisy in actions, not in religion. Note the logical arguments supporting this.
4
Consider the critique of al-Hasan's view
The commentator critiques a statement by al-Hasan al-Baṣrī, arguing that if hypocrisy is defined as inner disbelief with outer faith, then a person with inner faith and outer sin is the opposite, so the hadith does not apply.
5
Reflect on the doctrinal significance
This lesson reinforces that grave sins do not remove one from the faith (īmān) or make one a hypocrite in the theological sense. The distinction between hypocrisy in actions and in faith is crucial.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
احتج) من زعم أنه أي مرتكب الكبيرة ( منافق بوجهين ( الأول : ) نقلي وهو قوله عليه الصلاة والسلام : آية المنافق ثلاث إذا وعد أخلف وإذا حدث كذب وإذا اؤتمن خان قلنا هو متروك الظاهر لأن من وعد غيره أن يخلع عليه قوله : ( وإلا لزم كون كل مجرم مكذباً فالمراد المجرمون الكاملون أو المجرمون المخصوصون . قوله : ( والجواب عنه قد مر (مثله يمكن أن يجاب أيضاً بأن الانتفاء قد يكون بالتصديق عن التكذيب، ومقاتلة الكافر يدل عليه، ففساق المؤمنين يدخلون في الذين اتقوا، اللهم إلا أن يقال : سوق بعض فساقهم إلى النار مما لا شبهة فيه فيلزم أن يكونوا كافرين ولا قائل بالفصل وحينئذ يحتاج إلى جواب المتن قطعاً . قوله : ( وهو قوله عليه السلام : آية المنافق) قيل : يحتمل أن يراد المنافق في الأعمال لا في الدين، وقد يقال : لا معنى لقول الحسن رضي الله عنه لأن النفاق، إظهاراً لصلاح مع فساد الباطن، صلحت سريرته وظهر فساده فكان ضد المنافق، وأنت خبير بأنه إذا كان مراده والفاسق من بالمنافق مبطن الكفر ومظهر الإيمان لم يتجه ما ذكر.
He argued, whoever claims that the grave sinner is a hypocrite, with two proofs: The first is transmitted (naqlī): his saying, peace and blessings be upon him: 'The sign of the hypocrite is three: when he promises, he breaks; when he speaks, he lies; when he is entrusted, he betrays.' We said: It is abandoned in its apparent meaning, because whoever promises another that he will confer on him... His saying: 'And otherwise it would require that every criminal is a denier, so what is meant is the complete criminals or the specific criminals.' His saying: 'And the answer to it has already passed similarly. It can also be answered that the denial might be by affirmation of the denial, and fighting the disbeliever indicates it. So the sinners among the believers are included among the righteous (muttaqūn), unless it is said: The driving of some of their sinners to the Fire is indisputable, so it would require that they be disbelievers, and no one holds a distinction, and then a definitive answer to the text is needed.' His saying: 'And it is his saying, peace be upon him: The sign of the hypocrite.' It is said: It is possible that what is meant is the hypocrite in actions, not in religion. And it may be said: The statement of al-Hasan, may Allah be pleased with him, has no meaning, because hypocrisy is the manifestation of righteousness with inner corruption. [If] his inner self is righteous and his corruption appears, he would be the opposite of a hypocrite. And you are aware that if his intention is that the hypocrite is one who conceals disbelief and manifests faith, what was mentioned does not follow.
Source pages p. 368

Commentary
This passage addresses the claim that the grave sinner (fāsiq) is a hypocrite (munāfiq). Two proofs are mentioned, but only the first (transmitted) is given here. The transmitted proof is the famous hadith: the signs of a hypocrite are breaking promises, lying, and betraying trusts. The response is that this hadith is not taken literally because if taken universally, it would lead to absurdities. The commentator then adds several glosses: the earlier response (about criminals being specific) is recalled; another possible answer is that the denial in question is not actual but by affirmation; and sinners of believers might be included among the righteous, unless one insists that some sinners are driven to the Fire, which would make them disbelievers—but nobody holds that distinction, so a textual response is needed. Then an interpretation of the hadith is offered: it could refer to hypocrisy in actions (nifāq al-aʿmāl) not in religion (nifāq al-dīn). The commentator then critiques a statement of al-Hasan al-Baṣrī, arguing that if the hypocrite is defined as one who conceals disbelief and manifests faith, then a person whose inner is righteous and outer is sinful would be the opposite, so the hadith does not apply to the grave sinner. The passage is dense with layered commentary, but the main point is that the hadith about hypocrisy is not evidence that the grave sinner is a hypocrite in faith.

آية المنافق
النفاق
المنافق في الأعمال

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The hadith about the signs of a hypocrite applies to all who commit these sins, making the grave sinner a hypocrite in faith.
Correction: The hadith is not taken literally; it refers to hypocrisy in actions, not in religious belief. The grave sinner remains a believer, though sinful.

Checkpoint
How does the response interpret the hadith 'The sign of the hypocrite is three' when applied to the grave sinner?

B) It is abandoned in its apparent meaning and refers to hypocrisy in actions, not in religion.
C) It is abrogated by later verses.
D) It applies only to polytheists, not monotheists.

Key Takeaway
The hadith about the hypocrite's signs is interpreted as referring to hypocrisy in actions, not in faith, so the grave sinner remains a believer.

The Fourteenth Proof: The Fate of the Fasiq
Opponent's proof from al-Sajda 32:20 and its refutation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the Ash'ariyya refute the claim that Surah al-Sajda 32:20 proves grave sinners are disbelievers.

Core Idea
A verse's apparent generality may be limited by rational necessity.

The opponent uses the verse about the fasiq's punishment to argue that every fasiq is a kafir. But does the verse really say that? The Ash'ari response shows that taking it literally leads to an absurd conclusion, forcing us to understand it differently. Let's examine the proof and its refutation.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic passage for the fourteenth proof and its translation. Notice how the opponent first states the verse then draws a conclusion.
2
Identify the opponent's argument
The opponent claims the verse indicates that every fasiq is a kafir. Find the exact phrase that expresses this.
They argue from the threat of eternal fire.
3
Identify the Ash'ari response
The response denies that the verse remains on its apparent generality. What reason is given?
The reason involves a necessary consequence about denial of the Resurrection.
4
Reflect on the principle
Consider why the Ash'ariyya limit the verse's generality. How does this protect the possibility of a fasiq being a believer?
The principle is that apparent meanings yielding false rational implications are abandoned.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الرابع عشر : قوله تعالى : وأما الذين فسقوا فمأواهم النار ﴾ [السجدة : ٢٠] الآية وتمامها كلما أرادوا أن يخرجوا منها أعيدوا فيها وقيل لهم ذوقوا عذاب النار الذي كنتم به تكذبون ﴾ [السجدة : ٢٠]، فإنه يدل على أن كل فاسق كافر. قلنا : ليس قوله : وأما الذين فسقوا ﴾ [السجدة : ٢٠]، باقياً على عمومه الظاهر لأنه يقتضي أن كل فاسق مكذب بالقيامة وإنه باطل قطعاً. الخامس عشر قوله تعالى: يتساءلون عن المجرمين ما سلككم في سقر [المدثر : ٤٠-٤٢] إلى قوله : وكنا نكذب بيوم الدين ﴾ [المدثر : ٤٦] فثبت بذلك أن كل مجرم داخل في النار كافر ولا شبهة في أن الفاسق مجرم لا يدخل النار، قلنا : قد مر جوابه وهو أن الآية متروكة الظاهر وإلا لزم كون كل مجرم مكذباً بالقيامة وهو باطل قطعاً.
The fourteenth: the saying of the Most High: 'And as for those who deviated, their abode is the Fire' (al-Sajda 32:20) the verse, and its completion: 'Every time they wish to exit from it, they are returned to it and it is said to them: Taste the punishment of the Fire which you used to deny' (al-Sajda 32:20). For it indicates that every deviant (fasiq) is a disbeliever (kafir). We say: His saying 'And as for those who deviated' (al-Sajda 32:20) is not remaining upon its apparent generality, because it would require that every deviant is a denier of the Resurrection, and that is certainly false. The fifteenth: the saying of the Most High: 'They ask one another about the criminals: What drove you into Saqar?' (al-Muddaththir 74:40-42) up to His saying: 'And we used to deny the Day of Judgment' (al-Muddaththir 74:46). So it is established by that that every criminal (mujrim) is an inhabitant of the Fire, a disbeliever, and there is no doubt that the deviant is a criminal who enters the Fire. We say: its answer has already passed, which is that the verse is abandoned in its apparent meaning, otherwise it would necessitate that every criminal is a denier of the Resurrection, and that is certainly false.
Source pages p. 368

Commentary
This passage presents two Quranic proofs used by opponents to argue that all grave sinners are disbelievers, followed by the Ash'ari response. The first proof is from Surah al-Sajda (32:20), which describes the fate of 'those who deviated' (al-fasiqun) as the Fire, with repeated punishment. The opponent argues this proves every fasiq is a kafir. The answer is that the verse cannot be taken literally, because if it meant every fasiq is a denier of the Resurrection, that would be false (since some fasiqs believe in the Resurrection). The second proof is from Surah al-Muddaththir (74:40-46), where criminals are asked what led them to hell and they reply 'we denied the Day of Judgment'. The opponent argues this proves every criminal (including the fasiq) is a disbeliever. The answer is as before: the verse is not literal; otherwise it would imply every criminal denies the Resurrection, which is false. The author follows the order: first the proof, then the refutation, emphasizing that the apparent meaning cannot hold.

Key Terms
فاسق
كافر
عموم
ظاهر

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Believing that the Ash'ariyya reject the Quranic threats entirely.
Correction: They accept the threats but interpret them as not applying to every fasiq absolutely, based on rational necessity.

Checkpoint
Why does the Ash'ari response reject the literal interpretation of the verse about the fasiq in al-Sajda 32:20?
A) Because the verse is abrogated
B) Because it would imply that every fasiq denies the Resurrection, which is false
C) Because the verse refers only to polytheists
D) Because the verse is ambiguous

Key Takeaway
The apparent generality of a Quranic threat may be limited when it leads to a false rational conclusion.

The Fifteenth Proof: The Fate of the Mujrim
Opponent's proof from al-Muddaththir 74:40-46 and its refutation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the refutation of the claim that Surah al-Muddaththir 74:40-46 proves all criminals are disbelievers.

Core Idea
The same rational limitation applies to the verse about criminals.

The opponent also uses the verse about criminals (mujrimin) in hell, who confess to denying the Day of Judgment. They argue this proves that every criminal is a disbeliever, including the fasiq. The Ash'ari response again invokes the principle of limiting apparent generality to avoid absurd consequences.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic passage for the fifteenth proof and its translation. Notice the structure: proof followed by response.
2
Identify the opponent's argument
The opponent uses the criminals' admission that they denied the Day of Judgment to claim that every criminal is a kafir. Find where this conclusion is stated.
They say 'so it is established that every criminal is an inhabitant of the Fire, a disbeliever.'
3
Identify the Ash'ari response
The response refers back to the earlier answer. What is that answer?
It says 'its answer has already passed' – the verse is not literal.
4
Connect to the previous lesson
Notice how the same principle is applied: apparent generality leads to falsehood if taken literally.
The consequence would be that every criminal denies the resurrection, which is false.

So it is established by that that every criminal (mujrim) is an inhabitant of the Fire, a disbeliever, and there is no doubt that the deviant is a criminal who enters the Fire. We say: its answer has already passed, which is that the verse is abandoned in its apparent meaning, otherwise it would necessitate that every criminal is a denier of the Resurrection, and that is certainly false.

This passage presents two Quranic proofs used by opponents to argue that all grave sinners are disbelievers, followed by the Ash'ari response. The first proof is from Surah al-Sajda (32:20), which describes the fate of 'those who deviated' (al-fasiqun) as the Fire, with repeated punishment. The opponent argues this proves every fasiq is a kafir. The answer is that the verse cannot be taken literally, because if it meant every fasiq is a denier of the Resurrection, that would be false (since some fasiqs believe in the Resurrection). The second proof is from Surah al-Muddaththir (74:40-46), where criminals are asked what led them to hell and they reply 'we denied the Day of Judgment'. The opponent argues this proves every criminal (including the fasiq) is a disbeliever. The answer is as before: the verse is not literal; otherwise it would imply every criminal denies the Resurrection, which is false. The author follows the order: first the proof, then the refutation, emphasizing that the apparent meaning cannot hold.

Key Terms
مجرم
كافر
ظاهر

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Thinking that the Ash'ariyya inconsistency between the two verses.
Correction: They apply the same principle of limiting apparent generality to both verses.

Checkpoint
What is the basis of the Ash'ari refutation of the fifteenth proof?
A) The verse is ambiguous
B) The verse is abrogated
C) The apparent meaning is abandoned because it would imply every criminal denies the Resurrection
D) The verse refers only to polytheists

Key Takeaway
The same principle of limiting generality applies to threats against criminals as to threats against deviants.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you read a Quranic verse that describes punishment for a certain type of person, do you automatically assume it applies to everyone who commits that sin? How does the Ash'ari principle of limiting the apparent meaning challenge your tendency to make sweeping judgments about others?
?
Think of a Muslim you know who commits a major sin but still believes in the Day of Judgment. Does this lesson help you see how a general threat in the Quran might not apply to them in the same way? What does that mean for how you view them?
?
The opponents in this lesson used a verse to condemn all criminals to the Fire. In your own life, are there people you have written off as hopeless because of their sins? How can the Ash'ari refusal to take the apparent meaning at face value free you to have hope for them while still respecting the Quran?

The Sixteenth Proof: Quranic Division of People
Quranic proof and its refutation

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the Ash'ari school refutes the claim that the Quranic division of people into muttaqi and kafir excludes an intermediate state.

Core Idea
Mentioning two categories does not prove a third does not exist.

The sixteenth proof attempts to use Surah al-Zumar's description of two groups—the muttaqun driven to Paradise and the kafirun driven to Hell—to argue that no third category exists. But this inference is not logically necessary. The Ash'ari response points out that enumerating two groups does not rule out a third, especially when the verse's context may not be exhaustive.

Guided Path
1
Read the proof and its answer
Read the Arabic text and translation for the sixteenth proof. Notice the opponent's argument from the verse and the Ash'ari reply.
The reply is simple: mention of two does not negate a third.
2
Understand the logical fallacy
Reflect on why the opponent's inference is invalid. The Quran often divides people in specific contexts without claiming exhaustive categories.
This is a common tactic in kalam: attacking an argument by questioning its logical completeness.
3
Apply the principle
Consider how this principle (mentioning two does not exclude a third) applies to other theological debates you might encounter.
It's a versatile rebuttal against false dichotomies.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
السادس عشر قوله تعالى : وسيق الذين كفروا ﴾ [الزمر : ٧١] إلى قوله وسيق الذين اتقوا [الزمر : ٧٣] إذ يعلم منه أن الإنسان إما متق يساق إلى الجنة أو كافر يساق إلى النار. الجواب عنه : قد مر مثله، وهو أن ذكر قسمين لا يدل على عدم قسم ثالث. السابع عشر : قوله عليه السلام من ترك صلاة متعمداً فقد كفر. وقوله عليه السلام : من مات ولم يحج فليمت إن شاء يهودياً وإن شاء نصرانياً. قلنا : الآحاد لا تعارض الإجماع المنعقد قبل حدوث المخالفين. والثامن عشر ولاية الله وعداوته ضدان فلا واسطة بينهما وولاية الله إيمان وعداوته كفر. قلنا : لا نسلم عدم بين كل ضدين، فإن السواد والبياض متضادان وبينهما واسطة، فجاز أن يكون بين ولايته وعداوته واسطة.
The sixteenth: the saying of the Most High: 'And those who disbelieved are driven' (al-Zumar 39:71) up to His saying 'And those who feared are driven' (al-Zumar 39:73). For it is known from it that man is either one who fears (muttaqi) driven to Paradise or a disbeliever (kafir) driven to the Fire. The answer to it: the like of it has already passed, which is that mentioning two categories does not indicate the non-existence of a third category. The seventeenth: his saying, peace be upon him: 'Whoever leaves prayer deliberately has disbelieved.' And his saying, peace be upon him: 'Whoever dies without performing Hajj, let him die if he wishes as a Jew or if he wishes as a Christian.' We say: solitary reports (ahad) do not oppose the consensus (ijma') that was established before the appearance of dissenters. And the eighteenth: the friendship of Allah and His enmity are two opposites, so there is no intermediate between them; the friendship of Allah is faith (iman) and His enmity is disbelief (kufr). We say: we do not accept that there is no intermediate between every two opposites. For black and white are opposites yet there is an intermediate between them. So it is possible that there is an intermediate between His friendship and His enmity.
Source pages p. 368

Commentary
This passage presents three more proofs and their refutations. The sixteenth proof is from Surah al-Zumar (39:71-73), which divides people into two groups: the muttaqun driven to Paradise and the kafirun driven to the Fire. The opponent argues this division is exhaustive, leaving no room for a grave sinner who is neither fully muttaqi nor kafir. The answer is that mentioning two categories does not prove the absence of a third; the verse is not necessarily exhaustive. The seventeenth proof cites two hadiths: 'Whoever leaves prayer deliberately has disbelieved' and 'Whoever dies without Hajj, let him die as a Jew or Christian.' The answer is that these are solitary reports (ahad) and cannot override the consensus (ijma') of the community on the status of grave sinners, which was established before dissenters arose. The eighteenth proof is logical: Allah's friendship (wilaya) and enmity ('adawwa) are opposites, with no intermediate; since faith is friendship and disbelief is enmity, there is no intermediate state. The answer is that not every pair of opposites lacks an intermediate; for example, black and white are opposites yet there are intermediate colors (like gray). Similarly, there can be an intermediate between faith and disbelief, i.e., the grave sinner. The author proceeds in order: each proof followed by its answer, with a clear logical response.

Key Terms
متق
كافر

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The Quranic verses 39:71-73 prove that all people are either completely faithful or completely disbelieving, with no intermediate state for grave sinners.
Correction: The verses describe the fate of two groups at the end, but they do not assert that these are the only possible categories. The Ash'ari response clarifies that enumeration does not equal exhaustiveness.

Checkpoint

B) Mentioning two categories does not prove the absence of a third.
C) The grave sinner is actually considered a kafir.
D) The verses have been abrogated.

Key Takeaway
A list of two categories does not prove there is no third category; the Quranic verses may not be exhaustive.

The Seventeenth Proof: Hadiths on Prayer and Hajj
Hadith-based proof and its refutation

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the Ash'ari school rejects the evidential force of certain hadiths that seem to declare grave sinners as disbelievers, based on the principle that solitary reports cannot override established consensus.

Core Idea
Solitary hadith cannot overturn the consensus of the community.

The opponent brings two prophetic hadiths: one equating deliberate abandonment of prayer with disbelief, and another that harshly condemns one who dies without Hajj. These seem to support the view that grave sinners are disbelievers. The Ash'ari response appeals to the hierarchy of evidence: these are ahad (solitary) reports, and they cannot contradict the ijma (consensus) of the companions and early scholars on the status of grave sinners as believers with shortcomings.

Guided Path
1
Read the two hadiths and the response
Read the Arabic and translation carefully. Note the strong language of each hadith and the concise Ash'ari reply.
The reply relies on a technical distinction between types of hadith.
2
Understand the concept of ahad vs. mutawatir
Learn that ahad hadith are those not transmitted by a large number at each generation, and they yield probabilistic knowledge. Mutawatir hadith yield certainty.
Consensus (ijma) is a source of certainty in Sunni theology.
3
Apply the principle
Consider why the Ash'aris prioritize consensus over ahad reports in matters of fundamental belief.
This hierarchy protects core doctrines from being overturned by weak or misinterpreted evidence.

The Eighteenth Proof: Wilaya and 'Adawwa as Opposites
Logical proof from opposition and its refutation

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the argument from friendship and enmity being opposites does not force an exclusive dichotomy between faith and disbelief, using the example of black and white.

Core Idea
Not all opposites lack an intermediate—black and white have gray.

The eighteenth proof is a logical one: Allah's friendship (wilaya) and enmity ('adawwa) are opposites, so there cannot be an intermediate state. Since faith is friendship and disbelief is enmity, grave sinners cannot be in between. The Ash'ari response counters by observing that not every pair of opposites lacks an intermediate; for instance, black and white are opposites yet there exist intermediate colors like gray. Similarly, there can be an intermediate between faith and disbelief, namely the grave sinner who is neither a complete believer nor a disbeliever.

Guided Path
1
Read the proof and its refutation
Read the Arabic and translation for the eighteenth proof. Note the opponent's logical claim and the Ash'ari counterexample.
The counterexample from colors is both intuitive and powerful.
2
Analyze the concept of opposition (tadadd)
Understand that in logic, opposites can be binary (like life/death) or have intermediates (like hot/cold with warm). The Ash'ari claim is that wilaya and 'adawwa are like the latter.
This is a crucial distinction in kalam logic.
3
Apply the concept to the issue of grave sinners
Reflect on how the existence of an intermediate between faith and disbelief allows for the category of the grave sinner (fasiq) as a believer with major sin.
This defence preserves the unity of the Muslim community.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

Transmitted Argument for Hypocrisy and Its Refutation
First argument (naqli) and its refutation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the hadith about the signs of a hypocrite does not prove that a grave sinner is a hypocrite.

Core Idea
Signs of hypocrisy require a fixed disposition, not occasional lapses.

The opponent uses a famous hadith listing three signs of hypocrisy to argue that any grave sinner is a hypocrite. The Ash'ari response, however, distinguishes between occasional lapses and a fixed disposition, and also notes that a sign may not be definitive. This lesson unpacks that response.

Guided Path
1
Read the transmitted argument and refutation
Start by reading the opponent's transmitted argument and the Ash'ari reply. Pay attention to how the response challenges the literal interpretation.
The example of Joseph's brothers is used to show that even major sins don't make one a hypocrite.
2
Understand the concept of 'disposition' (malaka)
The response clarifies that the hadith's signs only indicate hypocrisy when they become a fixed disposition. Temporary sins do not suffice.
This is a key distinction.
3
Grasp that signs are not always definitive
The response also points out that a sign may not be conclusively indicative; the signified may fail to occur.
This logical point strengthens the refutation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
احتج من زعم أنه أي مرتكب الكبيرة منافق بوجهين: الأول: نقلي وهو قوله عليه الصلاة والسلام: آية المنافق ثلاث إذا وعد أخلف وإذا حدث كذب وإذا ائتمن خان. قلنا: هو متروك الظاهر لأن من وعد غيره أن يخلع عليه خلعة نفيسة ثم أخلفه لم يخرج بذلك عن الإيمان إلى النفاق إجماعاً، وقيل: معناه أن هذه الخصال الثلاث إذا صارت معاً ملكة الشخص كانت علامة لنفاقه، وأما بدون كونها ملكة فلا، ألا ترى أن إخوة يوسف وعدوا أباهم أن يحفظوا فأخلفوا وائتمنهم أبوهم فخانوا وكذبوا في قولهم فأكله الذئب وما كانوا منافقين اتفاقاً، على أن العلامة الدالة على شيء قد لا تكون قطعية الدلالة فيجوز تخلف المدلول عنها. الثاني: عقلي وهو أن من اعتقد من العقلاء أن في هذا الجحر حية لم يدخل يده فيه فإذا زعم ذلك ثم أدخل يده فيه علم أنه قاله لا عن اعتقاد، وكذا الحال فيمن ارتكب الكبيرة. قلنا: مضرة الحية عاجلة محققة بخلاف عقاب الذنب لأنها آجلة وغير محققة، إذ يجوز التوبة والعفو فافترقا.
One who claimed that he—i.e., the perpetrator of a major sin—is a hypocrite (munafiq) argued with two aspects. The first: a transmitted (naqli) one, which is his saying, peace and prayer be upon him: 'The sign of the hypocrite is three: when he promises he breaks his promise, when he speaks he lies, and when he is entrusted he betrays.' We say: It is abandoned in its apparent meaning, because if someone promises another a precious robe and then breaks his promise, he does not thereby exit from faith into hypocrisy by consensus. And it is said: its meaning is that these three characteristics, when they together become a disposition (malaka) of the person, are a sign of his hypocrisy; but without being a disposition, no. Do you not see that the brothers of Joseph promised their father to protect him, then broke their promise, and their father entrusted them and they betrayed, and they lied in their saying 'the wolf ate him,' yet they were not hypocrites by agreement. Moreover, a sign indicating something may not be definitively indicative, so the signified may fail to occur. The second: a rational one, which is that if a rational person believes that there is a snake in this hole, he will not put his hand into it; if he claims that yet then puts his hand in, it is known that he said it without belief. The same applies to the one who commits a major sin. We say: the harm of the snake is immediate and certain, unlike the punishment of sin, because it is deferred and not certain, since repentance and pardon are possible. So they differ.
Source pages p. 368

Commentary
This passage addresses an opponent who claims that the grave sinner is a hypocrite (munafiq), using two arguments. The first is a transmitted argument: the hadith listing three signs of a hypocrite: breaking promises, lying, and betraying trust. The response is that the hadith is not taken literally. First, if someone breaks a promise about a robe, he does not become a hypocrite by consensus. Second, the signs only indicate hypocrisy when they become a fixed disposition; temporary lapses do not. The example of Joseph's brothers is given: they committed all three signs but were not hypocrites. Also, a sign may not be conclusively indicative. The second argument is rational: if someone truly believes there is a snake in a hole, he will not put his hand in; similarly, if a grave sinner truly believed in punishment, he would not sin. The response: the analogy fails because the snake's harm is immediate and certain, while the punishment of sin is deferred and not certain (due to possible repentance and forgiveness). The author presents each argument and its refutation in order, highlighting the differences that invalidate the analogy.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Anyone who lies or breaks a promise is a hypocrite.
Correction: The hadith only applies when these traits become a fixed disposition, not to occasional lapses.

D) Never

Key Takeaway
The hadith about the signs of a hypocrite only applies when those traits become a fixed disposition, not to occasional lapses.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Which of the three signs of hypocrisy—breaking promises, lying, or betraying trust—have you committed this week? Was it an isolated incident, or is it becoming a habit? What specific step will you take today to ensure it does not become your nature?
?
Joseph’s brothers broke promises, lied, and betrayed trust, yet they were not hypocrites because it was not their fixed disposition. But if you continue in such behaviors without repentance, what does that say about your character? Are you willing to examine yourself honestly and change before these traits become your malaka?
?
If someone judged you as a hypocrite based on a single sin, you would feel wronged. Do you grant the same mercy to others? Will you now commit to judging people by their overall character rather than isolated mistakes, and to seeking forgiveness for your own lapses before they solidify?

Rational Argument for Hypocrisy and Its Refutation
Second argument (aqli) and its refutation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the rational analogy of the snake in the hole fails to prove that a grave sinner is a hypocrite.

Core Idea
The snake's harm is immediate and certain; divine punishment is deferred and uncertain.

The opponent uses a rational analogy: if someone truly believes there is a snake in a hole, they won't put their hand in; similarly, if a grave sinner truly believed in punishment, they wouldn't sin. The Ash'ari response highlights a crucial difference: the snake's harm is immediate and certain, while punishment is delayed and uncertain due to possible repentance and forgiveness.

Guided Path
1
Read the rational argument and its refutation
Read the opponent's analogy and the response. Notice the key point of difference.
2
Compare immediate vs. deferred harm
The response argues that the snake's harm is immediate and certain, while the punishment of sin is deferred and not certain because of potential repentance and forgiveness.
3
Understand why the analogy fails
Because of this difference, the analogy does not hold. The grave sinner may still have true faith but be overcome by desire or doubt about the certainty of punishment.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
احتج من زعم أنه أي مرتكب الكبيرة منافق بوجهين: الأول: نقلي وهو قوله عليه الصلاة والسلام: آية المنافق ثلاث إذا وعد أخلف وإذا حدث كذب وإذا ائتمن خان. قلنا: هو متروك الظاهر لأن من وعد غيره أن يخلع عليه خلعة نفيسة ثم أخلفه لم يخرج بذلك عن الإيمان إلى النفاق إجماعاً، وقيل: معناه أن هذه الخصال الثلاث إذا صارت معاً ملكة الشخص كانت علامة لنفاقه، وأما بدون كونها ملكة فلا، ألا ترى أن إخوة يوسف وعدوا أباهم أن يحفظوا فأخلفوا وائتمنهم أبوهم فخانوا وكذبوا في قولهم فأكله الذئب وما كانوا منافقين اتفاقاً، على أن العلامة الدالة على شيء قد لا تكون قطعية الدلالة فيجوز تخلف المدلول عنها. الثاني: عقلي وهو أن من اعتقد من العقلاء أن في هذا الجحر حية لم يدخل يده فيه فإذا زعم ذلك ثم أدخل يده فيه علم أنه قاله لا عن اعتقاد، وكذا الحال فيمن ارتكب الكبيرة. قلنا: مضرة الحية عاجلة محققة بخلاف عقاب الذنب لأنها آجلة وغير محققة، إذ يجوز التوبة والعفو فافترقا.
One who claimed that he—i.e., the perpetrator of a major sin—is a hypocrite (munafiq) argued with two aspects. The first: a transmitted (naqli) one, which is his saying, peace and prayer be upon him: 'The sign of the hypocrite is three: when he promises he breaks his promise, when he speaks he lies, and when he is entrusted he betrays.' We say: It is abandoned in its apparent meaning, because if someone promises another a precious robe and then breaks his promise, he does not thereby exit from faith into hypocrisy by consensus. And it is said: its meaning is that these three characteristics, when they together become a disposition (malaka) of the person, are a sign of his hypocrisy; but without being a disposition, no. Do you not see that the brothers of Joseph promised their father to protect him, then broke their promise, and their father entrusted them and they betrayed, and they lied in their saying 'the wolf ate him,' yet they were not hypocrites by agreement. Moreover, a sign indicating something may not be definitively indicative, so the signified may fail to occur. The second: a rational one, which is that if a rational person believes that there is a snake in this hole, he will not put his hand into it; if he claims that yet then puts his hand in, it is known that he said it without belief. The same applies to the one who commits a major sin. We say: the harm of the snake is immediate and certain, unlike the punishment of sin, because it is deferred and not certain, since repentance and pardon are possible. So they differ.
Source pages p. 368

Commentary
This passage addresses an opponent who claims that the grave sinner is a hypocrite (munafiq), using two arguments. The first is a transmitted argument: the hadith listing three signs of a hypocrite: breaking promises, lying, and betraying trust. The response is that the hadith is not taken literally. First, if someone breaks a promise about a robe, he does not become a hypocrite by consensus. Second, the signs only indicate hypocrisy when they become a fixed disposition; temporary lapses do not. The example of Joseph's brothers is given: they committed all three signs but were not hypocrites. Also, a sign may not be conclusively indicative. The second argument is rational: if someone truly believes there is a snake in a hole, he will not put his hand in; similarly, if a grave sinner truly believed in punishment, he would not sin. The response: the analogy fails because the snake's harm is immediate and certain, while the punishment of sin is deferred and not certain (due to possible repentance and forgiveness). The author presents each argument and its refutation in order, highlighting the differences that invalidate the analogy.

Key Terms
عقلي
حية
عاجلة
آجلة
توبة
عفو

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: If someone sins, they must not truly believe in punishment.
Correction: The punishment is not immediate or certain, so the analogy fails; the sinner may still have faith.

Checkpoint
Why does the Ash'ari response reject the rational analogy of the snake?
A) Because snakes are not always harmful
B) Because the snake's harm is immediate and certain while punishment is deferred and uncertain
C) Because the person might be irrational
D) Because the hadith is stronger

Key Takeaway
The rational analogy fails because the harm of the snake is immediate and certain, while divine punishment is deferred and uncertain.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you sin, do you truly believe in the punishment of Allah? The snake's harm stops your hand instantly, but divine punishment feels distant. Will you allow the delay to make you careless, or will you treat every sin as if its consequence is already upon you?
?
The passage says punishment is uncertain because repentance and pardon are possible. Does this comfort you or make you complacent? Examine your heart: do you sin thinking 'I will repent later', or does the hope of forgiveness drive you to immediate repentance?
?
If you were certain that a sin would cause you immediate worldly harm, you would avoid it. Since the harm is deferred, you might not. What does that reveal about your faith? Will you train yourself to see the spiritual harm of sin as equally real and immediate as a snake's strike?

First Mu'tazilite Argument on Faith and Consensus
Mu'tazilite argument that the fasiq is neither mu'min nor kafir, with Ash'ari reply.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Mu'tazilite argument that the fasiq is neither believer nor disbeliever and the Ash'ari response based on a different definition of faith.

Core Idea
The status of the grave sinner hinges on how we define faith.

The Mu'tazilites challenged the Ash'ari view that a Muslim who commits grave sins remains a believer. They argued that the treatment of such a person by the early community shows he is neither fully a believer nor a disbeliever. This lesson examines their reasoning and the Ash'ari counterpoint.

Guided Path
1
Read the Mu'tazilite argument carefully
Read the Arabic passage once, then the translation. Notice the two distinct aspects they present.
The argument is based on consensus and practical rulings.
2
Identify the two sub-arguments
The first sub-argument uses the treatment of the fasiq by the Salaf; the second uses a reductio argument about marital separation.
Both aim to show the fasiq is neither mu'min nor kafir.
3
Understand the consensus-based reasoning
The Mu'tazilites claim that since the Salaf applied hadd penalties but did not treat the fasiq as a disbeliever, the fasiq cannot be kafir. Yet he is not a believer because faith is obedience.
This relies on their definition of faith as acts of obedience.
4
Examine the reductio argument
If the fasiq were a kafir, then a mere accusation of adultery would automatically separate spouses, which is absurd. This supports their intermediate position.
The argument assumes that both the truthful accuser and the guilty party would be kafir.
5
Note the Ash'ari response
The Ash'ari response is brief: the fasiq is a believer, referring back to the earlier discussion on the reality of faith (tasdiq, not actions).
This shows the disagreement is rooted in different definitions of faith.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
احتج المعتزلة بوجهين: الأول: إن الفاسق ليس مؤمناً لما مر من أن الإيمان عبارة عن الطاعات، ولا كافراً بالإجماع لأنهم أي الصحابة ومن بعدهم من السلف كانوا يقيمون عليه الحد في الزنا وشرب الخمر وقذف المحصنات ولا يقتلونه ولا يحكمون بردته ويدفنونه في مقابر المسلمين، مع إجماعهم على أن الكافر لا يعامل كذلك، وأيضاً يلزم من كون الفاسق كافراً بينونة المرأة عن زوجها بمجرد رمي الزوج إياها بالزنا من غير لعان وقضاء قاض، لأنه إن صدق الزوج فهي كافرة بارتكاب الزنا، وإن كذب فهو كافر بارتكاب قذف المحصنة فكانت البينونة واقعة على التقديرين. قلنا: هو مؤمن وقد مر الكلام فيه في بيان حقيقة الإيمان.
The Mu'tazilites argued with two aspects. The first: The fasiq is not a believer (mu'min) because—as has passed—faith is expression of acts of obedience; and he is not a disbeliever by consensus, for they—i.e., the Companions and those after them from the Salaf—used to enforce the prescribed punishment (hadd) upon him for adultery, drinking wine, and false accusation of chaste women; they did not kill him, nor did they judge him to be an apostate, and they buried him in the Muslim cemeteries, despite their consensus that the disbeliever is not treated likewise. Also, if the fasiq were a disbeliever, then the separation of the wife from her husband would necessarily follow simply from the husband accusing her of adultery without mutual imprecation (li'an) and a judge's ruling, because if the husband is truthful, she is a disbeliever for committing adultery; if he is lying, he is a disbeliever for falsely accusing a chaste woman. Thus separation would occur in both cases. We say: He (i.e., the fasiq) is a believer, and discussion about it has already passed in the explanation of the reality of faith.
Source pages p. 369

Commentary
This passage presents the first Mu'tazilite argument against the Ash'ari position that the grave sinner is a believer. The Mu'tazilites argue that the fasiq is not a believer because faith is acts of obedience (which the fasiq lacks), and not a disbeliever because the Salaf applied hadd penalties to fasiqs but not death or apostasy rulings, and buried them in Muslim cemeteries—unlike disbelievers. They also raise a reductio: if the fasiq were a disbeliever, then simple accusation of adultery would cause automatic separation of spouses without proper legal process, which is absurd. The Ash'ari response is brief: the fasiq is a believer, referring to previous discussion on the reality of faith (that faith is primarily tasdiq, not obedience). The text follows a clear objection-and-answer format.

Key Terms
المعتزلة
فاسق
مؤمن
كافر
حد
بينونة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that the Mu'tazilite consensus argument is decisive and proves the fasiq is neither believer nor disbeliever.
Correction: The Ash'ari response shows that the conclusion depends on the definition of faith; with faith as tasdiq, the fasiq remains a believer.

Checkpoint
What is the Mu'tazilites' main point about the treatment of the fasiq by the Salaf?
A) They killed him as an apostate
B) They applied hadd penalties but did not treat him as a disbeliever
C) They buried him in separate cemeteries
D) They excommunicated him

Key Takeaway
The Mu'tazilites used practical consensus to argue the fasiq is in an intermediate position, but the Ash'aris maintain that faith is primarily internal affirmation.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you sin, do you ever feel that your faith is completely lost? How does the Ash'ari position that the fasiq remains a believer challenge that feeling, and what does that mean for your relationship with Allah when you fall into disobedience?
?
The Mu'tazilites used the consensus of the Salaf to argue that the fasiq is not a disbeliever. How often do you rely on what 'everyone does' to justify your own behavior? Is your conscience shaped more by community norms or by revealed truth? What would it take for you to align your actions with what you truly believe?
?
Consider the reductio argument about marital separation if the fasiq were a disbeliever. How does this logical consequence reveal the implications of theological beliefs on daily life? Do you ever consider the practical effects of your beliefs about sin and faith on your interactions with others? What change might God be calling you to make in how you view fellow Muslims who struggle with sin?

Wasil's Argument and the Ash'ari Refutation
Wasil's argument and the Ash'ari refutation

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Wasil ibn 'Ata's argument for the 'position between two positions' and the Ash'ari response based on prior consensus.

Core Idea
The Mu'tazilites appealed to agreement, but the Ash'aris appealed to a deeper consensus.

Wasil ibn 'Ata, the founder of the Mu'tazila, proposed a simple argument: since everyone agrees the grave sinner is a fasiq, we should adopt that term and stop arguing about belief or disbelief. This lesson presents that argument and the Ash'ari refutation, which invokes an earlier consensus that every person is either a believer or a disbeliever.

Guided Path
1
Read Wasil's argument
Read the first part of the passage where Wasil presents his reasoning: take the agreed-upon description (fasiq) and leave the disputed one (mu'min/kafir).
2
Identify the Ash'ari response
The Ash'ari response states that there is a prior consensus that every mukallaf is either mu'min or kafir, so the intermediate violates that consensus.
3
Reflect on the logic
Consider which consensus is more binding: the one Wasil appeals to (agreement on fisq) or the one the Ash'aris appeal to (the binary division).
This shows a key methodological principle in kalam: earlier consensus overrides later partial agreement.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الثاني: ما قاله واصل بن لعمرو بن عبيد فرجع عمرو إلى مذهبه وهو أن فسقه معلوم وفاقاً، وإيمانه مختلف فيه أي الأمة مجمعة على أن صاحب الكبيرة فاسق، واختلفوا في كونه مؤمناً أو كافراً، فنترك المختلف فيه ونأخذ بالمتفق عليه. قلنا: قد أنه قطعاً ولا خلاف فيه ممن قبله من الأمة، بل قد أجمع قبله على أنه أي المكلف إما مؤمن أو كافر، فالقول بالواسطة خرق بالإجماع المنعقد على الانحصار في ذينك القسمين، فيكون باطلاً بلا اشتباه. قوله: (احتج المعتزلة) قال في شرح المقاصد: وفي كلام المتأخرين من المعتزلة ما يرفع النزاع وذلك أنهم لا ينكرون وصف الفاسق بالإيمان بمعنى التصديق أو بمعنى إجراء الأحكام، بل بمعنى استحقاق المدح وغاية التعظيم وهو الذي نسميه الإيمان الكامل ونعتبر فيه الأعمال وتنفيه عن الفاسق فيكون لهم منزلة بين منزلة النوع من الإيمان وبين منزلة الكفر باتفاق، وكأنه رجوع عن المذهب وإعراض كما يقال في نفي الصفات: إنا نريد ما هو من قبيل الأعراض وإلا فقد ماؤهم يصرحون بأن من أخل بالطاعة ليس بمؤمن بحسب الشرع، بل بمجرد اللغة وبأن القول بتعدد التقديم كفر من غير فرق بين العرض وغيره.
The second: what Wasil ibn 'Amr ibn 'Ubayd said—for 'Amr returned to his school—which is that his fisq is known by agreement, and his iman is disputed; i.e., the community is agreed that the possessor of a major sin is a fasiq, and they disagreed about whether he is a believer or a disbeliever. So we leave what is disputed and take what is agreed upon. We say: It is [known] definitively and there is no disagreement among those before him from the community; rather, consensus existed before him that the morally responsible person is either a believer or a disbeliever. Thus, asserting an intermediate violates the consensus established upon restriction to these two categories, so it is false without doubt. His saying: (The Mu'tazilites argued) said in Sharh al-Maqasid: In the words of the later Mu'tazilites there is what removes the dispute, in that they do not deny describing the fasiq as having iman in the sense of tasdiq (affirmation) or in the sense of applying legal rulings, but rather in the sense of deserving praise and ultimate veneration, which we call complete iman and in which we consider deeds, and they negate it from the fasiq. Thus they have a station between the station of the type of iman and the station of kufr by agreement. It is as if it is a return from the school and a turning away, as is said in the negation of attributes: we mean what is of the category of accidents; otherwise, their statement explicitly says that whoever neglects obedience is not a believer according to the Law, but only according to language, and that the statement of multiple levels of iman is disbelief without distinction between accident and other.
Source pages p. 369

Commentary
This passage presents the second Mu'tazilite argument, attributed to Wasil ibn 'Ata (founder of the Mu'tazilite school). His argument is: we all agree the grave sinner is a fasiq, but disagree whether he is a believer or disbeliever; so we should accept the common ground (fasiq) and set aside the disputed matter. The Ash'ari response is that there is a prior consensus that every mukallaf is either a believer or a disbeliever (with no third category), so adopting an intermediate position breaks that consensus. Then, a marginal note from Sharh al-Maqasid explains that later Mu'tazilites actually reduce the conflict: they do not deny the fasiq has iman in the sense of tasdiq or legal treatment, but only deny 'complete iman' which requires deeds. This effectively gives the fasiq a 'position between the two positions' (manzila bayn al-manzilatayn), but the sharh suggests this may be a retreat from the original Mu'tazilite doctrine. The passage moves from the argument, to the refutation, to a deeper commentary showing how the dispute may be terminological.

Key Terms
واصل بن عطا
المنزلة بين المنزلتين
فاسق
مؤمن
كافر
إجماع

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Ash'ari response, why is Wasil's argument invalid?
A) Because the definition of fasiq is disputed
B) Because there is a prior consensus that every mukallaf is either a believer or a disbeliever
C) Because Wasil's position was rejected by his own teacher
D) Because the Mu'tazilites later changed their view

Key Takeaway
Wasil's argument fails because it ignores the prior consensus that every person is either a believer or a disbeliever, leaving no room for a third category.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You have just read that Wasil ibn 'Ata tried to use agreement to settle a disagreement, but his argument was refuted because it ignored a deeper consensus. In your own life, have you ever confidently accepted a 'common ground' without checking if it conflicts with a more fundamental truth? What was that situation, and how might this lesson change how you approach such dilemmas?
?
The Ash'ari response appeals to the authority of an earlier consensus that every person is either a believer or a disbeliever. Does this binary sit comfortably with you? Are there areas of your own faith where you are tempted to create a third category to avoid the sharpness of divine judgment? What would it mean for you today to accept that there is no intermediate position with God?
?
When faced with a theological dispute, Wasil's method seems reasonable: set aside what is disputed and hold to what is agreed. But the lesson shows that even reasonable methods can be wrong if they miss a prior truth. Think of a belief you hold that you consider 'agreed upon' among your peers. Could it be that you are ignoring a more foundational principle taught by the early generations? Will you examine it today?
