The Later Mu'tazilite Position: A Terminological Reconciliation
Later Mu'tazilite redefinition of iman from Sharh al-Maqasid

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how later Mu'tazilites redefined terms to reconcile their position with mainstream Islam, effectively returning to a moderate stance.

Core Idea
Later Mu'tazilites affirmed iman as tasdiq, only denying 'complete iman' — reducing the dispute to terminology.

This lesson explores a note from Sharh al-Maqasid that explains how later Mu'tazilites clarified their position. They do not deny that the fasiq has iman in the sense of affirmation (tasdiq) or in the sense of legal treatment; they only deny the fasiq possesses 'complete iman' that includes deeds and deserves the highest praise. This effectively gives the fasiq a 'position between two stations' (manzila bayn al-manzilatayn) between the type of iman and kufr, which the commentator suggests may be a return from the original school.

Guided Path
1
Read the later Mu'tazilite clarification
Read the passage from Sharh al-Maqasid that describes the later Mu'tazilite position. Note how they distinguish between different senses of iman.
2
Identify what they affirm and deny
They affirm iman as tasdiq and legal rulings, but deny 'complete iman' which requires deeds. This is key to understanding the reconciliation.
3
Consider the commentator's assessment
The commentator says this is 'as if it is a return from the school and a turning away'. Reflect on whether this is a genuine retreat or just a clarification.
4
Connect to the earlier argument
Notice how this later position effectively gives the fasiq a 'station between two stations' (manzila bayn manzilatayn) between the type of iman and kufr by agreement, which resolves the earlier dispute.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الثاني: ما قاله واصل بن لعمرو بن عبيد فرجع عمرو إلى مذهبه وهو أن فسقه معلوم وفاقاً، وإيمانه مختلف فيه أي الأمة مجمعة على أن صاحب الكبيرة فاسق، واختلفوا في كونه مؤمناً أو كافراً، فنترك المختلف فيه ونأخذ بالمتفق عليه. قلنا: قد أنه قطعاً ولا خلاف فيه ممن قبله من الأمة، بل قد أجمع قبله على أنه أي المكلف إما مؤمن أو كافر، فالقول بالواسطة خرق بالإجماع المنعقد على الانحصار في ذينك القسمين، فيكون باطلاً بلا اشتباه. قوله: (احتج المعتزلة) قال في شرح المقاصد: وفي كلام المتأخرين من المعتزلة ما يرفع النزاع وذلك أنهم لا ينكرون وصف الفاسق بالإيمان بمعنى التصديق أو بمعنى إجراء الأحكام، بل بمعنى استحقاق المدح وغاية التعظيم وهو الذي نسميه الإيمان الكامل ونعتبر فيه الأعمال وتنفيه عن الفاسق فيكون لهم منزلة بين منزلة النوع من الإيمان وبين منزلة الكفر باتفاق، وكأنه رجوع عن المذهب وإعراض كما يقال في نفي الصفات: إنا نريد ما هو من قبيل الأعراض وإلا فقد ماؤهم يصرحون بأن من أخل بالطاعة ليس بمؤمن بحسب الشرع، بل بمجرد اللغة وبأن القول بتعدد التقديم كفر من غير فرق بين العرض وغيره.
The second: what Wasil ibn 'Amr ibn 'Ubayd said—for 'Amr returned to his school—which is that his fisq is known by agreement, and his iman is disputed; i.e., the community is agreed that the possessor of a major sin is a fasiq, and they disagreed about whether he is a believer or a disbeliever. So we leave what is disputed and take what is agreed upon. We say: It is [known] definitively and there is no disagreement among those before him from the community; rather, consensus existed before him that the morally responsible person is either a believer or a disbeliever. Thus, asserting an intermediate violates the consensus established upon restriction to these two categories, so it is false without doubt. His saying: (The Mu'tazilites argued) said in Sharh al-Maqasid: In the words of the later Mu'tazilites there is what removes the dispute, in that they do not deny describing the fasiq as having iman in the sense of tasdiq (affirmation) or in the sense of applying legal rulings, but rather in the sense of deserving praise and ultimate veneration, which we call complete iman and in which we consider deeds, and they negate it from the fasiq. Thus they have a station between the station of the type of iman and the station of kufr by agreement. It is as if it is a return from the school and a turning away, as is said in the negation of attributes: we mean what is of the category of accidents; otherwise, their statement explicitly says that whoever neglects obedience is not a believer according to the Law, but only according to language, and that the statement of multiple levels of iman is disbelief without distinction between accident and other.
Source pages p. 369

Commentary
This passage presents the second Mu'tazilite argument, attributed to Wasil ibn 'Ata (founder of the Mu'tazilite school). His argument is: we all agree the grave sinner is a fasiq, but disagree whether he is a believer or disbeliever; so we should accept the common ground (fasiq) and set aside the disputed matter. The Ash'ari response is that there is a prior consensus that every mukallaf is either a believer or a disbeliever (with no third category), so adopting an intermediate position breaks that consensus. Then, a marginal note from Sharh al-Maqasid explains that later Mu'tazilites actually reduce the conflict: they do not deny the fasiq has iman in the sense of tasdiq or legal treatment, but only deny 'complete iman' which requires deeds. This effectively gives the fasiq a 'position between the two positions' (manzila bayn al-manzilatayn), but the sharh suggests this may be a retreat from the original Mu'tazilite doctrine. The passage moves from the argument, to the refutation, to a deeper commentary showing how the dispute may be terminological.

Key Terms
شرح المقاصد
المنزلة بين المنزلتين
تصديق
إيمان كامل
أحكام

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think the Mu'tazilite position is that the grave sinner is neither a believer nor a disbeliever, and that this is irreconcilable with mainstream Islam.
Correction: Later Mu'tazilites clarified that they only deny the fasiq has 'complete iman' but they affirm he has iman in the sense of tasdiq and legal rulings, thus the fasiq is still a Muslim in the basic sense.

Checkpoint
According to Sharh al-Maqasid, in what sense do later Mu'tazilites deny iman for the fasiq?
A) They deny iman in every sense
B) They deny 'complete iman' that includes deeds
C) They deny iman as tasdiq
D) They deny the legal treatment of a believer

Key Takeaway
Later Mu'tazilites resolved the dispute by distinguishing between basic iman (tasdiq) and complete iman (with deeds), making the fasiq a believer in the basic sense but not in the complete sense.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you judge your own faith, do you base it more on your inner affirmation (tasdiq) or your outward obedience? Which one feels more secure to you, and why does this lesson challenge that security?
?
If 'complete iman' requires deeds, what do you possess right now that deserves ultimate praise? If you are honest, do you live as though you have complete iman, or do you rely on basic iman while hoping for more?
?
This passage shows that theological disputes can be reduced to definitions. Where in your own heart have you used redefinitions to avoid facing an uncomfortable truth about your faith? Will you now examine that area with the same scrutiny?

The Sign of Hypocrisy and the Exception of Yusuf's Brothers
Clarifies that the three signs of hypocrisy are not definitive proof.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that the three signs of hypocrisy are not conclusive indicators of actual hypocrisy unless they become a settled character trait (malaka).

Core Idea
A sign is not a verdict — hypocrisy requires a settled disposition.

The Prophet ﷺ mentioned three signs that indicate hypocrisy: speaking lies, breaking promises, and betraying trusts. But does merely exhibiting one of these once make someone a hypocrite? The scholars clarified that these signs only indicate true hypocrisy when they become a person's ingrained character (malaka). This lesson explains that important distinction using the case of Yusuf's brothers.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the translation. Observe the author's claim and the example.
Notice the phrase 'if they become a person's disposition'.
2
Identify the core claim
The passage states that the three characteristics are a sign of hypocrisy only when they together become a person's disposition (malaka). Without being a disposition, they are not a sign.
The term 'malaka' means a firmly rooted trait.
3
Study the example of Yusuf's brothers
The brothers had all three signs: they broke a promise, betrayed a trust, and lied. Yet they were not hypocrites by consensus. This shows that a sign is not definitive.
Understand why the brothers are an exception.
4
Reflect on the principle
The passage concludes that a sign indicating something may not be conclusive; the signified can be absent even when the sign exists.
This principle applies to many theological discussions.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المرصد الثالث - المقصد الرابع في أن مرتكبي الكبيرة من أهل الصلاة خلعة نفيسة ثم أخلفه لم يخرج ) بذلك ) عن الإيمان إلى النفاق إجماعاً)، وقيل: معناه أن هذه الخصال الثلاث إذا صارت معاً ملكة الشخص كانت علامة لنفاقه، وأما بدون كونها ملكة ،فلا ألا ترى أن إخوة يوسف وعدوا أباهم أن يحفظوا فأخلفوا وائتمنهم أبوهم فخانوا وكذبوا في قولهم فأكله الذئب وما كانوا منافقين اتفاقاً على أن العلامة الدالة على شيء قد لا تكون قطعية الدلالة فيجوز تخلف المدلول عنها.
The third observation - the fourth aim: concerning that the perpetrators of major sins among the people of prayer. A precious garment then he broke his promise did not thereby exit from faith to hypocrisy by consensus. And it is said: its meaning is that these three characteristics when they together become a person's disposition are a sign of his hypocrisy, but without being a disposition, no. Do you not see that the brothers of Yusuf promised their father to protect him and then broke their promise, and their father entrusted them and they betrayed and lied in their saying 'the wolf ate him' and they were not hypocrites, unanimously, because the sign indicating something may not be definitive in indication, so the signified may be absent from it.
Source pages p. 369

Commentary
This passage is an explanation of a prophetic tradition about three signs of hypocrisy. It begins with an introductory heading stating the topic: the perpetrator of major sins among the people of prayer. Then it mentions that if someone possesses these three signs but breaks his promise, he does not thereby exit from faith to hypocrisy by consensus. It clarifies that these three characteristics are only a sign of hypocrisy when they become a person's disposition together; without that, they are not a sign. It gives the example of the brothers of Yusuf: they promised to protect him, broke their promise, betrayed their father's trust, and lied, yet they were not hypocrites by agreement. The reason given is that a sign is not necessarily conclusive; the signified may be absent even though the sign exists.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Believing that anyone who exhibits one of the three signs (lying, breaking promises, betraying trusts) is automatically a hypocrite.
Correction: The passage clarifies that these signs indicate hypocrisy only when they become a person's settled character (malaka). Temporary or isolated acts do not make one a hypocrite, as shown by Yusuf's brothers.

Checkpoint
Why does the author argue that the brothers of Yusuf were not hypocrites despite possessing the three signs?
A) Because the signs were not all present simultaneously.
B) Because they did not have the disposition; the signs were not a firm character.
C) Because they repented.
D) Because the sign is always definitive.

Key Takeaway
The three signs of hypocrisy are only conclusive when they become a person's settled character; otherwise, a sign does not guarantee the presence of the signified.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you catch yourself lying or breaking a promise, do you immediately assume you are a hypocrite? How can distinguishing between occasional lapses and settled disposition protect you from despair while still motivating repentance?
?
The brothers of Yusuf exhibited all three signs yet were not hypocrites. Have you ever judged someone harshly based on a single mistake? How will you use this lesson to stop yourself from making hasty judgments about others' faith?
?
This passage teaches that signs are not verdicts. Where in your life have you treated an outward action as definitive proof of someone's inner state—whether of yourself or others? What would change if you consistently applied the principle that a sign is not always decisive?

The Snake Hole Analogy and Its Refutation
Presents a rational analogy and its refutation regarding faith and major sins.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the rational argument (snake hole analogy) used by those who claim major sins negate belief, and the counterargument that distinguishes between immediate/certain worldly harm and deferred/uncertain divine punishment.

Core Idea
Faith is not disproven by sin; the snake hole analogy fails because punishment is neither immediate nor certain.

This passage presents a rational proof (proof by analogy) that was used to argue that committing a major sin indicates lack of true belief. The author then refutes it by highlighting a crucial difference between the analogy's scenario and the reality of divine punishment. Understanding this distinction is essential for correct Islamic theology on faith and sin.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once and its literal translation. Notice how the author first presents the analogy, then provides the refutation.
The passage is short but dense with logical reasoning.
2
Understand the analogy and its claim
The analogy: if someone truly believes a snake is in a hole, he will not put his hand in. If he does, his belief is false. Similarly, if a believer truly believed in divine punishment, he would not sin. So sinning proves lack of belief.
This is the opponent's argument, not the author's.
3
Grasp the refutation's key point
The author points out a disanalogy: snake harm is immediate and certain, while divine punishment is deferred and uncertain because repentance and pardon are possible. Therefore the analogy fails.
This distinction shows why sinning does not necessarily negate belief.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الثاني ) عقلي وهو (أن من اعتقد ) من العقلاء ( أن في هذا الجحر حية لم يدخل يده فيه فإذا زعم ذلك ثم أدخل يده فيه علم أنه قاله لا عن اعتقاد، وكذا الحال فيمن ارتكب الكبيرة ( قلنا : مضرة الحية عاجلة محققة بخلاف عقاب الذنب لأنها آجلة ) وغير محققة، إذ يجوز التوبة والعفو فافترقا
Second: rational, and it is that whoever among rational people believes that in this hole there is a snake, he will not put his hand into it. So if he claims that and then puts his hand into it, it is known that he said it without belief. And likewise the case of the one who commits a major sin. We say: the harm of the snake is immediate and certain, unlike the punishment of the sin because it is deferred and not certain, since repentance and pardon are possible, so they differ.
Source pages p. 369

Commentary
This passage is an argument against those who say that committing a major sin proves one did not truly believe. It presents a rational analogy: if someone believes a snake is in a hole, he will not put his hand inside; if he does, it shows he did not truly believe. Similarly, if someone truly believed in divine punishment, he would not sin. The response (قلنا) distinguishes the two cases: the snake's harm is immediate and certain, while the punishment for sin is deferred and not certain because repentance and pardon are possible. Therefore, the analogy fails.

Key Terms

اعتقاد

عقاب
توبة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The analogy between worldly harm and divine punishment is perfect; sinning always proves disbelief.
Correction: The author shows the disanalogy: snake harm is immediate and certain, while divine punishment is deferred and uncertain due to repentance and pardon.

Checkpoint
What is the key difference between the snake hole scenario and sinning that the author points out?
A) The snake is visible, punishment is invisible.
B) The harm from the snake is immediate and certain, while punishment is deferred and uncertain.
C) The snake hole is a metaphor for sin.
D) The person does not actually believe in the snake.

Key Takeaway
The snake hole analogy fails because divine punishment is neither immediate nor certain, unlike snake harm, so sinning does not necessarily contradict belief.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you commit a sin, do you genuinely believe in Allah's punishment? Or do you rely on the excuse that punishment is deferred? What does that reveal about the strength of your faith?
?
If the snake's harm were delayed and uncertain, would you risk your hand? Then why do you risk Allah's punishment? Does your behavior match your claim of belief?
?
Are you using the possibility of repentance as a license to sin? Or does it drive you to seek forgiveness? Examine your heart: which attitude predominates?

Mu'tazila's First Proof: Hadd Penalties and Marital Separation
Refutes the Mu'tazila argument that the sinful is neither believer nor disbeliever based on legal treatment.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Mu'tazila's argument that the transgressor is neither believer nor disbeliever based on legal treatment, and the Ash'ari response that the issue hinges on the definition of faith.

Core Idea
Hadd penalties do not settle the status of faith; only the definition of faith does.

The Mu'tazila attempt to use the legal treatment of sinners by the Companions as evidence that the sinner is in an intermediate position between faith and disbelief. By examining their argument and the brief Ash'ari response, this lesson clarifies why such legal criteria cannot determine theological status.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once to grasp the Mu'tazila's claim and the response. Notice the structure: proof followed by rebuttal.
2
Identify the two evidences
The Mu'tazila give two pieces of evidence: the application of hadd penalties and the argument about marital separation. Find these in the text.
The first is that Companions applied hadd but treated the person as a Muslim; the second is that if the sinner were a disbeliever, mere accusation would cause separation.
3
Understand the Ash'ari response
The response is brief: 'We say: he is a believer' and refers back to the definition of faith. This shows that the debate is really about what faith means, not about legal rulings.
The response does not provide new evidence but redirects to the earlier definition.
4
Reflect on the key insight
Consider why legal treatment does not decide theological status. The Companions' actions are consistent with viewing the sinner as a believer who deserves punishment.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
احتج المعتزلة بوجهين : الأول : إن الفاسق ليس مؤمناً لما مر من أن الإيمان عبارة عن الطاعات ( ولا كافراً بالإجماع لأنهم ) أي الصحابة ومن بعدهم من السلف ( كانوا يقيمون عليه الحد) في الزنا وشرب الخمر وقذف المحصنات ) ولا يقتلونه ولا يحكمون بردته ويدفنونه في مقابر المسلمين، مع إجماعهم على أن الكافر لا يعامل ... كذلك، ( وأيضاً فيلزم ) من كون الفاسق كافراً ( بينونة المرأة ) عن زوجها ( بمجرد رمي الزوج إياها بالزنا من غير لعان وقضاء قاض، لأنه إن صدق الزوج ( فهي كافرة بارتكاب الزنا، (وإن كذب فهو كافر بارتكاب قذف المحصنة فكانت البينونة واقعة على التقديرين قلنا هو مؤمن وقد مر الكلام فيه في بيان حقيقة الإيمان
The Mu'tazila argued with two proofs: First: The transgressor is not a believer because, as previously mentioned, faith is expressions of obedience, and he is not a disbeliever by consensus because they (i.e., the Companions and those after them from the Salaf) used to apply the prescribed punishment on him for adultery, drinking wine, and false accusation of chaste women, and they did not kill him nor rule his apostasy and buried him in Muslim cemeteries, while they agreed that the disbeliever is not treated likewise. Also, it would follow from the transgressor being a disbeliever the separation of the wife from her husband merely by the husband accusing her of adultery without mutual cursing and a judge's ruling, because if the husband is truthful, she is a disbeliever by committing adultery; if he is false, he is a disbeliever by committing false accusation of a chaste woman, so separation would occur in both cases. We say: he is a believer, and the discussion about that has already passed in the explanation of the reality of faith.
Source pages p. 369

Commentary
This passage presents the first proof of the Mu'tazila and its answer. The Mu'tazila argue that the perpetrator of a major sin (فاسق) is not a believer because faith consists of obedience, and not a disbeliever because the Companions applied hadd penalties to them but did not treat them as apostates. They also argue that if the sinner were a disbeliever, marital separation would occur merely by accusation, which is absurd. The answer (قلنا) simply asserts that he is a believer and refers to earlier discussion on the reality of faith. The passage does not provide a detailed refutation here; it summarizes the debate.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The Mu'tazila's argument from legal treatment decisively proves that the sinner is neither believer nor disbeliever.
Correction: The Ash'ari response shows that the issue is settled by the definition of faith; legal treatment does not determine theological status.

Checkpoint
Why do the Mu'tazila claim that the transgressor is not a disbeliever?
A) Because the Companions killed them.
B) Because the Companions applied hadd penalties to them and buried them in Muslim cemeteries, unlike disbelievers.
C) Because they still prayed.
D) Because they repented.

Key Takeaway
The Mu'tazila's reliance on legal evidence does not resolve the nature of faith; the true criterion is the definition of faith itself.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever looked at a fellow Muslim committing a major sin and, in your heart, doubted whether they are truly a believer? How does this lesson—which affirms that the transgressor is still a believer—challenge that tendency and call you to re-examine your own judgment?
?
If your faith were measured solely by your outward obedience and the legal penalties you receive, what would that reveal about your iman? How does the answer 'he is a believer' (despite sin) redirect your focus from actions to the reality of faith as defined by Allah and His Messenger?
?
The Mu'tazila argued that a sinner cannot be a believer because faith equals obedience. Do you subconsciously equate your religious performance with your standing before Allah? What would it mean for you to truly accept that a person can be a believer yet fall into major sins—and how should that change how you view yourself and others?

Mu'tazila's Second Proof: Wāṣil's Argument for an Intermediate Position
Refutes Wāṣil's argument using prior consensus.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Wāṣil's argument for the intermediate position and its refutation based on prior consensus that every person is either a believer or a disbeliever.

Core Idea
The intermediate position breaks the pre-existing consensus on a binary classification.

Wāṣil ibn ‘Aṭā’ proposed a third category between faith and disbelief for the perpetrator of a major sin. He argued: since all agree he is a transgressor but differ on his faith, we take the agreed description. The author refutes this by pointing to the consensus before Wāṣil that every morally responsible person is either a believer or a disbeliever. Introducing a third category violates that consensus, so the argument is invalid.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage and its literal translation once to grasp the flow of the argument.
Notice the structure: Wāṣil's premise, then the refutation.
2
Identify Wāṣil's reasoning
Wāṣil says: 'We leave what is disputed (faith/disbelief) and take what is agreed upon (transgression).' Understand how he derives the intermediate position.
The intermediate position is simply calling the sinner a 'transgressor' without ruling on faith.
3
Analyze the refutation
The author asserts there was a prior consensus that a mukallaf is either mu'min or kafir. Introducing a third category breaks that consensus, making the argument false.
This refutation relies on the authority of ijmā' (consensus).
4
Reflect on the use of consensus
Consider how consensus is used as a decisive proof in kalam. Why is breaking consensus considered a fatal flaw?

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الثاني : ما قاله واصل بن لعمرو بن عبيد فرجع عمرو (إلى مذهبه وهو أن فسقه معلوم) وفاقاً، (وإيمانه مختلف فيه أي الأمة مجمعة على أن صاحب الكبيرة فاسق، واختلفوا في كونه مؤمناً أو كافراً ( فنترك المختلف فيه ونأخذ بالمتفق عليه، قلنا: قد أنه قطعاً ولا خلاف فيه ممن قبله من الأمة ( بل قد أجمع ) قبله ( على أنه ) أي المكلف إما مؤمن أو كافر، فالقول بالواسطة خرق بالإجماع المنعقد على الانحصار في ذينك القسمين، فيكون باطلاً بلا اشتباه .
Second: what Wāṣil ibn ‘Aṭā’ said—so ‘Amr returned to his doctrine—and that is that his transgression is agreed upon, and his faith is disputed, i.e., the community agrees that the perpetrator of a major sin is a transgressor, and they differ on whether he is a believer or a disbeliever. So we leave what is disputed and take what is agreed upon. We say: it is definite and there is no disagreement from those before him of the community; rather, consensus before him held that the morally responsible person is either a believer or a disbeliever. So the assertion of an intermediate position breaks the consensus on the restriction to those two categories, thus it is false without doubt.
Source pages p. 369

Commentary
This passage presents Wāṣil ibn ‘Aṭā’s argument for the intermediate position (al-manzila bayn al-manzilatayn). He argues that since everyone agrees the sinner is a transgressor (fāsiq) but disagrees on whether he is a believer or disbeliever, we should stick with the agreed-upon description (transgressor) and leave the disputed one. The answer rejects this by pointing to the pre-existing consensus that a mukallaf is either a believer or a disbeliever; introducing a third category violates that consensus, so the argument is invalid. This refutation is considered definitive by the author.

Key Terms
واصل بن عطاء
فسق
إيمان
كفر
منزلة بين منزلتين
إجماع

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The intermediate position is a logical compromise between conflicting views.
Correction: The response insists that it breaks the original consensus on binary classification, so it is not a valid compromise but a violation of established doctrine.

Checkpoint
How does the author refute Wāṣil's argument for the intermediate position?
A) By proving that the sinner is actually a believer.
B) By stating that there is a consensus before Wāṣil that a person is either a believer or a disbeliever.
C) By arguing that the sinner is a disbeliever.
D) By accepting the intermediate position but redefining it.

Key Takeaway
The intermediate position is rejected because it contradicts the pre-existing consensus that every mukallaf is either a believer or a disbeliever.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you encounter a theological disagreement, do you tend to seek a middle ground to avoid conflict, or do you hold firmly to established teachings? How does this lesson challenge your natural inclination towards compromise?
?
This refutation relies on the authority of prior consensus. In your own life, do you accept that the consensus of the early generations is binding on matters of creed? If so, how does that affect your attitude towards contemporary theological innovations or compromises?
?
The passage forces a binary choice: believer or disbeliever. Are there areas in your faith where you have avoided taking a clear stance, thinking a 'middle way' is safer? What would it mean for you to fully commit to the binary classification taught by the consensus?

Later Mu'tazila: Faith in the Sense of Tasdiq but Not Perfect Faith
Explains that later Mu'tazila accept calling the sinner a believer in the sense of tasdiq but not in the sense of perfect faith.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the distinction later Mu'tazila made between faith as inner affirmation and legal status versus faith as perfect virtue requiring good deeds.

Core Idea
Later Mu'tazila reconciled by affirming faith as tasdiq but denying perfect faith.

This passage from Sharḥ al-Maqāṣid clarifies that later Mu'tazila did not deny all senses of faith to a sinner. They accepted faith as inner affirmation (taṣdīq) and in legal treatment (aḥkām), but they denied the highest sense of faith that deserves ultimate praise and veneration. This distinction effectively resolves the long-standing dispute between Sunnis and Mu'tazila over the status of the grave sinner.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once, then the literal translation. Notice how the commentator frames the issue as a resolution of dispute.
The passage begins by citing al-Maqāṣid.
2
Identify the three senses of faith
The passage mentions three distinct meanings: faith as taṣdīq (inner affirmation), faith as application of legal rulings, and faith as perfect faith (al-īmān al-kāmil) deserving praise. Locate each in the text.
The third sense is what the Mu'tazila deny to the sinner.
3
Understand the resolution
Consider how this distinction removes the dispute: Sunnis and Mu'tazila can agree the sinner has the first two but not the third. The commentator suggests this is practically a return from the original Mu'tazilite position.
Compare to the similar strategy in the attributes debate mentioned at the end.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : ( احتج المعتزلة) قال في شرح المقاصد : وفي كلام المتأخرين من المعتزلة ما يرفع النزاع وذلك أنهم لا ينكرون وصف الفاسق بالإيمان بمعنى التصديق أو بمعنى إجراء الأحكام، بل بمعنى استحقاق المدح وغاية التعظيم وهو الذي نسميه الإيمان الكامل ونعتبر فيه الأعمال وتنفيه عن الفاسق فيكون لهم منزلة بين منزلة النوع من الإيمان وبين منزلة الكفر باتفاق، وكأنه رجوع عن المذهب وإعراض كما يقال في نفي الصفات: إنا نريد ما هو من قبيل الأعراض وإلا فقد ماؤهم يصرحون بأن من أخل بالطاعة ليس بمؤمن بحسب الشرع، بل بمجرد اللغة وبأن القول بتعدد التقديم كفر من غير فرق بين العرض وغيره .
His saying: 'The Mu'tazila argued' – he said in Sharh al-Maqāṣid: In the speech of the later Mu'tazila there is something that removes the dispute, and that is that they do not deny describing the transgressor as having faith in the sense of tasdiq or in the sense of applying legal rulings, but rather in the sense of deserving praise and utmost veneration, which is what we call perfect faith and in which we consider deeds, and they negate it from the transgressor. So for them there is a station between the station of the type of faith and the station of disbelief by agreement. And it is as if it is a return from the doctrine and a turning away, as is said in the negation of attributes: we mean what is of the category of accidents, otherwise their water [i.e., their doctrine] declares that whoever neglects obedience is not a believer according to the Sharia, but merely according to language, and that the statement of multiple prepositions is disbelief without difference between accident and other.
Source pages p. 369

Commentary
This passage is a commentary from Sharh al-Maqāṣid explaining that later Mu'tazila actually resolved the dispute. They do not deny that the sinner has faith in the sense of tasdiq (affirmation) or in the sense of receiving legal rulings (like being treated as a Muslim in worldly matters), but they deny that the sinner has faith in the sense of deserving praise and ultimate honor, which is called 'perfect faith' (al-īmān al-kāmil) and which requires good deeds. Thus, the sinner is in an intermediate station between the category of faith and disbelief. The commentator suggests this is practically a return from the original Mu'tazilite position. He compares it to the issue of divine attributes where some try to resolve by saying they mean accidents.

Key Terms
تصديق
إيمان كامل
أحكام
مدح
تعظيم
منزلة بين منزلتين

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That the Mu'tazila deny any faith to the grave sinner in all senses.
Correction: Later Mu'tazila actually affirm faith in the sense of inner belief (tasdiq) and legal treatment, but deny perfect faith that entails praise and is conditional on deeds.

Checkpoint
According to the commentary, what sense of 'faith' do later Mu'tazila deny to the sinner?
A) Faith in the sense of tasdiq.
B) Faith in the sense of legal rulings.
C) Faith in the sense of perfect faith deserving praise.
D) Faith in the sense of language.

Key Takeaway
Later Mu'tazila affirm faith in the sense of inner belief and legal treatment but deny the perfect faith that entails praise and is conditional on deeds.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The later Mu'tazila say that a sinner has tasdiq but not perfect faith because perfect faith requires deeds. Do you ever assume that just because you say the shahada, your faith is complete? Look at your life today: where are you neglecting obedience, and what does that say about the perfection of your faith?
?
This lesson shows a theological distinction between basic faith and perfect faith. But for you, personally, are you content to have only the minimum that saves you from eternal disbelief? Or do you long for that faith that deserves Allah's praise—a faith that transforms your actions? What one deed can you commit to today to move from tasdiq toward perfect faith?
?
The concept of an 'intermediate station' might sound like a comfortable middle ground. But honestly, is your life truly in between, mixing good deeds with sins, without a clear commitment to obedience? Repent from the sins that keep you from that perfect faith, and ask Allah to grant you a faith that shows in your actions.

The Consensus on Not Declaring Ahl al-Qibla as Disbelievers
States the consensus that no one from Ahl al-Qibla is declared a disbeliever, with evidence from early scholars.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the classical consensus that no one from Ahl al-Qibla is declared a disbeliever, along with the evidence from early scholars and the principle of reciprocity in takfir.

Core Idea
Unity in Qibla, not in belief: majority refused to declare anyone who prays toward Mecca a disbeliever.

This passage presents the fifth aim of the discussion: whether opponents of truth among the people of the Qibla are declared disbelievers. The author establishes that the majority of theologians and jurists hold that no one from Ahl al-Qibla is declared a disbeliever, supported by statements from key figures like al-Ash'ari, al-Shafi'i, and Abu Hanifa. The passage also notes cases where some groups did declare others disbelievers, but the dominant position is restraint.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and identify the main claim
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the translation. Notice that the opening sentence states the consensus clearly.
The phrase 'جمهور المتكلمين والفقهاء على أنه لا يكفر أحد من أهل القبلة' is the thesis.
2
Examine the evidence from early scholars
The author quotes al-Ash'ari, al-Shafi'i, and Abu Hanifa. Pay attention to what each one said and how their statements support the consensus.
Al-Ash'ari emphasizes that Islam unites them despite differences; al-Shafi'i only excludes the Khaṭṭābiyya.
3
Understand the principle of reciprocity in takfir
The passage ends with Abu Ishaq's principle: we only declare disbelievers those who declare us disbelievers. Reflect on how this limits takfir.
This principle is a key lesson in restraint and avoiding mutual excommunication.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
[ المقصد الخامس : الاتفاق على أنه لا يكفر أحد من أهل القبلة ] في أن المخالف للحق من أهل القبلة هل يكفر أم لا. جمهور المتكلمين والفقهاء على أنه لا يكفر أحد من أهل القبلة فإن الشيخ أبا الحسن قال في أول كتاب مقالات الإسلاميين: اختلف المسلمون بعد نبيهم عليه السلام في أشياء، ضلل بعضهم بعضاً وتبرأ بعضهم عن بعض، فصاروا فرقاً متباينين إلا أن الإسلام يجمعهم فهذا مذهبه وعليه أكثر أصحابنا، وقد نقل عن الشافعي أنه قال : لا أرد شهادة أحد من أهل الأهواء إلا الخطابية، فإنهم يعتقدون حل الكذب، وحكى الحاكم صاحب المختصر في كتب المنتقى عن أبي حنيفة رحمة الله عليه أنه لم يكفر أحداً من أهل القبلة وحكى أبو بكر الرازي مثل ذلك عن الكرخي وغيره ( والمعتزلة الذين كانوا ( قبل أبي الحسين تجامعوا فكفروا الأصحاب) في أمور سيأتيك تفصيلها، (فعارضه بعضنا بالمثل فكفرهم في أمور أخر يستطلع عليها ( وقد كفر المجسمة مخالفوهم من المعتزلة ( وقال الأستاذ : ) أبو إسحاق ( كل مخالف يكفرنا فنحن نكفره وإلا فلا
The fifth aim: the agreement that no one from the people of the qibla is declared a disbeliever. Concerning whether the opponent of truth from the people of the qibla is declared a disbeliever or not. The majority of theologians and jurists hold that no one from the people of the qibla is declared a disbeliever. For Shaykh Abu al-Hasan said at the beginning of his book Maqālāt al-Islāmiyyīn: The Muslims after their Prophet, peace be upon him, differed in matters, some declaring others misguided and dissociating from each other, so they became divergent sects, but Islam gathers them. This is his doctrine and upon it are most of our companions. It was transmitted from al-Shafi'i that he said: I do not reject the testimony of anyone from the people of innovation except the Khaṭṭābiyya, for they believe in the permissibility of lying. And al-Hakim, author of al-Mukhtasar, narrated in the books of al-Muntaqā from Abu Hanifa, may Allah have mercy on him, that he did not declare anyone from the people of the qibla a disbeliever. And Abu Bakr al-Razi narrated similarly from al-Karkhi and others. The Mu'tazila who were before Abu al-Husayn gathered and declared the companions disbelievers in matters whose details will come to you. So some of us reciprocated and declared them disbelievers in other matters that will be presented. And the Mujassima declared their opponents from the Mu'tazila disbelievers. And al-Ustadh Abu Ishaq said: Every opponent who declares us disbelievers, we declare him a disbeliever; otherwise, no.
Source pages p. 369

Commentary
This passage opens the fifth aim (al-maqsad al-khāmis) which addresses whether opponents of truth from Ahl al-Qibla are declared disbelievers. It begins by stating that the majority of theologians and jurists hold that no one from Ahl al-Qibla is declared kāfir. It provides evidence from prominent figures: Abu al-Hasan al-Ash'ari's statement that despite differences, Islam unites them; al-Shafi'i's exception of only the Khaṭṭābiyya for their belief that lying is permissible; Abu Hanifa's rejection of takfīr of Ahl al-Qibla, transmitted by al-Hakim and Abu Bakr al-Razi; and the practice of the Mu'tazila before Abu al-Husayn who declared the Companions disbelievers, to which some responded in kind. The Mujassima also declared Mu'tazila disbelievers. Finally, Abu Ishaq's principle: we only declare those who declare us disbelievers. The passage establishes the cautious position of not takfīr except in extreme cases.

Correction: The passage simply refrains from declaring them disbelievers despite disagreements; it does not imply they are all correct.

Key Takeaway
The majority of classical scholars held that despite grave theological disagreements, as long as a person identifies as Muslim and prays toward Mecca, they should not be declared a disbeliever.

?

?
Imagine you are in a community where a group prays toward Mecca but holds a theological view you consider false. How would you treat them? Would you exclude them or include them based on their qibla? What does this say about your heart?

The Prophet's Precedent on Disputed Issues
Argues from Prophetic practice against declaring Ahl al-Qibla disbelievers.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the Prophet's practice of not investigating disputed theological details provides the basis for not declaring Ahl al-Qibla disbelievers.

Core Idea
The Prophet did not probe disputed creedal details—nor should we.

This lesson examines a key argument from the classical kalam tradition: the Prophet Muhammad (ﷺ) did not require detailed theological precision on every disputed issue before accepting someone's Islam. The Companions and Followers followed this example. Therefore, we too should refrain from declaring fellow Muslims disbelievers over matters of legitimate difference.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Note that the text is cut off at the end, but the intended conclusion is clear from the context.
The Arabic ends with 'فعلم أن' – the conclusion is implied.
2
Identify the disputed issues listed
The passage mentions several examples of theological disputes among Ahl al-Qibla: divine attributes (knowledge by a knowledge), creation of actions, direction, visibility. List them.
These are not the only disputes, but representative ones.
3
Recognize the Prophet's inaction
The key claim is that the Prophet did not investigate (لم يبحث) belief in these details for the purpose of judging a person's Islam. The same is said of the Companions and Followers.
The argument uses the absence of investigation as evidence.
4
Draw the doctrinal conclusion
Since the Prophet and early generations did not require detailed agreement on these issues, we should not declare those who differ on them as disbelievers. That is the chosen position (المختار عندنا).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
لنا على ما هو المختار عندنا وهو أن لا نكفر أحداً من أهل القبلة ( إن المسائل التي اختلف فيها أهل القبلة من كون الله تعالى عالماً بعلم أو موجداً لفعل العبد أو غير متحيز ولا في جهة ونحوها) ككونه مرئياً أولا، (لم يبحث النبي اعتقاد حكم بإسلامه فيها ولا الصحابة ولا التابعون فعلم أن
For us, based on what is chosen for us, which is that we do not declare anyone from the people of the qibla a disbeliever: the issues about which the people of the qibla differed, such as whether Allah is knowing by a knowledge or is the creator of the servant's action, or is not a body nor in a direction and similar matters, such as whether He is visible or not, the Prophet did not investigate [concerning] belief in a ruling of his Islam regarding them, nor did the Companions nor the Followers. So it is known that...
Source pages p. 370

Commentary
This passage provides the main proof for not declaring Ahl al-Qibla disbelievers. It states that the issues of dispute (like divine attributes, creation of actions, direction, visibility of God) were not investigated by the Prophet (ﷺ) in terms of requiring a specific belief for a person to be considered Muslim. Neither the Companions nor the Followers examined these matters for the purpose of judging Islam. The argument is that if such investigations were necessary, the Prophet and early generations would have done so. The conclusion (implied by 'فعلن أن') is that it is not required, so we do not declare those who hold different views on these issues as disbelievers. The passage is cut off but the intent is clear.

General Faith Sufficient for Iman
Answers an objection: the Prophet's message in general is sufficient for faith.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that general acceptance of the Prophet's message suffices for valid faith, and that detailed theological agreement is not required for being a believer.

Core Idea
General faith suffices; details do not define disbelief.

This lesson addresses a key objection: must a believer affirm every theological detail? The commentator draws from Sharh al-Maqāṣid to argue that the Prophet's message in its general form is enough. This clarifies why scholars disagree on details without declaring each other disbelievers.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic fragment and its translation. Note that the text is incomplete but the core idea is clear.
The Arabic cuts off, but the meaning is preserved.
2
Identify the objection and the answer
The passage presents a potential objection (someone might say) and an answer: the Prophet's general message suffices. Locate the objection and the response.
3
Reflect on its significance
Consider why this matters: it allows for differences in detailed theology while maintaining unity of faith. Think about how this applies to debates among scholars.
This connects to the learning objective.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله: (لم يبحث النبي عليه السلام قال في شرح المقاصد : ولقائل أن يجيب بأن ما جاء به النبي عليه الصلاة والسلام إجمالاً كاف في صحة الإيمان وإنما يحتاج التصديق بجميع
His saying: 'The Prophet did not investigate' – he said in Sharh al-Maqāṣid: Someone may answer that what the Prophet brought in general is sufficient for the validity of faith; only affirmation of all that he brought is needed...
Source pages p. 370

Commentary
This passage is a commentary from Sharh al-Maqāṣid addressing a potential objection to the previous proof. The objection is: even if the Prophet did not investigate these details, his message in general is enough; one must affirm everything he brought (in general). The commentary suggests that the opponent might argue that the Prophet's general message (ijmālan) suffices for valid faith, but detailed belief is not required. This actually supports the position that disagreement on details does not make one a disbeliever. The text is cut off, but the idea is that believing in the general revelation is sufficient; thus those who differ on details are still believers.

Consensus on Not Excommunicating Ahl al-Qibla
The author states the consensus against takfir of Ahl al-Qibla and distinguishes essential from non-essential beliefs.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Sunni consensus that no one from Ahl al-Qibla is declared a disbeliever for differing on non-essential doctrinal issues, and the crucial distinction between essential and non-essential beliefs.

Core Idea
Only denial of absolutely necessary Islamic beliefs warrants excommunication; disagreement on secondary matters does not.

This passage from Sharh al-Mawaqif presents the mainstream Sunni position on takfir (excommunication). It surveys the views of founding imams, provides a proof from the practice of the Prophet and early generations, and clarifies the boundary between essential and non-essential beliefs. This is a key text in understanding inter-Muslim disputes.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own argument come through before you interpret.
Notice the author first states the consensus, then cites authorities, then gives a proof, then clarifies the rule.
2
Identify the consensus statements
The passage quotes al-Ash'ari, al-Shafi'i, Abu Hanifa, and others affirming that no Muslim is declared a disbeliever. Note each scholar's wording.
Al-Ash'ari's quote from Maqalat al-Islamiyyin is especially important.
3
Locate the proof
Find the argument that the Prophet (peace be upon him) and the early generations did not investigate or judge the disputed issues. This is the core evidence for the preferred view.
4
Understand the distinction
The passage distinguishes between 'necessities of Islam' (ضروريات الإسلام) and other issues. Denial of the former is kufr; disagreement on the latter is not. Also note the caveat about pre-eternity of some bodies.
This distinction is crucial to avoid both extremism and negligence.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المقصد الخامس: الاتفاق على أنه لا يكفر أحد من أهل القبلة. في أن المخالف للحق من أهل القبلة هل يكفر أم لا. جمهور المتكلمين والفقهاء على أنه لا يكفر أحد من أهل القبلة، فإن الشيخ أبا الحسن قال في أول كتاب مقالات الإسلاميين: اختلف المسلمون بعد نبيهم عليه السلام في أشياء، ضلل بعضهم بعضاً وتبرأ بعضهم عن بعض، فصاروا فرقاً متباينين إلا أن الإسلام يجمعهم. فهذا مذهبه وعليه أكثر أصحابنا. وقد نقل عن الشافعي أنه قال: لا أرد شهادة أحد من أهل الأهواء إلا الخطابية، فإنهم يعتقدون حل الكذب. وحكى الحاكم صاحب المختصر في كتب المنتقى عن أبي حنيفة رحمة الله عليه أنه لم يكفر أحداً من أهل القبلة. وحكى أبو بكر الرازي مثل ذلك عن الكرخي وغيره. والمعتزلة الذين كانوا قبل أبي الحسين تجامعوا فكفروا الأصحاب في أمور سيأتيك تفصيلها. فعارضه بعضنا بالمثل فكفرهم في أمور أخر يستطلع عليها. وقد كفر المجسمة مخالفوهم من المعتزلة. وقال الأستاذ أبو إسحاق: كل مخالف يكفرنا فنحن نكفره، وإلا فلا. لنا على ما هو المختار عندنا وهو أن لا نكفر أحداً من أهل القبلة. إن المسائل التي اختلف فيها أهل القبلة من كون الله تعالى عالماً بعلم أو موجداً لفعل العبد أو غير متحيز ولا في جهة ونحوها، ككونه مرئياً أولا، لم يبحث النبي عليه السلام ولا الصحابة ولا التابعون عن اعتقاد حكم بإسلامه فيها. قوله: (كل مخالف يكفر فنحن نكفره) فإن من قال للمسلم يا كافر يكفر لقوله عليه السلام: من قال لأخيه المسلم: يا كافر فقد باء به أحدهما. وسيجيء جوابه. قوله: (لا يكفر أحد من أهل القبلة) معناه أن الذين اتفقوا على ما هو من ضروريات الإسلام كحدوث العالم وحشر الأجساد وما أشبه ذلك، واختلفوا في أصول سواه كمسألة الصفات وخلق الأعمال وعموم الإرادة وقدم الكلام وجواز الرؤية ونحو ذلك مما لا نزاع أن الحق فيها واحد، لا يكفر المخالف للحق في ذلك. وإلا فلا نزاع في كفر أهل القبلة المواظب طول العمر إلى الطاعات، باعتقاد قدم العالم ونفي الحشر ونفي العلم بالجزئيات ونحو ذلك، وكذا بصدور شيء من موجبات الكفر عنه. كذا في شرح المقاصد. ولعله أراد اعتقاد قدمه مع نفي الحشر كفر، وإلا فقد ذهب كثير من حكماء الإسلام إلى قدم بعض الأجسام، والفحول من أرباب المكاشفة ذهبوا إلى قدم العرش والكرسي دون سائر الأفلاك، فلا وجه للتكفير إذ لا تكذيب فيه للنبي عليه السلام. والله أعلم بمراده.
The Fifth Objective: The Agreement that No One from the People of the Qibla is Declared a Disbeliever. Concerning whether the one who opposes the truth from the People of the Qibla is declared a disbeliever or not. The majority of theologians and jurists are of the opinion that no one from the People of the Qibla is declared a disbeliever. Indeed, Shaykh Abu al-Hasan said at the beginning of his book Maqalat al-Islamiyyin: 'The Muslims after their Prophet (peace be upon him) disagreed in matters, some of them declared others misguided and some dissociated themselves from others, so they became divergent sects, but Islam unites them.' This is his doctrine and upon it are most of our companions. And it was transmitted from al-Shafi'i that he said: 'I do not reject the testimony of anyone from the people of desires except the Khattabiyya, for they believe in the permissibility of lying.' And al-Hakim, author of al-Mukhtasar, narrated in the books of al-Muntaqa from Abu Hanifa (may Allah have mercy on him) that he did not declare anyone from the People of the Qibla a disbeliever. And Abu Bakr al-Razi narrated similar from al-Karkhi and others. And the Mu'tazila who were before Abu al-Husayn gathered and declared the companions disbelievers in matters whose details will come to you. So some of us opposed them with the like and declared them disbelievers in other matters that will be looked into. And the Mujassima declared their opponents from the Mu'tazila disbelievers. And al-Ustadh Abu Ishaq said: 'Every opponent who declares us disbelievers, we declare him a disbeliever; otherwise not.' Our proof for what is chosen by us, which is that we do not declare anyone from the People of the Qibla a disbeliever, is that the issues over which the People of the Qibla disagreed, such as whether Allah is knowing by knowledge or is the originator of the servant's action, or is not a body nor in a direction, and the like, and whether He is visible or not, the Prophet (peace be upon him) did not investigate, nor did the Companions or the Followers, concerning the belief of a person's Islam being judged therein. His statement: 'Every opponent who declares us disbelievers, we declare him a disbeliever' — for whoever says to a Muslim 'O disbeliever' becomes a disbeliever because of his statement (peace be upon him): 'Whoever says to his Muslim brother "O disbeliever," then it returns upon one of them.' And its answer will come. His statement: 'No one from the People of the Qibla is declared a disbeliever' means that those who agreed upon what is from the necessities of Islam, such as the temporal origination of the world, the resurrection of bodies, and the like, and disagreed in other principles such as the issue of the attributes, the creation of deeds, the generality of the divine will, the pre-eternity of speech, the permissibility of the vision [of God], and the like, concerning which there is no dispute that the truth is one in them, the one who opposes the truth in that is not declared a disbeliever. Otherwise, there is no dispute about declaring a disbeliever the one from the People of the Qibla who, despite constant obedience over a long lifetime, believes in the pre-eternity of the world, the denial of resurrection, the denial of knowledge of particulars, and the like, and similarly by committing something from the causes of disbelief. Thus in Sharh al-Maqasid. And perhaps he meant that belief in its pre-eternity along with denial of resurrection is disbelief; otherwise, many of the sages of Islam have affirmed the pre-eternity of some bodies, and the great men of the people of unveiling affirmed the pre-eternity of the Throne and the Chair without the other spheres, so there is no ground for declaring disbelief since there is no denial of the Prophet (peace be upon him) in that. And Allah knows best his intent.
Source pages p. 370

Commentary
This passage states the position of the majority of theologians and jurists that no one from the People of the Qibla (i.e., Muslims) is declared a disbeliever simply for opposing the truth in matters of doctrinal detail. The author begins by citing the founder of the Ash'ari school, Abu al-Hasan al-Ash'ari, who in his Maqalat al-Islamiyyin noted that despite disagreements and mutual declarations of misguidance, Islam unites them. This is followed by quotes from al-Shafi'i (who only excepted the Khattabiyya due to their belief in lying), Abu Hanifa (who did not excommunicate any Muslim), and others. The Mu'tazila and Mujassima are mentioned as having engaged in mutual takfir. The preferred view is not to excommunicate. The author then clarifies that the issues in dispute (e.g., attributes, creation of acts, divine will, speech, beatific vision) were not investigated by the Prophet or the early generations, implying they are not essential to Islam. A crucial distinction is made: matters that are from the necessities of Islam (ضروريات الإسلام) such as the creation of the world and bodily resurrection, if denied, do constitute disbelief. But differences in other issues do not warrant takfir. A caveat is added: some early sages held that certain bodies (like the Throne) are pre-eternic, but this does not entail denial of the Prophet, so it is not kufr. The passage concludes with the phrase 'Allah knows best his intent,' indicating a cautious approach.

Key Terms
تكفير
أهل القبلة
ضروريات الإسلام
مخالف

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Many people think any doctrinal error or innovation makes a person a disbeliever.
Correction: The passage shows that only denial of absolutely necessary beliefs (like creation and resurrection) constitutes kufr; other disagreements do not.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the basis for not declaring a Muslim a disbeliever for differing in non-essential doctrinal issues?
A) Because all opinions are equally valid
B) Because the Prophet and early Muslims did not investigate or judge such differences
C) Because the Mu'tazila prohibited takfir
D) Because all Muslims are guaranteed Paradise

Key Takeaway
The Sunni consensus is that no Muslim is declared a disbeliever for contradictory views on non-essential issues; only denial of foundational Islamic beliefs such as creation and resurrection constitutes kufr.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever silently dismissed another Muslim as misguided or outside the fold because they hold a different view on a secondary issue? How does the Prophet’s silence on these matters confront your heart today?
?
If you honestly examine your own heart, do you find more unity with Muslims who disagree with you on non-essentials, or do you feel a sense of superiority? What would it cost you to embrace the majority position of 'Islam unites them'?
?
When you hear of a fellow Muslim who believes in something you consider wrong (e.g., on attributes or destiny), does your first reaction lean towards condemnation or compassion? Will you intentionally seek to hold your tongue and your judgment, asking Allah to guide them, starting today?

Refuting the Objection of the Prophet's Silence on Doctrinal Details
The author rebuts the claim that the Prophet's silence implies tacit approval of all views.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the Prophet's silence on detailed doctrinal issues does not imply that all views are acceptable, and how the foundations of Islam are clear while detailed disagreements are not grounds for takfir.

Core Idea
The Prophet's silence does not mean approval of all views—foundational beliefs are clear, details are not essential.

This passage addresses a key objection: if the Prophet did not discuss detailed theological issues, does that mean he tacitly approved of all opinions? The author argues that this assumption is unfounded, because the basic principles of Islam are self-evident and sufficient for salvation, while detailed disagreements involve complex interpretations that are not essential. Through a powerful illustration from Imam al-Razi, we see that even common people can grasp the foundational proofs for God's existence and prophethood. This lesson guides you through the objection, its refutation, and the implications for theology.

Guided Path
1
Understand the objection
The objection claims that the Prophet's silence means he approved of all later doctrinal views, using the parallel of God's attributes.
The objector argues that just as the Prophet did not inquire about belief in God's knowledge and power because it was known summarily, so too for other issues.
2
Examine the refutation
The author calls the objection 'stubbornness' and points out that Bedouins could not have had such detailed knowledge. Foundational attributes are necessary for prophethood, so general belief implies them; contested issues are not.
Key difference: belief in God's knowledge and power is required for the miracle to be evidence of prophethood.
3
Learn from al-Razi's example
Imam al-Razi gives a clear example: seeing a garden with uneven ripening shows the maker is a free agent. This proves that foundational proofs for Islam are apparent to everyone.
The example shows natural, necessary knowledge of a free agent through observed design.
4
Apply the distinction
Disputed issues are not like clear foundations; they involve conflicting interpretations, so they cannot be prerequisites for Islam or grounds for excommunication.
The author warns against takfir due to great danger.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله: (لم يبحث النبي عليه السلام) قال في شرح المقاصد: ولقائل أن يجيب بأن ما جاء به النبي عليه الصلاة والسلام إجمالاً كاف في صحة الإيمان وإنما يحتاج تلك صحة دين الإسلام لا تتوقف على معرفة الحق في تلك المسائل. وأن الخطأ فيها ليس قادحاً في حقيقة الإسلام، إذ لو توقفت عليها وكان الخطأ قادحاً في الحقيقة لوجب أن يبحث عن كيفية اعتقادهم فيها، لكن لم يجر حديث شيء منها في زمانه عليه السلام ولا في زمانهم أصلاً. فإن قيل: لعله عليه السلام عرف منهم ذلك (أي كونهم عالمين بها إجمالاً) فلم يبحث عنها لذلك، كما لم يبحث عن علمهم بعلمه وقدرته مع وجوب اعتقادهما، وما ذلك إلا لعلمه بأنهم عالمون على طريق الجملة بأنه تعالى عالم قادر، فكذا الحال في تلك المسائل. قلنا: ما ذكرتموه مكابرة، لأنا نعلم أن الأعراب الذين جاؤوا إليه عليه السلام ما كانوا كلهم عالمين بأنه تعالى عالم بالعلم لا بالذات، وأنه مرئي في الدار الآخرة، وأنه ليس بجسم ولا مكان وجهة، وأنه قادر على أفعال العباد كلها، وأنه موجد لها بأسرها، فالقول بأنهم كانوا عالمين بها مما علم فساده بالضرورة. وأما العلم والقدرة فيهما مما يتوقف عليه ثبوت نبوته لتوقف دلالة المعجزة عليهما، فكان الاعتراف والعلم بها (أي بالنبوة) دليلاً للعلم بها، ولو إجمالاً، فلذلك لم يبحث عنهما. قال الإمام الرازي: الأصول التي يتوقف عليها صحة نبوة محمد صلى الله عليه وسلم أدلتها على ما يليق بأصحاب الجمل ظاهرة، فإن من دخل بستاناً ورأى أزهاراً حادثة بعد أن لم تكن، ثم رأى عنقود عنب قد اسود جميع حباته إلا حبة واحدة مع تساوي نسبة الماء والهواء وحر الشمس إلى جميع تلك الحبات، فإنه يضطر إلى العلم بأن محدثه فاعل مختار، لأن دلالة الفعل المحكم على علم فاعله واختياره ضرورية، ودلالة المعجزة على صدق المدعى ضرورية أيضاً. وإذا عرف هذه الأصول أمكن العلم بصدق الرسول. فثبت أن أصول الإسلام جلية وأن أدلتها مجملة واضحة، فلذلك لم يبحث عنها. بخلاف المسائل التي اختلف فيها فإنها في الظهور والجلاء ليست مثل تلك الأصول، بل أكثرها مما ورد في الكتاب والسنة ما يتخيله المبطل معارضاً لما يحتج به المحق فيها، وكل واحد منهما يدعي أن التأويل المطابق لمذهبه أولى، فلا يمكن جعلها مما يتوقف عليه صحة الإسلام، فلا يجوز الإقدام على التكفير إذ فيه خطر عظيم. إلى بيان الحق في التفاصيل عند ملاحظتها، وإن كانت مما لا خلاف في تكفير المخالف فيها كحدوث العالم، فكم من مؤمن لم يعرف معنى الحادث والقديم أصلاً ولم يخطر بباله حديث حشر الأجساد قطعاً، لكن إذا لاحظ ذلك فلو لم يصدق كان كافراً. والجواب: إنا نقطع بأن منهم من وقف على التفاصيل وإن لم يكن كلهم كذلك. قوله: (بأصحاب الجمل) أي أرباب الإجمال فإنهم لا يقدرون على تنقيح الدليل
His statement: 'The Prophet (peace be upon him) did not investigate' — he said in Sharh al-Maqasid: And it is possible for an objector to answer that what the Prophet (peace and blessings be upon him) brought in summary is sufficient for the validity of faith, and it is only that… the validity of the religion of Islam does not depend on knowledge of the truth in those issues. And error in them is not detrimental to the reality of Islam, for if it depended on them and error were detrimental to the reality, then it would have been necessary to investigate the nature of their belief in them, but no mention of any of them occurred in his time (peace be upon him) nor in their time at all. If it is said: Perhaps he (peace be upon him) knew from them that (i.e., that they were knowledgeable of them in summary) and therefore did not investigate them, just as he did not investigate their knowledge of His knowledge and power despite the obligation of believing in them, and that was only because he knew that they knew in a general way that He is Knowing and Powerful; so likewise in those issues. We say: What you have mentioned is stubbornness, for we know that the Bedouins who came to him (peace be upon him) were not all knowledgeable that He is Knowing by knowledge not by essence, and that He is visible in the Hereafter, and that He is not a body nor in a place nor direction, and that He is powerful over all the actions of servants, and that He is the originator of them all; so the statement that they were knowledgeable of them is known to be necessarily false. As for knowledge and power, they are things on which the establishment of his prophethood depends because the indication of the miracle depends on them, so acknowledgment and knowledge of them (i.e., of prophethood) is a proof for knowledge of them, even if in summary; therefore he did not investigate them. Imam al-Razi said: The principles on which the validity of the prophethood of Muhammad (may Allah bless him and grant him peace) depends, their proofs are apparent in a way befitting the people of summary. For if someone enters a garden and sees flowers that came into being after not having been, then sees a bunch of grapes that have all turned black except one grape, despite the equal proportion of water, air, and sun heat to all the grapes, he will be compelled to know that its originator is a free agent, because the indication of the precise action to the knowledge and choice of its agent is necessary, and the indication of the miracle to the truthfulness of the claimant is also necessary. When these principles are known, knowledge of the truthfulness of the messenger becomes possible. Thus it is established that the principles of Islam are clear and their proofs are summarized and evident; therefore he did not investigate them. Unlike the issues over which disagreement occurred, for they are not in clarity like those principles; rather most of them are among what is mentioned in the Book and the Sunnah that the false claimant imagines as contradicting what the truthful one uses as proof therein, and each of them claims that the interpretation consistent with his school is more appropriate; so they cannot be made among what the validity of Islam depends on. Thus it is not permissible to proceed to excommunication, for there is great danger in it. To clarify the truth in the details when they are considered, even if they are among what there is no disagreement about excommunicating the one who opposes in them, like the temporal origination of the world — how many a believer does not know the meaning of 'temporal' and 'pre-eternal' at all, and has never occurred to his mind the talk of resurrection of bodies, definitely; but if he considers that and then does not believe, he would be a disbeliever. And the answer: We are certain that among them are those who stood upon the details, even if not all of them are like that. His statement: 'The people of summary' meaning those who are generalists, for they are not capable of refining the proof.
Source pages p. 370

Commentary
This passage is an objection-and-answer (فإن قيل... قلنا) followed by an argument from Imam al-Razi to support the author's position. The objection is that the Prophet (ﷺ) did not inquire into the detailed doctrinal issues because he knew that the people had general knowledge of them, just as he did not inquire about their belief in God's knowledge and power because that was known through general belief. The answer is that this is stubborn (مكابرة), because it is obvious that the Bedouins who came to the Prophet were not all knowledgeable about such details as God being knowing by knowledge not by essence, or being visible, or not being a body, etc. The difference is that belief in God's knowledge and power is necessary for recognizing prophethood because miracles depend on them; therefore, general belief in the Prophet implies belief in those attributes. But the detailed issues are not necessary for the validity of Islam. The passage then quotes Imam al-Razi, who illustrates that the proofs for the foundational principles of Islam (such as the existence of a Creator and the truthfulness of the messenger) are clear even to common people, using the example of seeing a garden with consistent conditions and a bunch of grapes with only one black grape, which compels the observer to know that the maker is a free agent. Since these foundations are clear, the Prophet did not need to investigate them. In contrast, the disputed issues are not as clear and involve conflicting interpretations, so they cannot be made prerequisites for Islam, and takfir should be avoided due to its great danger. The author then adds a final remark: even for clear essentials like the temporal origination of the world, many believers may not have considered them, but if they do and deny, they become disbelievers, but we know that some of the Prophet's audience did know the details even if not all. The comment on 'أرباب الجمل' (people of summary) concludes that such people cannot refine a proof.

Key Terms
إجمال
تفصيل
دلالة المعجزة
فاعل مختار
أصول الإسلام

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The Prophet's silence on detailed theological issues means he tacitly approved of all later opinions and that all views are equally valid.
Correction: The Prophet did not discuss details because foundational beliefs are clear and sufficient; his silence does not indicate approval of all interpretations, and disputed issues are not grounds for excommunication.

Checkpoint
Why does the author reject the argument that the Prophet remained silent on detailed issues because he knew people already had general knowledge of them?
A) Because the Prophet never kept silent on any issue
B) Because it is impossible that all Bedouins had such detailed knowledge
C) Because the Companions later clarified the issues
D) Because general knowledge suffices and details are unnecessary

Key Takeaway
The Prophet's silence on detailed issues does not mean he endorsed all views; foundational beliefs are clear and sufficient for Islam, while disputed details are not grounds for takfir.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you encounter a Muslim who does not know the theological details you hold, does your heart feel superior or grateful that Allah has guided them to the foundation of faith? The Bedouins believed simply—would you consider their faith less than yours because they lacked detail?
?
Have you ever dismissed someone's Islam because they disagreed with you on a detail of theology, forgetting the Prophet's silence on such matters? How would you feel if Allah judged you for a detail you never heard?
?
What if, on the Day of Judgment, you are asked about the heart of your faith—not your opinions on disputed issues—will you be able to say you truly believed in the clear fundamentals? Or have you let pride in your knowledge distract you from the simple, life-changing reality of tawheed?

The First Refutation: Who Are the Great Companions?
Sanusi presents the objection and the first refutation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the first response to the objection that takfir of some Companions implies the excommunicator is a kafir.

Core Idea
If the excommunicated are not seen as great Companions, no takfir follows.

The objector claims that since each sect excommunicates some great Companions, they themselves become kafir. The author immediately points out a flaw: those doing the excommunicating do not recognize their targets as great Companions at all, so the charge does not apply.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the translation. Notice the structure: the objection is stated, then the author's response.
The passage opens with 'The third observation - the fifth aim'.
2
Identify the objection and response
Find where the objector's claim is presented and where the author counters. The response is: the excommunicators do not acknowledge the targets as great Companions.
The phrase 'لا يسلمون كونهم من أكابر الصحابة' is key.
3
Reflect on the logic
Think: if the objectors do not consider a person a great Companion, then by their own standard, excommunicating that person does not violate the principle of not excommunicating great Companions.
This is a dialectical move: turn the opponent's premise against them.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المرصد الثالث - المقصد الخامس : الاتفاق على أنه لا يكفر أحد من أهل القبلة عظماء الصحابة وكل واحد من الفريقين يكفر بعض هؤلاء العظماء فيكون كافراً، قلنا : هؤلاء ) أي من كفر جماعة مخصوصة من الصحابة ) لا يسلمون كونهم من أكابر الصحابة وعظمائهم فلا يلزم كفره الثالث : قوله عليه السلام «من قال لأخيه المسلم يا كافر فقد باء به (( أي بالكفر :( أحدهما قلنا آحاد ) وقد أجمعت الأمة على أن إنكار الآحاد ليس كفراً ، ( و) مع ذلك نقول : ( المراد مع اعتقاد أنه مسلم فإن من ظن بمسلم أنه يهودي أو نصراني فقال له : يا كافر لم يكن ذلك كفراً بالإجماع)، واعلم أن عدم تكفير أهل القبلة موافق لكلام الشيخ الأشعري والفقهاء كما مر، لكنا إذا فتشنا عقائد فرق الإسلاميين وجدنا فيها ما يوجب الكفر قطعاً كالعقائد الرجعية إلى وجود إله غير الله سبحانه وتعالى أو إلى حلوله في بعض أشخاص الناس، أو إلى إنكار نبوة محمد عليه السلام أو إلى ذمه واستخفافه أو إلى استباحة المحرمات وإسقاط الواجبات الشرعية وإليه الإشارة بقوله : ( وسنزيد لهذا الذي ذكرناه في المقصد الخامس ( تحقيقاً إذا فصلنا الفرق الإسلامية وبينا عقائدهم (في ذيل هذا الكتاب ) . والله الموفق لتحقيق الحق
The third observation - the fifth aim: The agreement that none of the great Companions of the Ahl al-Qibla (people of the qibla) is excommunicated, and each of the two groups excommunicates some of these great ones, so that one would be an excommunicator. We said: These (i.e., those who excommunicate a specific group of Companions) do not acknowledge that they are from the great and mighty Companions, so his excommunication does not follow. Third: His saying, peace be upon him: 'Whoever says to his Muslim brother 'O kafir', it returns upon him' – i.e., with kufr (unbelief). We said: First: It is ahad (solitary hadith), and the community has agreed that rejection of solitary reports is not kufr. And with that we say: The intended meaning is with the belief that he is a Muslim, for if someone supposes a Muslim to be a Jew or Christian and says to him 'O kafir', that is not kufr by consensus. Know that the non-excommunication of Ahl al-Qibla agrees with the statement of Shaykh al-Ash'ari and the jurists as passed. However, when we investigate the beliefs of the Islamic sects, we find in them what necessarily necessitates kufr, such as beliefs returning to the existence of a god other than Allah, Most High, or to His indwelling in some persons, or to denial of the prophethood of Muhammad, peace be upon him, or to reviling and belittling him, or to permitting forbidden things and dropping obligatory religious duties. And to this is indicated by his saying: 'And we will add to this which we mentioned in the fifth aim a verification when we detail the Islamic sects and explain their beliefs in the appendix of this book.' And Allah is the Grantor of success to realization of truth.
Source pages p. 375

Commentary
This passage refutes the objection that since each group (Sunni and Shia) excommunicates some major Companions, they themselves become kafir. The author provides two responses. First, those who excommunicate specific Companions do not consider them major Companions, so they are not guilty of excommunicating a Muslim. Second, the opponent cites the hadith 'whoever says to his brother 'O kafir' it returns upon him'. The author replies: this hadith is ahad (solitary), and the community agrees that rejecting ahad reports is not kufr. Furthermore, the hadith is understood to apply when the speaker believes the person is Muslim but calls him kafir out of malice; here, the speaker genuinely considers the person a kafir based on their actions, so the hadith does not apply. The passage concludes by stating that the position of not excommunicating Ahl al-Qibla agrees with Ash'ari and the jurists, but warns that there are beliefs (such as polytheism, incarnationism, denial of prophethood, etc.) that clearly necessitate kufr, and these will be detailed later.

Key Terms
تكفير
صحابة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Any excommunication of a Companion automatically makes the excommunicator a kafir.
Correction: The excommunication only matters if the person acknowledges the target as a great Companion; the objectors here do not.

Checkpoint
What is the first reason the author gives for why excommunicators of specific Companions are not themselves kafir?
A) The hadith about 'brother' is not authentic.
B) The excommunicators do not accept that the targeted Companions are among the great ones.
C) The agreement only applies to the first three caliphs.
D) The excommunicators are protected by consensus.

Key Takeaway
The takfir argument fails because the objectors do not concede that their targets are great Companions.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever judged someone's faith based on their view of a historical figure you revere? How does this lesson's insistence on acknowledging that others may not share your classification of 'great' challenge you to reconsider your own judgments?
?
The refutation works by entering the opponent's framework. In your own disagreements, do you first try to understand the other's premises before condemning? What would it cost you to adopt that humility?
?
This passage deals with the gravity of takfir. When have you been quick to mentally 'excommunicate' someone from your heart over a disagreement? What would it take for you to leave that judgment to Allah and instead assume they have a valid reason for their position?
