Refuting the Hadith Argument Against Takfir
Sanusi refutes the objection based on the hadith.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand two reasons why the hadith 'Whoever says to his brother 'O kafir'' does not condemn those who excommunicate Companions.

Core Idea
The hadith is ahad and conditioned on belief of the target's Islam.

The objector cites a hadith threatening whoever calls a Muslim 'kafir'. The author gives two decisive responses: the hadith is not mutawatir, so denying it is not kufr, and the hadith only applies when the speaker believes the person is Muslim but calls him kafir anyway.

Guided Path
1
Read the hadith and its context
Read the Arabic passage that presents the hadith and the author's response. Notice the structure: the hadith is quoted, then the author gives two responses.
The hadith is 'من قال لأخيه المسلم يا كافر فقد باء به'
2
Understand the first response: ahad
The author says the hadith is ahad (solitary transmission). The community agrees that rejecting ahad reports is not kufr. Therefore, even if the hadith were to apply, denying it would not be kufr.
This appeals to the principle that only mutawatir reports give certainty.
3
Understand the second response: condition
The hadith is intended for cases where the speaker believes the person is Muslim but calls him kafir (out of hostility). Here, the accuser genuinely believes the person is not a Muslim based on actions.
The phrase 'مع اعتقاد أنه مسلم' is key.
4
Reflect on the combined force
Both responses together show that the hadith does not support the objector's claim. The first weakens the authority of the hadith, the second restricts its application.
These are standard kalam refutations.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المرصد الثالث - المقصد الخامس : الاتفاق على أنه لا يكفر أحد من أهل القبلة عظماء الصحابة وكل واحد من الفريقين يكفر بعض هؤلاء العظماء فيكون كافراً، قلنا : هؤلاء ) أي من كفر جماعة مخصوصة من الصحابة ) لا يسلمون كونهم من أكابر الصحابة وعظمائهم فلا يلزم كفره الثالث : قوله عليه السلام «من قال لأخيه المسلم يا كافر فقد باء به (( أي بالكفر :( أحدهما قلنا آحاد ) وقد أجمعت الأمة على أن إنكار الآحاد ليس كفراً ، ( و) مع ذلك نقول : ( المراد مع اعتقاد أنه مسلم فإن من ظن بمسلم أنه يهودي أو نصراني فقال له : يا كافر لم يكن ذلك كفراً بالإجماع)، واعلم أن عدم تكفير أهل القبلة موافق لكلام الشيخ الأشعري والفقهاء كما مر، لكنا إذا فتشنا عقائد فرق الإسلاميين وجدنا فيها ما يوجب الكفر قطعاً كالعقائد الرجعية إلى وجود إله غير الله سبحانه وتعالى أو إلى حلوله في بعض أشخاص الناس، أو إلى إنكار نبوة محمد عليه السلام أو إلى ذمه واستخفافه أو إلى استباحة المحرمات وإسقاط الواجبات الشرعية وإليه الإشارة بقوله : ( وسنزيد لهذا الذي ذكرناه في المقصد الخامس ( تحقيقاً إذا فصلنا الفرق الإسلامية وبينا عقائدهم (في ذيل هذا الكتاب ) . والله الموفق لتحقيق الحق
The third observation - the fifth aim: The agreement that none of the great Companions of the Ahl al-Qibla (people of the qibla) is excommunicated, and each of the two groups excommunicates some of these great ones, so that one would be an excommunicator. We said: These (i.e., those who excommunicate a specific group of Companions) do not acknowledge that they are from the great and mighty Companions, so his excommunication does not follow. Third: His saying, peace be upon him: 'Whoever says to his Muslim brother 'O kafir', it returns upon him' – i.e., with kufr (unbelief). We said: First: It is ahad (solitary hadith), and the community has agreed that rejection of solitary reports is not kufr. And with that we say: The intended meaning is with the belief that he is a Muslim, for if someone supposes a Muslim to be a Jew or Christian and says to him 'O kafir', that is not kufr by consensus. Know that the non-excommunication of Ahl al-Qibla agrees with the statement of Shaykh al-Ash'ari and the jurists as passed. However, when we investigate the beliefs of the Islamic sects, we find in them what necessarily necessitates kufr, such as beliefs returning to the existence of a god other than Allah, Most High, or to His indwelling in some persons, or to denial of the prophethood of Muhammad, peace be upon him, or to reviling and belittling him, or to permitting forbidden things and dropping obligatory religious duties. And to this is indicated by his saying: 'And we will add to this which we mentioned in the fifth aim a verification when we detail the Islamic sects and explain their beliefs in the appendix of this book.' And Allah is the Grantor of success to realization of truth.
Source pages p. 375

Commentary
This passage refutes the objection that since each group (Sunni and Shia) excommunicates some major Companions, they themselves become kafir. The author provides two responses. First, those who excommunicate specific Companions do not consider them major Companions, so they are not guilty of excommunicating a Muslim. Second, the opponent cites the hadith 'whoever says to his brother 'O kafir' it returns upon him'. The author replies: this hadith is ahad (solitary), and the community agrees that rejecting ahad reports is not kufr. Furthermore, the hadith is understood to apply when the speaker believes the person is Muslim but calls him kafir out of malice; here, the speaker genuinely considers the person a kafir based on their actions, so the hadith does not apply. The passage concludes by stating that the position of not excommunicating Ahl al-Qibla agrees with Ash'ari and the jurists, but warns that there are beliefs (such as polytheism, incarnationism, denial of prophethood, etc.) that clearly necessitate kufr, and these will be detailed later.

Key Terms
آحاد
إجماع
كفر

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The hadith applies unconditionally to anyone who calls a Muslim a kafir.
Correction: The hadith is conditioned on the speaker believing the person is Muslim; if the speaker genuinely thinks the person is not Muslim, the hadith does not apply.

Checkpoint
Why does the author say the hadith's ahad status is relevant?
A) Because rejecting ahad reports is permissible in all cases.
B) Because the community agreed that rejecting ahad reports is not kufr.
C) Because ahad reports cannot be used in legal rulings.
D) Because the hadith is fabricated.

Key Takeaway
The hadith does not support the objection due to its weak transmission and conditional meaning.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever used a hadith to justify a harsh judgment against someone without verifying its authenticity or proper interpretation? What does this lesson reveal about the weight of such a statement?
?
When you hear someone accuse another of disbelief, do you reflexively accept it or pause to ask: 'Does this person genuinely believe the target is a kafir based on sound evidence?'
?
If you were to call someone a kafir tomorrow, would you be able to defend it with the same precision Sanusi uses here, or would you be relying on emotion or general knowledge?

Beliefs That Necessitate Excommunication
Sanusi lists beliefs that clearly necessitate excommunication.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that while Ahl al-Qibla are not excommunicated as a rule, certain beliefs clearly necessitate kufr, and the author promises to detail these later.

Core Idea
Some beliefs, like worshipping another god, clearly necessitate excommunication.

The author concludes by affirming the general principle of not excommunicating Ahl al-Qibla, but warns that there are extreme beliefs that undeniably constitute kufr. He promises to detail these when discussing Islamic sects in the appendix.

Guided Path
1
Read the concluding passage
Read the Arabic passage that starts with 'واعلم أن عدم تكفير أهل القبلة...'. This is a transition to the next section.
Notice the shift: from refutation to a broader principle and preview.
2
Identify the beliefs listed
The author lists several beliefs that necessitate kufr: belief in another god, incarnation, denial of prophethood, reviling the Prophet, permitting the forbidden, and dropping obligations.
These are clear examples of apostasy.
3
Connect to future lessons
The author promises to add verification when detailing Islamic sects. This shows that the upcoming sections will cover these deviant beliefs.
The phrase 'في ذيل هذا الكتاب' indicates an appendix.

Ijtihad as the Standard for Imamate
Refutes the claim that Abu Bakr lacked knowledge of all rulings by citing his ijtihad.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that the qualification for the imamate is ijtihad, not prefect knowledge of all rulings, and that Abu Bakr's actions are justified through ijtihad.

Core Idea
The imam's knowledge requirement is ijtihad, not instant mastery.

In this passage, the author responds to a key objection against Abu Bakr's caliphate: that he lacked comprehensive knowledge of all rulings. The response establishes a crucial principle—the imam must be a mujtahid, not omniscient. Let's examine how this defense works through specific examples.

Guided Path
1
Understand the core principle
Read the opening statement where the author denies the principle that an imam must know all rulings without need for reflection. Instead, the obligation is ijtihad.
This is the foundation of the entire response.
2
Examine the examples
Look at the three examples: burning of Fuja'a, cutting off the left hand, and the grandmother's inheritance. Notice how each is explained as a valid ijtihad or mistake.
These concrete cases show how Abu Bakr's actions fit the ijtihad model.
3
Synthesize the defense
Reflect on how the examples collectively prove that Abu Bakr was a mujtahid and that the objection is unfounded.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والأصل) وهو كون الإمام عالماً الأحكام ( ممنوع . وإنما الواجب الاجتهاد ولا يقتضي كون جميع الأحكام عتيدة ) أي حاضرة ( عنده ) بحيث لا يحتاج المجتهد إلى نظر وتأمل ( وأنه ( أي أبا بكر ( مجتهد إذ ما من مسألة في الغالب إلا وله فيها قول مشهور عند أهل العلم وإحراق فجاءة ) إنما كان لاجتهاده وعدم قبول توبته لأنه زنديق ولا تقبل توبة الزنديق في الأصح وأما قطع اليسار فلعله من غلط الجلاد أو رآه في المرة ( الثالثة : ( من السرقة ( وهو رأي الأكثر من العلماء، ( ووقوفه في مسألة الجدة ورجوعه إلى الصحابة ) في ذلك ( لأنه غير بدع من المجتهد البحث عن مدارك الأحكام
And the principle (al-aṣl) — that the imam must be knowledgeable of the rulings — is denied. Rather, the obligation is ijtihad, and it does not require that all rulings be present (ʿatīda) before him so that the mujtahid would need no consideration and reflection. And that he (i.e., Abu Bakr) was a mujtahid, for there is hardly any issue except that he has a well-known opinion among the people of knowledge. And the burning of Fuja'a was only due to his ijtihad and his decision not to accept his repentance because he was a zindiq, and the repentance of a zindiq is not accepted according to the soundest opinion. As for cutting off the left hand, perhaps it was a mistake by the executioner, or he saw it as the third time (of theft), which is the view of most scholars. And his pausing on the issue of the grandmother and returning to the Companions regarding that is because it is not unusual for a mujtahid to seek the sources of rulings.
Source pages p. 389

Commentary
This passage responds to the objection that Abu Bakr was not fit for the imamate because he lacked knowledge of all rulings. The author first denies the premise that an imam must have all rulings present in his mind without need for reflection. Instead, the obligation is ijtihad — the ability to derive rulings from evidence. Abu Bakr was a mujtahid, evidenced by the fact that for nearly every issue he has a well-known opinion among scholars. The author then addresses three examples cited against him: (1) The burning of Fuja'a: this was an ijtihadic decision because Fuja'a was a zindiq (heretic), and the sound opinion is that a zindiq's repentance is not accepted. (2) Cutting off the left hand (instead of the right for theft): this was either a mistake by the executioner or was done on the third theft, which is a valid view of some scholars. (3) His hesitation on the inheritance of the grandmother and returning to the Companions: this is not a flaw, as it is the method of a mujtahid to research and consult. Thus, the objection fails.

Key Terms
أصل
اجتهاد
محتاج
زنديق
جلاد

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: An imam must know every ruling instantly without needing to reflect or consult others.
Correction: The imam is only required to be a mujtahid; he can engage in ijtihad, research, and consultation.

Checkpoint
What is the author's main response to the objection that Abu Bakr lacked knowledge of all rulings?
A) He knew all rulings by heart.
B) The imam is only required to have ijtihad, not perfect knowledge.
C) The objection is irrelevant because imams do not need knowledge.
D) Abu Bakr was not a mujtahid.

Key Takeaway
Abu Bakr's qualification for the imamate is proven by his ijtihad, not by prefect knowledge, and the examples given by critics are actually consistent with ijtihad.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you trust that the Companions, like Abu Bakr, were capable of ijtihad, or do you harbor doubts that the first Caliph was truly qualified? Reflect on whether your heart accepts their authority fully, and ask Allah to remove any lingering skepticism.
?
When you face a complex Islamic ruling, do you demand instant answers or do you humbly seek knowledge through ijtihad? What does this say about your approach to faith? Commit today to trusting the process of scholarly derivation.
?
Consider a time you judged a leader based on a single mistake. How does this lesson's treatment of Abu Bakr's actions challenge your inclination to dismiss qualified people based on isolated incidents? Repent of hasty judgments and resolve to seek the full picture.

Umar's Criticism of Abu Bakr: The al-Hutay'a Incident
Umar's comment about Abu Bakr regarding al-Hutay'a.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the first instance of alleged criticism by Umar of Abu Bakr and how it is interpreted in the context of ijtihad and personal judgment.

Core Idea
Umar's criticism of Abu Bakr was a matter of ijtihad, not enmity.

The fourth objection to Abu Bakr's caliphate is that Umar himself criticized him. The first example is Umar's harsh words about Abu Bakr when the poet al-Hutay'a was brought before him. This lesson examines that incident and sets the stage for understanding that such statements are not considered actual disparagement but rather differences in judgment.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage
Read the Arabic text which presents Umar's comment and the context of al-Hutay'a's intercession.
2
Understand the context
Note that 'Abd al-Rahman ibn Abi Bakr interceded for the poet al-Hutay'a, and Umar's response was to insult both the poet and Abu Bakr.
3
Recognize the objection
This is presented as a criticism of Abu Bakr's judgment in handling the situation.
4
Consider the response
Think about how the author will later argue that this is not real blame but a difference of ijtihad.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الرابع : ( من الوجوه النافية لصلوحه للإمامة (عمر مع أنه حميمه وناصره وله العهد ) ، أي : عهد الإمامة ( من قبله قد ذمه حيث شفع إليه عبد الرحمن بن أبي بكر الحطيئة ) الشاعر ) فقال : دويبة سوء وهو خير من أبيه وأنكر عمر عليه ) أي على أبي بكر ( عدم قتل خالد بن الوليد حيث قتل مالك بن نويرة ) وهو مسلم طمعاً في امرأته لجمالها . (و) لذلك ) تزوج بزوجته ( من ليلته وضاجعها فأشار عليه عمر بقتله قصاصاً ، فقال أبو بكر : لا أغمد سيفاً شهره الله على الكفار، ( وقال : ( عمر مخاطباً لخالد لأن وليت الأمر لأقيدنك به وقال : ( عمر في ذمه أيضا (إن بيعة أبي بكر ( كانت فلتة وقى الله شرها فمن عاد مثلها فاقتلوه قلنا : نسبة الذم إليه من الباردة فإن عمر مع كمال عقله ووفور حزمه حتى قيل: في في حقه هو أعقل من أن يخدع وأورع من أن يخدع ، (و) قد ( كانت إمامته بعهد أبي بكر إليه والقدح في أبي بكر قدح في إمامته كيف يتصور منه ذلك وإنكاره عدم قتل خالد ) أي : عدم قتله ) من إنكار المجتهدين بعضهم على بعض فيما أدى إليه اجتهادهم) . فإنه نقل أن خالداً إنما قتل مالكاً لأنه ارتد ورد على قومه صدقاتهم لما بلغه وفاة رسول الله وخاطب خالداً بأنه مات صاحبك فعلم خالد قصده أنه ليس صاحباً له فتيقن ردته، وأما تزوجه امرأته فلعلها كانت مطلقة قد انقضت عدتها إلا أنها كانت محبوسة عنده
The fourth (of the objections negating his suitability for the imamate): Umar, despite being his intimate friend and supporter, and having the covenant (i.e., the covenant of the imamate) from him, criticized him. For when 'Abd al-Rahman ibn Abi Bakr interceded for al-Hutay'a (the poet), he said: 'A vile little creature, and he is better than his father.' And Umar objected to him (i.e., to Abu Bakr) for not killing Khalid ibn al-Walid when he killed Malik ibn Nuwayra — who was a Muslim — out of desire for his wife due to her beauty. Therefore, he married his wife on the same night and consummated the marriage. 'Umar advised him to kill him as retribution, but Abu Bakr said: 'I will not sheathe a sword that Allah has drawn against the disbelievers.' And he (Umar) said, addressing Khalid: 'If I were given authority, I would surely demand retribution from you.' And 'Umar also said in criticism of him: 'The pledge to Abu Bakr was a sudden affair (falta), Allah protected from its evil; whoever repeats such an act, kill him.' We say: Attributing criticism to him (Umar) is cold (baseless). For 'Umar, with his perfect intellect and abundant caution — it was even said about him: 'He is too intelligent to be deceived, and too pious to be deceived' — and his imamate was by the covenant of Abu Bakr to him; thus, disparaging Abu Bakr would be disparaging his own imamate. How can one imagine that from him? And his objection to not killing Khalid is (merely) the objection of one mujtahid to another regarding what their ijtihad led to. For it is narrated that Khalid only killed Malik because he had apostatized and returned the alms to his people when news of the Prophet's death reached him, and he said to Khalid: 'Your companion has died.' So Khalid understood that he meant he was not a companion to him, so he was certain of his apostasy. As for his marrying his wife, perhaps she was divorced and her waiting period had ended, but she was held captive by him.
Source pages p. 389

Commentary
This passage addresses the fourth objection to Abu Bakr's suitability: that Umar himself criticized him. The author lists three instances: (1) Umar said of Abu Bakr regarding the poet al-Hutay'a: 'A vile little creature, and he is better than his father.' (2) Umar objected to Abu Bakr not executing Khalid ibn al-Walid for killing Malik ibn Nuwayra and marrying his wife immediately. Abu Bakr refused, saying he would not sheathe a sword drawn against disbelievers. Umar threatened Khalid. (3) Umar said the pledge to Abu Bakr was a 'falta' (sudden, unconsidered act). The author responds that attributing actual criticism to Umar is baseless: Umar was too intelligent and pious to disparage the one who appointed him; doing so would undermine his own imamate. The objection about Khalid is just a difference of ijtihad among scholars. The author then explains that Khalid killed Malik because he was convinced of Malik's apostasy: Malik had said 'Your companion has died,' implying he no longer considered Muhammad as his companion. As for marrying Malik's wife, it is possible she was already divorced and had completed her waiting period.

Key Terms
ذم
شفاعة
دويبة سوء

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Umar's words show he truly thought ill of Abu Bakr.
Correction: In Sunni theology, these words are understood as a momentary expression of ijtihad, not a permanent judgment of Abu Bakr's worth.

Checkpoint
What did Umar say about Abu Bakr regarding the poet al-Hutay'a?
A) 'He is a good leader.'
B) 'A vile little creature, and he is better than his father.'
C) 'He is the best of the ummah.'
D) 'He is unfit for leadership.'

Key Takeaway
Umar's remark about Abu Bakr is cited as an objection but is later explained as a matter of ijtihad.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When someone you deeply respect says something harsh about another person you admire, do you immediately assume the worst of them, or do you pause to consider the context and possibility of ijtihad? How can you train yourself to do the latter?
?
Think of a recent time you criticized a close friend or family member. Was your criticism rooted in genuine concern or a misunderstanding that felt justified in the moment? How does the example of Umar—who was an intimate friend yet still criticized—challenge you to examine your own motives and the weight of your words?
?
If you were in ‘Abd al-Rahman’s position, interceding for someone, and a respected leader responded harshly, would you be able to see beyond the words to the underlying intent? What would it take for you to maintain unity and trust in the face of such criticism?

The Khalid ibn al-Walid Incident: Ijtihad Disagreement
Umar's objection to not punishing Khalid ibn al-Walid.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the second objection concerning Khalid ibn al-Walid and how the author explains it as a difference of ijtihad among companions.

Core Idea
Umar's objection to not executing Khalid was a valid ijtihad, not a criticism of Abu Bakr's judgment.

The second example of Umar's alleged criticism is his objection to Abu Bakr's decision not to punish Khalid ibn al-Walid for killing Malik ibn Nuwayra and marrying his wife. This lesson presents the incident and the author's response that it was a legitimate difference of ijtihad between two mujtahids.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic describing Umar's objection and Abu Bakr's response.
2
Understand the details
Note the sequence: Khalid killed Malik, married his wife, Umar advised retribution, Abu Bakr refused.
3
Identify the author's explanation
The author says this is merely the objection of one mujtahid to another.
4
Reflect on the justification
Consider the narration that Malik had apostatized, which justified Khalid's action.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الرابع : ( من الوجوه النافية لصلوحه للإمامة (عمر مع أنه حميمه وناصره وله العهد ) ، أي : عهد الإمامة ( من قبله قد ذمه حيث شفع إليه عبد الرحمن بن أبي بكر الحطيئة ) الشاعر ) فقال : دويبة سوء وهو خير من أبيه وأنكر عمر عليه ) أي على أبي بكر ( عدم قتل خالد بن الوليد حيث قتل مالك بن نويرة ) وهو مسلم طمعاً في امرأته لجمالها . (و) لذلك ) تزوج بزوجته ( من ليلته وضاجعها فأشار عليه عمر بقتله قصاصاً ، فقال أبو بكر : لا أغمد سيفاً شهره الله على الكفار، ( وقال : ( عمر مخاطباً لخالد لأن وليت الأمر لأقيدنك به وقال : ( عمر في ذمه أيضا (إن بيعة أبي بكر ( كانت فلتة وقى الله شرها فمن عاد مثلها فاقتلوه قلنا : نسبة الذم إليه من الباردة فإن عمر مع كمال عقله ووفور حزمه حتى قيل: في في حقه هو أعقل من أن يخدع وأورع من أن يخدع ، (و) قد ( كانت إمامته بعهد أبي بكر إليه والقدح في أبي بكر قدح في إمامته كيف يتصور منه ذلك وإنكاره عدم قتل خالد ) أي : عدم قتله ) من إنكار المجتهدين بعضهم على بعض فيما أدى إليه اجتهادهم) . فإنه نقل أن خالداً إنما قتل مالكاً لأنه ارتد ورد على قومه صدقاتهم لما بلغه وفاة رسول الله وخاطب خالداً بأنه مات صاحبك فعلم خالد قصده أنه ليس صاحباً له فتيقن ردته، وأما تزوجه امرأته فلعلها كانت مطلقة قد انقضت عدتها إلا أنها كانت محبوسة عنده
The fourth (of the objections negating his suitability for the imamate): Umar, despite being his intimate friend and supporter, and having the covenant (i.e., the covenant of the imamate) from him, criticized him. For when 'Abd al-Rahman ibn Abi Bakr interceded for al-Hutay'a (the poet), he said: 'A vile little creature, and he is better than his father.' And Umar objected to him (i.e., to Abu Bakr) for not killing Khalid ibn al-Walid when he killed Malik ibn Nuwayra — who was a Muslim — out of desire for his wife due to her beauty. Therefore, he married his wife on the same night and consummated the marriage. 'Umar advised him to kill him as retribution, but Abu Bakr said: 'I will not sheathe a sword that Allah has drawn against the disbelievers.' And he (Umar) said, addressing Khalid: 'If I were given authority, I would surely demand retribution from you.' And 'Umar also said in criticism of him: 'The pledge to Abu Bakr was a sudden affair (falta), Allah protected from its evil; whoever repeats such an act, kill him.' We say: Attributing criticism to him (Umar) is cold (baseless). For 'Umar, with his perfect intellect and abundant caution — it was even said about him: 'He is too intelligent to be deceived, and too pious to be deceived' — and his imamate was by the covenant of Abu Bakr to him; thus, disparaging Abu Bakr would be disparaging his own imamate. How can one imagine that from him? And his objection to not killing Khalid is (merely) the objection of one mujtahid to another regarding what their ijtihad led to. For it is narrated that Khalid only killed Malik because he had apostatized and returned the alms to his people when news of the Prophet's death reached him, and he said to Khalid: 'Your companion has died.' So Khalid understood that he meant he was not a companion to him, so he was certain of his apostasy. As for his marrying his wife, perhaps she was divorced and her waiting period had ended, but she was held captive by him.
Source pages p. 389

Commentary
This passage addresses the fourth objection to Abu Bakr's suitability: that Umar himself criticized him. The author lists three instances: (1) Umar said of Abu Bakr regarding the poet al-Hutay'a: 'A vile little creature, and he is better than his father.' (2) Umar objected to Abu Bakr not executing Khalid ibn al-Walid for killing Malik ibn Nuwayra and marrying his wife immediately. Abu Bakr refused, saying he would not sheathe a sword drawn against disbelievers. Umar threatened Khalid. (3) Umar said the pledge to Abu Bakr was a 'falta' (sudden, unconsidered act). The author responds that attributing actual criticism to Umar is baseless: Umar was too intelligent and pious to disparage the one who appointed him; doing so would undermine his own imamate. The objection about Khalid is just a difference of ijtihad among scholars. The author then explains that Khalid killed Malik because he was convinced of Malik's apostasy: Malik had said 'Your companion has died,' implying he no longer considered Muhammad as his companion. As for marrying Malik's wife, it is possible she was already divorced and had completed her waiting period.

Key Terms
قصاص
اجتهاد
ردة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Umar's demand for qisas means he considered Abu Bakr's decision wrong.
Correction: In ijtihad, both opinions are valid; Umar's demand does not imply Abu Bakr was in error.

Checkpoint
Why did Abu Bakr refuse to kill Khalid according to the passage?
A) He feared Umar's anger.
B) He said Khalid's sword was drawn against disbelievers.
C) He believed Khalid was innocent.
D) He wanted to punish him later.

Key Takeaway
Umar's objection to not executing Khalid is explained as a legitimate difference of ijtihad, not a criticism of Abu Bakr.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you hear of a disagreement among respected scholars, do you tend to pick sides or seek the underlying wisdom? How can you apply the lesson of Umar and Abu Bakr's ijtihad to resolve a conflict you are facing right now?
?
Are there people you have judged harshly because you misunderstood their intent? This lesson shows Umar's objection was ijtihad, not rebellion. Who in your life might you be misinterpreting, and what would it take to give them the benefit of the doubt today?
?
The author defends Khalid's actions based on the possibility that Malik had apostatized. How often do you assume the worst about others' motives? What would change in your heart if you actively sought the best interpretation of their actions, especially those you disagree with?

The 'Falta' Statement and Defense of Umar
Umar's 'falta' statement and the refutation of criticism.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Umar's statement about the pledge to Abu Bakr being a 'falta' and the comprehensive defense that Umar's words cannot be taken as criticism.

Core Idea
Umar's 'falta' remark is explained as a factual description, not a condemnation, and his integrity precludes true criticism of Abu Bakr.

The third example of criticism is Umar's famous statement that the pledge to Abu Bakr was a 'falta' (sudden affair) from which Allah protected the community. The author mounts a strong defense, arguing that Umar's character and his own appointment by Abu Bakr make it impossible that he truly criticized him. This lesson examines that defense.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic containing Umar's 'falta' statement and the author's refutation.
2
Understand the 'falta' statement
Umar said the pledge was a sudden affair, but Allah protected from its evil.
3
Analyze the defense
The author argues that Umar was too intelligent and pious to criticize the one who appointed him.
4
See the conclusion
Thus, all alleged criticisms are either ijtihad differences or misinterpretations.

Understanding Umar's 'Falta' Saying
Explains Umar's 'falta' saying and defends consensus on Abu Bakr's imamate.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the true meaning of Umar's statement about the pledge to Abu Bakr being a 'falta' and how it does not undermine the consensus on his imamate.

Core Idea
Umar's 'falta' was a warning against haste, not a rejection of Abu Bakr's caliphate.

In this passage, the author addresses a famous saying of Umar ibn al-Khattab, who described the pledge to Abu Bakr as a 'falta' (a sudden act). Some have misinterpreted this as a criticism of Abu Bakr's legitimacy. The author clarifies Umar's intent and shows that the consensus on Abu Bakr's imamate remains unshaken by such objections.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and its translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then the literal translation. Notice the author's careful explanation of Umar's words.
Pay attention to the word 'falta' and its context.
2
Identify the author's interpretation of 'falta'
The author says that 'falta' means proceeding without consultation, which could lead to discord. Understand why Umar warned against repeating it.
3
See how the author refutes objections
The author states that objections about Abu Bakr's suitability do not contradict the consensus (ijma') on his imamate. Consider why consensus is a strong proof.
Consensus is a key concept in Sunni political theology.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
( وأما قوله في بيعة أبي بكر فمعناه أن الإقدام على مثله بلا مشاورة الغير وتحصيل الاتفاق منه مظنة للفتنة العظيمة ( فلا يقدمن عليه أحد على أني أقدمت عليه فسلمت وتيسر الأمر بلا تبعة ثم إنك خبير بأن أمثال هذه الوجوه التي تمسكوا بها على انتفاء صلاحيته للإمامة ( لا تعارض الإجماع على إمامته المستلزم للإجماع على أهليته للإمامة .
As for his statement regarding the pledge to Abu Bakr, its meaning is that proceeding to such an act without consulting others and obtaining agreement from them is a cause for great discord. Therefore, no one should proceed to such an act, whereas I proceeded to it and was saved, and the matter became easy without blame. Then, you are indeed aware that such objections that they have clung to for negating his suitability for the imamate do not contradict the consensus on his imamate, which necessitates consensus on his qualification for the imamate.
Source pages p. 390

Commentary
This passage clarifies Umar's famous remark: 'The pledge to Abu Bakr was a falta (sudden act), Allah protected from its evil; whoever repeats such an act, kill him.' The author explains that Umar was not condemning the pledge itself, but warning against undertaking such an important action without prior consultation and agreement, because that could lead to great discord. Umar says that he himself engaged in that action (i.e., participated in the pledge) and it turned out well, but others should not imitate it without proper procedure. The author then concludes that all these objections raised by the Shi'a do not negate the consensus on Abu Bakr's imamate, which in turn proves his qualification.

Key Terms
بيعة
فلتة
مظنة
إجماع

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think 'falta' means the pledge was invalid, but Umar used it to warn against haste.
Correction: Umar's statement was a caution against repeating such haste without proper consultation, not a criticism of the pledge's validity.

Checkpoint
According to the author, what was Umar's purpose in calling the pledge a 'falta'?
A) To invalidate Abu Bakr's imamate.
B) To warn against acting without consultation in the future.
C) To express regret for his own participation.
D) To insult the Companions.

Key Takeaway
Umar's 'falta' saying is not a criticism of Abu Bakr's caliphate but a warning against hasty action; consensus on his imamate remains unaffected by objections.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?

The Shi'a Argument for an Explicit Text on Ali's Imamate
Shi'a claim of explicit text with two proofs.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Shi'a reasoning for claiming there was an explicit text appointing Ali as imam, based on the Prophet's practice and compassion.

Core Idea
The Shi'a argue: if the Prophet appointed deputies in life, he must have done so for his death.

This passage presents the fifth argument used by Shi'a theologians to prove Ali's imamate: the claim that the Prophet explicitly designated him. They present two reasons why such a text must have existed, even if we don't have its exact wording. Let's examine their reasoning carefully.

Guided Path
1
Read the Shi'a claim and their two proofs
Start by reading the passage that presents the general claim and the two reasons. Notice that they argue for the existence of an explicit text (nass jalī) even though its exact wording is unknown.
2
Understand the first proof: Prophet's custom
The first proof is based on the Prophet's habit of appointing a successor when he was absent from Medina during his life. They argue that leaving the entire ummah without a leader after his death is even more inconceivable.
3
Understand the second proof: Prophet's compassion
The second proof appeals to the Prophet's known compassion and care, even teaching the minutiae of daily life. So it would be unthinkable for him not to appoint a guide for the community's religious and worldly affairs.
4
See how they conclude it must be Ali
They note that there is no such text for Abu Bakr or al-‘Abbas, so by elimination, the text must concern Ali.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وخامسها ) أي : خامس الأمور التي عليها مدار كلامهم في إثبات إمامة علي ( ادعاء النص على إمامة علي إجمالاً وتفصيلاً. أما إجمالاً فقالوا :) نحن ( نعلم) قطعاً ويقيناً ( وجود نص جلي وإن لم يبلغنا بعينه لوجهين: الأول : إن عادة الرسول تقضي باستخلافه على الأمة عند غيبته عنهم) في حال حياته ( كما كان يستخلف على المدينة عند نهوضه للغزوات ولا يخل بذلك ألبتة ولا يترك أهل البلد فوضى ) أي : متساوين لا رئيس لهم، فكيف يجوز أن يخلي الأمة بأجمعها عند الغيبة الكبرى التي لا رجوع بعدها بلا إمام يقتدون به ويرجعون إليه في مصالحهم ؟! ( والثاني : شفقته على الأمة معلومة مكشوفة لا سترة بها حتى قال : إنما أنا لكم مثل الوالد لولده ) وعلمهم في أمر خسيس كقضاء الحاجة دقائق آدابه فكيف لا يعين لهم من يصلح حالهم به معاشاً ومعاداً ومن البين أنه لا نص في حق أبي بكر والعباس فتعين أن يكون في حق علي . ( والجواب : أنه لما علم النبي عليه السلام ) أن الصحابة يقومون بذلك التعيين ) ولا يخلون به لم يفعل ذلك لعدم الحاجة إليه). كما أنه عليه السلام لم ينص على كثير من الأحكام الشرعية، بل وكلها إلى آراء المجتهدين الذين هم حماة الدين وأعلام الشرع ( ثم عدم النص) الجلي (معلوم قطعاً لأنه لو وجد لتواتر ولم يكن ستره عادة)؛ إذ هو مما تتوفر الدواعي إلى نقله ( وأيضاً لو وجد نص جلي على إمامة علي لمنع به غيره عن الإمامة كما منع أبو بكر الأنصار بقوله عليه السلام : الأئمة من قريش» مع كونه خبر واحد فأطاعوه وتركوا الإمامة لأجله فكيف يتصور أن يوجد نص جلي متواتر في علي وهو بين قوم لا يعصون خبر الواحد في ترك الإمامة وشأنهم في الصلابة في الدين ما يشهد به بذلهم الأموال والأنفس ومهاجرتهم الأهل والوطن وقتلهم الأولاد والآباء والأقارب
And the fifth (of the matters on which their discourse revolves in proving the imamate of Ali) is the claim of a text (nass) on the imamate of Ali, both in general and in detail. As for the general claim, they said: We know with certainty and conviction that there exists an explicit text (nass jalī), even if its exact wording has not reached us, for two reasons: First, the custom of the Messenger was to appoint a successor over the community when he was absent from them during his life, just as he would appoint a successor over Medina when he set out for campaigns. He never neglected that at all, nor did he leave the people of the city without a leader. So how could it be permissible for him to leave the entire community at the greater absence after which there is no return without an imam to follow and refer to in their interests? Second, his compassion for the community is known and manifest, without concealment; he even said: 'I am to you like a father to his child.' And he taught them even the minutest etiquettes of a trivial matter like relieving oneself. So how could he not designate for them someone to set right their affairs in this world and the next? And it is clear that there is no text concerning Abu Bakr or al-‘Abbas, so it must be concerning Ali. The answer: When the Prophet (peace be upon him) knew that the Companions would undertake that appointment and would not neglect it, he did not do so due to the lack of need for it. Just as he did not explicitly rule on many legal rulings, but delegated them to the opinions of the mujtahids, who are the guardians of the religion and its symbols. Moreover, the absence of an explicit text is known with certainty because if it existed, it would have been transmitted as widely (tawātur) and its concealment would not be customary, since there are abundant motives to transmit it. Also, if there were an explicit text on the imamate of Ali, it would have prevented others from claiming the imamate, just as Abu Bakr prevented the Ansar with the Prophet's saying 'The imams are from Quraysh,' even though it was a solitary report (khabar wāḥid); they obeyed and abandoned the imamate for its sake. So how could it be imagined that an explicit text transmitted widely (mutawātir) exists concerning Ali when he was among a people who would not disobey even a solitary report concerning abandoning the imamate, and their firmness in religion is attested by their sacrificing wealth, lives, migration, and killing their children, fathers, and relatives?
Source pages p. 390

Commentary
This passage presents the fifth argument of the Shi'a for Ali's imamate: the claim of a text (nass) from the Prophet appointing him, either in general or specific terms. For the general claim, they adduce two proofs: (1) The Prophet's practice of appointing a deputy when absent from Medina during his life; since he would never leave a city without a leader, it is inconceivable that he would leave the entire ummah without a leader after his death. (2) The Prophet's compassion and detailed teaching show he would not neglect to designate someone to guide the community in religious and worldly affairs. Since there is no such text for Abu Bakr or al-‘Abbas, they argue it must be for Ali. The response has several parts: (A) The Prophet knew the Companions would appoint a leader themselves, so he did not need to designate one—just as he did not detail every legal ruling but left it to ijtihad. (B) The absence of an explicit text is known with certainty because if it existed, it would have been transmitted by tawātur (mass transmission); it is unthinkable that it would be hidden, given the strong motives for transmission. (C) If such a text existed, it would have prevented others from claiming leadership, just as the solitary report 'The imams are from Quraysh' sufficed to make the Ansar withdraw their claim. Thus, it is impossible that a widely transmitted text existed for Ali while the Companions, who were firm in religion, would have obeyed it.

Key Terms
نص
إمامة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Shi'a argument, what is the first reason for the existence of an explicit text for Ali?
A) The Prophet's compassion
B) The Prophet's custom of appointing successors
C) The lack of text for others
D) The transmission by tawātur

Key Takeaway
The Shi'a claim of an explicit text for Ali's imamate is based on an analogy from the Prophet's practice and his compassion, not on a known transmitted text.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you consider the Shi'a argument that the Prophet's compassion demands an explicit appointment, does your own heart feel a similar urgency to seek out and follow the divinely appointed guide? Or do you remain content with inherited positions, assuming that the matter has been settled without personal investigation?
?
The Shi'a claim that the Prophet taught even the etiquette of relieving oneself, yet they argue he would not leave the community without an imam. Reflect: in your own life, do you give more importance to minor rituals than to the fundamental question of who truly leads the ummah? What does that reveal about your priorities?

Refutation of the Claim of Explicit Text
Refutation with three arguments.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Sunni refutation of the Shi'a claim, including the arguments from the Prophet's knowledge, the lack of tawatur, and the historical evidence.

Core Idea
If an explicit text existed for Ali, it would have reached us through tawatur and would have prevented all disputes.

Having presented the Shi'a claim, the author now provides a comprehensive refutation. He gives three distinct arguments: the Prophet relied on the Companions' ijtihad, the absence of tawatur proves the text's non-existence, and historical evidence shows that a solitary report sufficed to change the course of leadership, so a widely transmitted text would have been even more effective.

Guided Path
1
Read the refutation passage carefully
Start by reading the full refutation. The author responds directly to the Shi'a reasoning, offering multiple counter-arguments.
2
First argument: Prophet's knowledge of the Companions
The author argues that the Prophet knew the Companions would themselves appoint a leader, so there was no need for a text. He also left many legal rulings to ijtihad.
3
Second argument: Absence of tawatur
If an explicit text existed, it would have been transmitted by tawatur (mass transmission). Its absence shows it never existed, as there were strong motives to transmit it.
4
Third argument: Historical precedent with the Ansar
The example of Abu Bakr using a solitary report ('The imams are from Quraysh') to stop the Ansar's claim shows that a widely transmitted text would have been even more effective in preventing disputes. Its absence is proof.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وخامسها ) أي : خامس الأمور التي عليها مدار كلامهم في إثبات إمامة علي ( ادعاء النص على إمامة علي إجمالاً وتفصيلاً. أما إجمالاً فقالوا :) نحن ( نعلم) قطعاً ويقيناً ( وجود نص جلي وإن لم يبلغنا بعينه لوجهين: الأول : إن عادة الرسول تقضي باستخلافه على الأمة عند غيبته عنهم) في حال حياته ( كما كان يستخلف على المدينة عند نهوضه للغزوات ولا يخل بذلك ألبتة ولا يترك أهل البلد فوضى ) أي : متساوين لا رئيس لهم، فكيف يجوز أن يخلي الأمة بأجمعها عند الغيبة الكبرى التي لا رجوع بعدها بلا إمام يقتدون به ويرجعون إليه في مصالحهم ؟! ( والثاني : شفقته على الأمة معلومة مكشوفة لا سترة بها حتى قال : إنما أنا لكم مثل الوالد لولده ) وعلمهم في أمر خسيس كقضاء الحاجة دقائق آدابه فكيف لا يعين لهم من يصلح حالهم به معاشاً ومعاداً ومن البين أنه لا نص في حق أبي بكر والعباس فتعين أن يكون في حق علي . ( والجواب : أنه لما علم النبي عليه السلام ) أن الصحابة يقومون بذلك التعيين ) ولا يخلون به لم يفعل ذلك لعدم الحاجة إليه). كما أنه عليه السلام لم ينص على كثير من الأحكام الشرعية، بل وكلها إلى آراء المجتهدين الذين هم حماة الدين وأعلام الشرع ( ثم عدم النص) الجلي (معلوم قطعاً لأنه لو وجد لتواتر ولم يكن ستره عادة)؛ إذ هو مما تتوفر الدواعي إلى نقله ( وأيضاً لو وجد نص جلي على إمامة علي لمنع به غيره عن الإمامة كما منع أبو بكر الأنصار بقوله عليه السلام : الأئمة من قريش» مع كونه خبر واحد فأطاعوه وتركوا الإمامة لأجله فكيف يتصور أن يوجد نص جلي متواتر في علي وهو بين قوم لا يعصون خبر الواحد في ترك الإمامة وشأنهم في الصلابة في الدين ما يشهد به بذلهم الأموال والأنفس ومهاجرتهم الأهل والوطن وقتلهم الأولاد والآباء والأقارب
And the fifth (of the matters on which their discourse revolves in proving the imamate of Ali) is the claim of a text (nass) on the imamate of Ali, both in general and in detail. As for the general claim, they said: We know with certainty and conviction that there exists an explicit text (nass jalī), even if its exact wording has not reached us, for two reasons: First, the custom of the Messenger was to appoint a successor over the community when he was absent from them during his life, just as he would appoint a successor over Medina when he set out for campaigns. Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that the Prophet must have appointed a successor for after his death because he always appointed deputies in his life.
Correction: The refutation shows that the Prophet relied on the Companions' collective ijtihad to choose a leader, and that the lack of tawatur for any such text confirms it never existed.

Checkpoint
What is one reason given in the refutation that an explicit text for Ali does not exist?
A) The Prophet forgot to appoint a successor
B) It would have been transmitted by tawatur if it existed
C) Ali rejected the claim
D) The text was lost after the Prophet's death

Key Takeaway
The Sunni refutation demonstrates that the absence of a widely transmitted text, combined with the Prophet's trust in the Companions and the historical precedent of the Quraysh hadith, confirms that no explicit text existed for Ali's imamate.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The Companions abandoned their own claim to leadership based on a single prophetic report. Is there an area of your life where you are holding onto a position or desire, even though a clear command from Allah or His Prophet tells you otherwise? Will you submit like the Ansar, or will you continue to cling to your own preference?
?
The Prophet delegated many matters to the ijtihad of the community. Do you trust the collective wisdom of scholars and community leaders today, or do you insist on explicit texts for every issue? Where in your life does this lack of trust prevent you from following guidance?
?
The passage says that if a text for Ali existed, it would have been widely transmitted due to strong motives. But do you accept that the absence of such a text means there is no such command from Allah? Or do you make excuses for why a text you wish existed might have been hidden? Examine your heart: are you truly content with what Allah has revealed, or do you desire something else?

Deviant Belief: Faith as Primordial Covenant
Describes Murji'ah-like view on faith as primordial covenant.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the deviant view that faith is the pre-eternal acknowledgment from the covenant, and why it is rejected.

Core Idea
Faith is not today's confession but a primordial yes from pre-eternity.

This passage presents a radical view on faith from certain sects, claiming that true faith is the acknowledgment made by all souls before time began. They argue this primordial faith remains unchanged except by apostasy, leading to startling conclusions about hypocrites and prophets. Let's examine their argument and the prophetic condemnation.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its literal translation. Notice the key terms: الإيمان, الذر, الأزل, etc.
The passage uses parentheses for explanatory insertions; treat them as part of the source.
2
Identify the core claim about faith
Find where the passage defines faith. What is the 'statement of the atoms'? How does this differ from the usual definition?
The passage says faith is the acknowledgment from the primordial covenant.
3
Understand the implications and condemnation
Note the claims about equality of faith (hypocrite equals prophet) and the condition for the two words. Then read the condemnation. Why is this view considered astray?
The Prophet's hadith condemns all such sects to the Fire.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقالوا الإيمان قول الذر في الأزل (بلى أي الإيمان هو الإقرار الذي وجد من الذر حين قال تعالى: ألست بربكم) وهو باق في الكل على السوية (إلا المرتدين وإيمان المنافق مع كفره كإيمان الأنبياء لاستواء الجميع في ذلك الإيمان) والكلمتان ليستا بإيمان إلا بعد الردة فهذه هي الفرق الضالة الذين قال فيهم رسول الله كلهم في النار
And they said: Faith is the statement of the atoms in pre-eternity (i.e., faith is the acknowledgment that occurred from the atoms when the Exalted said: 'Am I not your Lord?'), and it remains in all equally (except the apostates, and the faith of the hypocrite along with his disbelief is like the faith of the prophets because all are equal in that faith), and the two words are not faith except after apostasy. These are the astray sects about whom the Messenger of God said: 'All of them are in the Fire.'
Source pages p. 431

Commentary
This passage presents a deviant theological view regarding the nature of faith (iman). The group (likely the Murji'ah or a similar sect) argues that true faith is not the verbal confession or inner conviction in this life, but rather the primordial acknowledgment made by all human souls (or atoms) during the pre-eternal covenant (mithaq) when God asked 'Am I not your Lord?' and they replied 'Yes.' According to them, this primordial faith remains in every person equally—including hypocrites—except for apostates who have explicitly renounced it. They further claim that the 'two words' (likely the shahada, 'There is no god but Allah, Muhammad is the Messenger of Allah') are not considered faith except after apostasy. The passage then condemns these groups as astray, quoting the prophetic hadith that all such sects are destined for the Fire.

Key Terms
الإيمان
الذر
الأزل
المرتدين
المنافق
الكلمتان
الفرق الضالة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That this deviant view represents orthodox Ash'ari theology.
Correction: This is a rejected, astray view contrary to Sunni orthodoxy.

Checkpoint
What do the deviant sects claim about faith according to this passage?
A) Faith is verbal confession.
B) Faith is the primordial acknowledgment from the covenant.
C) Faith is only in the heart.
D) Faith increases and decreases.

Key Takeaway
The deviant sects reduce faith to a single primordial event, negating the role of verbal confession and inner conviction in this life.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever treated your faith as a simple 'yes' from the past—perhaps a childhood declaration—without actively renewing it through daily confession and obedience? What one concrete action can you take today to move from a static to a living faith?
?
The deviant view claims a hypocrite's faith equals a prophet's. Does this reveal any hidden complacency in your own heart—do you assume your faith is secure because of a past event? Ask yourself: If I were judged only on my current state, would my faith be alive?
?
Consider the fate of the hypocrite in this deviant view: they are considered equal in faith to the prophets. How does this challenge you to examine your own sincerity? What step can you take this week to ensure your faith is not merely inherited but personally owned?

Criteria for Declaring Unbelief (Takfir)
Defines limited grounds for takfir within Ahl al-Qibla.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the limited grounds for takfir within Ahl al-Qibla according to Ash'ari theology.

Core Idea
Takfir is restricted to only five grave matters, not every error.

This passage from Sanusi's text carefully delimits when a person claiming to be Muslim can be declared an unbeliever. It is a restrictive list, not an expansive one. By studying these criteria, we see the Ash'ari commitment to the ummah's unity and the seriousness of takfir.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and identify the five exceptions
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Note each of the five specific causes that lead to takfir.
Pay attention to the structure: the list is introduced by 'إلا بما فيه'.
2
Understand the role of consensus and its types
The passage elaborates on the fifth exception (denial of consensus). Observe how it distinguishes between consensus on matters known necessarily from religion, speculative consensus (zanni), and definitive consensus (qat'i).
This shows the nuanced approach of Ash'ari scholars.
3
Reflect on the distinction between innovator and disbeliever
What does the passage say about errors other than the five? Why is it important to maintain this distinction?
This reinforces the principle that takfir is not to be taken lightly.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولا نكفر أحداً من أهل القبلة إلا بما فيه نفي للصانع القادر العليم أو شرك أو إنكار للنبوة أو إنكار ما علم مجيئه عليه السلام به ضرورة أو إنكار المجمع عليه كاستحلال المحرمات التي أجمع على حرمتها فإن كان ذلك المجمع عليه مما علم ضرورة من الدين فذلك ظاهر داخل فيما تقدم ذكره وإلا فإن كان إجماعاً ظنياً فلا كفر بمخالفته وإن كان قطعياً ففيه خلاف وأما ما عداه فالقائل به مبتدع غير كافر وللفقهاء في معاملتهم خلاف هو خارج عن فننا هذا
We do not declare anyone of the qibla to be an unbeliever except by what involves negation of the Powerful, Knowing Maker, or polytheism, or denial of prophethood, or denial of what is known necessarily to have been brought by him (peace be upon him), or denial of consensus, such as deeming permissible the prohibited things whose prohibition is agreed upon. If that which is agreed upon is known necessarily from the religion, then that is apparent and included in what has been mentioned previously. Otherwise, if it is a speculative consensus, then there is no unbelief in opposing it; and if it is definitive, then there is disagreement. As for other than that, the one who says it is an innovator but not an unbeliever, and jurists differ in their dealing with him, which is outside our discipline.
Source pages p. 431

Commentary
This passage lays out the careful Sunni doctrine on takfir (declaring a Muslim to be an unbeliever). It begins by restricting takfir to specific grave matters: denial of God's existence, shirk (polytheism), denial of prophethood, denial of something that is known necessarily (darura) to be from the religion (i.e., undeniable essentials), and denial of a matter of consensus (ijma'). An example is deeming permissible what is universally agreed to be forbidden. Then it distinguishes between types of consensus: if the consensus is on a matter known necessarily from the religion, denial constitutes unbelief; if the consensus is speculative (zanni), opposing it does not result in unbelief; if the consensus is definitive (qat'i), there is a difference of opinion among scholars on whether denial constitutes unbelief. For all other errors, the person is considered a misguided innovator (mubtadi') but not a disbeliever. Finally, the passage notes that the rules of dealing with such innovators are left to jurists and are outside the scope of this theological work.

Key Terms
تكفير
أهل القبلة
الشرك
النبوة
الإجماع
القطع
الظن
مبتدع

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That Ash'aris are quick to declare takfir
Correction: This passage shows their restraint and nuanced criteria; only five specific grave matters justify takfir.

Checkpoint
What is the ruling on opposing a speculative consensus (ijma' zanni)?
A) The person becomes a disbeliever.
B) The person is an innovator but not a disbeliever.
C) There is no unbelief in opposing it.
D) It depends on the judge's opinion.

Key Takeaway
Takfir is not a tool for every theological disagreement; it is strictly limited to denial of God, shirk, denial of prophethood, denial of essential religious truths, and denial of definitive consensus.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you encounter a Muslim who holds a different opinion on a secondary matter, do you find yourself looking down on them or questioning their faith? How does this passage expose the arrogance in your heart? Will you commit to treating them as a brother or sister unless they clearly deny one of the five matters?
?
Are there any beliefs or practices you hold that are not among the five criteria but you still consider essential to the point of judging others? Where did you get that standard, and are you willing to submit to the limits set by this lesson?
?
If you have ever said or thought 'that person is not a real Muslim' because of a minor disagreement, will you repent from that sin and seek forgiveness from Allah? How will you change your speech and inner judgment starting today?
